THE BED PAGE.
Australia for Australians.
ICT OT for the British : certainly not.
\ Nob for the Germans, nor for the
French, nor for any other European
nation ; and emphatically not for
ia. But for honest men of good European
>ck who will make Australia their home,
d put Australia Always First. The cowl
es nob make the monk, and it is not
itish blood that makes the Australian,
le Australian is the man who centres his
jals in Australia, and consciously or un-
nsciously devotes his life to making this
untry 4 ' prosper. The foreigner in Aus-
ilia is the man who, be he of British blood
not, works for an end outside Australia ;
d struggles to keep this country in vassal-
e to alien opinions, in order that the fruits
her growth may be utilised for the
vantage of Europeans who have no
sential sympathy with our national life. It
the British foreigner in Australia who has
cklessly squandered men and money in
sisting the British attempt to bring a new
rritory under tribute, and to establish a
sw Ireland, a new India, in South Africa,
istance alleviates the foreign disease :
Lme will cure it; but in the interests of his
'untry —which are his own—every Austra-
in should actively endeavour to hasten the
itural progress to national health.
* ♦ m 9
Australia is still under British domini-
on. At the census of 1891 there were in
lis country nearly the same number of men
: adult age, born in Britain, as there were
ten born in Australia. A man naturally
eaves to the land of his birth; and the
ritish in Australia necessarily endeavour to
ale Australia for the benefit of Britain,
'rom one point of view patriotism is intelli-
ent self-interest ; from another point
f view it is a sentimental prejudice ;
nd the British foreigner struggles be-
ween the sentiment which attaches him
o Britain, and the reason which tells
im that, so far as he lives and labours in
Lustralia, his interests are bound up in Aus-
ralia. Up to the present, the Briton in
Australia has been able to worship hereditary
entiment without seriously injuring himself,
typically speaking, he has not gone as a con-
ingenter to Africa. He has not given away
noney which he really needed. The Aus-
ralian contingents have been a national
vaste ; but they have involved no personal
sacrifice by the British foreigner. He has
seen patriotic at Australia’s expense, and,
is a Briton, he is not concerned with Aus-
tralia’s expense. In worshipping at the same
time his Jingo God and his peculiar Mammon
he is conscious that he has fulfilled his best
British attributes.
* • * *
With the British foreigner are linked his
descendants who, though burn in Australia,
have been taught to call Britain “ home,”
and to look upon Britain as the master,
Australia as the servant. They havd learnt
to cower before Britain as the convict
cowered before the whip. When the
last generation of Australian natives was
growing to manhood Britain was still
all in all, and their minds will
be permeated by the British leaven
until they die? 1 Though born here,
these men are more foreign in Australia
than the humblest German farmer who puts
away the thought of fatherland and settles
down to call his children’s fatherland his
home. And it is these British Australians
who still, in company with Britons, adminis-
ter our government —to British ends. Austra-
lian-born representatives number about one-
half of the Commonwealth Parliament ; and
the Australian-born representatives who have
learnt to put Australia First are few indeed.
Every year is altering that. Every year sees
the sentiment of Australian nationality take
firmer root and wider branching. Every
year Australian youths are growing up to
ask themselves the pregnant question, What
does Australia gain by remaining an appan-
age of the British Empire ? and how much
does she stand to lose ? And every year
more men aud more are realising that the
source of Australian greatness, the founda-
tion of Australian peace and security, the
guarantee of the prosperity of Australian
people and of the happiness of Australian
homes, are to be found in the concentration
of industry and effort and aspiration within
the borders of our own territory.
• * • •
What essential partorlot have we in foreign
ambitions or foreign quarrels, British or
other 1 Why should we marry our national
ideals to the corpses of European intrigue 1
Let the dead Past bury its debt : our debt is
to the Future. We have no reason to spend
ourselves to prop up the decaying greatness
of Britain. We have no reason to adopt the
jealousies and bigotries of Britain. We
have no reason to spurn Germany, or to
despise France, or to fear Russia—save and
except as a British dependency. Australia
should be cosmopolitan, welcoming all men
whose blood will mix with ours to produce
a vigorous offspring. Does this seem
lue ? It is not so vague as it seems.
:ions live by ideas, not by material bread
le. The reasons which ally us to Britain
reasons of trade and defence : our in-
itries are still in chief measure developed
British capital ; our coasts are still
rolled by British ships—though their
tection, if the British Isles be menaced,
lot worth the snap of a finger. But these
reasons for alliance, not for dependence,
en now, it is a question whether the drain
interest-tribute is not a loss outweighing
gain of commercial connection with
tain ; it is a question whether the risk of
ng made partner in Britain’s quarrels does
i outweigh the safety attributed to the
is of the British flag. To both these
Bstions the years will bring us answer,
the meantime, let Australians cease to re-
d Australia as a sponge to be* squeezed
the benefit of Britain.
* * • •
Make no mistake : in all this there is no
3tilicy to Britain. Britain is our natural
y, our best ally, and we will cleave
her as long as we may. Most of us are
rung from British stock ; and there is* no
tter stock in the world we could have
rung from. Denunciation of the vices of
e British character, the British nation,
es not exclude appreciation of the
lendid British virtues. In the survey
nations British greed and hypocrisy are
'ssibly more than balanced by British
lour, British independence, the stubborn
ritish defence of liberty and justice within
e British islands. It may be that in the
ogress of years the British virtue is
wakening, the British vicek are prepon-
srating; but we can never forget the Past.
) that little island in the North Sea
e world owes many an inspiring thought,
any a heroic example. We in Aus-
alia speak the English language, we
herit the English literature ; and we gladly
knowledge the debt as the best foundation
: a language and literature of our own. It
ill be many a century before Australia can
low a list of names to compare with the
lustrious roll of British worthies. Remem*
ering all this : who in Australia could be
ostile to Britain ? And yet
* * * *
Nations, like individuals, are guided by
;lf-iaterest; they must be guided by self-
iterest if they are to live. In all Britain’s
ansactions with Australia Britain’s self-
lterest has been the paramount factor. The
istory of Australia in relation to Britain
antains only three main epochs. In the
rst, this country was conceived solely as
mste land where British criminals could be
>ut out of sight and forgotten. As long as
he possibly could, Britain retained the
otion that Australia was merely a convict
ettlement, a receptacle for felons. Even when
ransportation had been formally abolished
he convicts were still sent hither. It was
nly when the Randolph was stopped at
*ort Phillip Heads, and the Hashemy’s
ntrance to Port Jackson excited a deter-
uined protest, that the British Govt, reluc-
antly gave way to the eastern States—and
ontinued to send convicts to the West and
o Tasmania. The total number of British
onvicts landed on these shores was not far
hort of 150,000 ; and to this day we suffer
rom the polluted stream. “As I walk along
he Sydney streets,” said a N.S.W. Supreme
Dourt Judge to the writer, “ I can trace con-
vict heredity in many a face.” For, mark
,his : crime does not die out for many
rears. . Its features may be weakened and
ittenuated ; but they persist to the utter-
nost generation. What is the compara-
;ive sluggishness of the denizens of New
South Wales, of Tasmania, and of West-
tralia—a sluggishness which has become a
byword—but a testimony to the superiority
of the honest blood which founded Victoria,
South Australia, and Maoriland ? What are
the unmentionable crimes hidden in the
Australian bush—the family incest, the
passionless murders, such as that by the
Dederers at Boggabri—but a witness to the
fact that the legacy of the “old hands”
has never died out, and will never
die out entirely 1 And it is this—this pol-
lution of the national life at its source—
which is Australia’s cardinal debt to Britain.
* * * •
When Australia ceased, to be a dumping-
ground for Britain’s criminal rubbish, it
passed into a new epoch as an arena for
the career of British younger sons, a
field for the investment of British capital.
The British Govt, neglected us, but private
self-interest took the place of public self-
interest, and Australia gained while Britain
gained. Now commences the third epoch,
in which Australia is seen as a re-
cruiting-ground for the British army, a prop
for the British Empire. Once more the
British Govt., after a long period of care-
lessness, has become alive to the fact that
Australia may be directly exploited for
British ends. It is full time to resist. Our
aims are not Britain’s aims. This
country cannot continually bleed to save the
British skin. From first to last, the guid-
>ing principle of Britain’s policy in relation
to Australia has been British self-interest ;
and it is time that Australian self-interest
was recognised as the motive for Austra-
lians. We desire to remain Britain’s friendly
ally, but if this country is to prosper we
must put Australia First.
* * * *
There will be a struggle. The British-
born and the Australian-oorn British have
in their hands the machinery of govern-
ment. They have at back a
diplomacy fertile in the bribes which
attract the weak-minded. They have beneath
them manj 7 people willing to be deceived by
specious shibboleths, ready to be dazzled by
flags and fetishes, many who would sell their
birthright for a mess of bitter pottage. Let
Young Australia come to the front with the
motto of Australia for Australians. This
question of independent national develop-
ment overshadows all others. Freetrade
versus Protection is a dying cry. Parties
must fall into the world-old division of
Liberal against Tory, Progress against
Stagnation. Let Young Australia remember,
now and always, that the best basis of
Australian progress is an Australian patriot-
ism which asks of every politician, of every
administrator, of every movement, of every
law : How will Australia be profited 1 How
will Australia be served ?—and which puts
Australia Always First.

The local opinion of Tommy Atkins (based chiefly
on numerous contingenters’ statements) is that his
language is filthy ; but that on the whole he is a
very decent fellow, and conducts himself as such.
Yet, how can you blame the Continent for believing
“monstrous calumnies” when Kipling is ac-
counted the exponent of Tommy’s morals? If
the tale seems steep—well, see what is said by the
Great English Poet, the darling of the Jingoes and
the Times. In the Barrack-Room Ballads Tommy
is disorderly and goes to cells for being “ Mad
drunk and resisting the Guard.” Tommy is a
thief-
Ow the loot!
Bloomin’ loot I
That’s the thing to make the boys get up an’ shoot .. .
An’ if you treat a nigger to a dose o’ cleanin’-rod
’E’s like to show you everything e’ owns . . .
You can mostly square a Sergint an’ a Quarter-master
too,
If you only take the proper way to go ; . . .
So ’ere’s good-luck to those that wears the Widow’s
clo’es,
An’ the Devil send ’em all they want o’ loot! . . .
Tommy’s inclinations are decidedly immoral—
Ship me somewhere east of Suez, where the best is like
the worst,
Where there are n’t no Ten Commandments . . .
Why ? Because, says Thomas—
We’re most of us liars, we ’re ’arf of us thieves, an’ the
rest are as rank as can be . . .
As to women—well, Tommy is no saint. Says he
by the mouth of his Prophet Kipling—
I've taken my fun where I’ve found it,
I’ve rogued an’ I’ve ranged in my time ;
I’ve ’ad my pickin’ o’ sweet-’earts,
An’ four o’ the lot was prime.
One was an ’airf-caste widow,
One was a woman at Prome,
One was the wife of a jemadar-sats.
An’ one is a girl at ’ome.
Then the Continent is told that Tommy has been
taking liberties with the Boer women, and the
Continent believes it. And, seeing that Budyard
is Kipling, and his “monstrous calumnies,”
printed in large editions, have raised him to the
pinnacle of Jingo fame —what else is the Continent
to do ?
Some of the Eng. reviews have been scolding
Henley for saying uncomplimentary things about
Stevenson, his protege and sometime friend. One
of them goes so far as to call H. (with pleasant alli-
teration) “ a literary leper.” The idea seems to be
that when a public man dies, his friends should
say nothing of him which is not eulogy. No
matter what false god he may be, exalted on no
matter what foolish pedestal, we should keep
silence and permit deceit to prosper. Well,
old Isaac Pitman taught us better in the
phonography primer. “ I may cuss Plato, I may
cuss Socrates,” said Major Veritas. And that is
right. You may cuss anybody you please in the
interests of truth and justice. De mortuis is a
stupid maxim. It is probably a remnant of the
tradition of ancestor-worship which we inherit
from stupid aboriginal forbears, trembling among
the superstitions of the race-dawn. Be a man dead
or living, there is no reason why public truth
(Should not be spoken of him, if public justice
demand speech. It can’t hurt the man who is
dead ; and it can’t hurt his friends who are living
unless they prefer rosy lies to russet verities. And
that preference puts them out of court as the
Lie’s shields and accomplices. Let us hug Truth,
even if her sword cuts our breasts. And De
mortuis nil nisi Verum.
“ A.M.” (a lady) writes—
I knew something of R. L. Stevenson in his Edinburgh
youth; and the three-lettered word, by which Henley
hinted that some described him, scarcely presents an
enigma. To his wholesome-minded, frank contemporaries
Stevenson was an “ ass ” ; his elders, with the leniency
that experience of life brings, shrugged their shoulders
and admitted that he was a “ puir creature.” A young
lady—of limited mental capacity—did describe him as a
“ very nice boy,” and is even known to have sat out
dances with him ; but then there were so few young men
in the ball-going clan of Edinburgh, and even she could
never succeed in reaching any high pitch of enthusiasm
for R.L.S. Ido not know that any young woman in
those days had ever the slightest desire to marry the
extremely plain Stevenson, for he was entirely devoid of
that fascination of dominant vitality which led ugly
Wilkes to declare that he was “ only half-an-hour behind
the handsomest man.” Besides, Stevenson did n.ot care
for the company of young women, and that was a sign
of his character. He was too egotistic, too interested
in himself, ever to “ fall in love ” ; but he liked elderly
women,#who petted and made much of him.
L. J. Brient, for ten years editor of Sydney
Daily Telegraph , faded quietly away to England
the other day. His sudden fall from power was the
biggest journalistic shock for many an Australian
day; and it gives fresh point to the journalistic
maxim, “ Never trust a board of directors.”
Brient trusted; he thought himself settled for
life; he held that he had acquired the art of
managing directors. About a year ago, when
the question of written engagements arose in
conversation, “I have no written engagement,”
said Brient; “ I never asked for one.” And
when someone pointed out the fate of his prede-
cessor Ward: “Yes,” said Brient; “but Ward
did not understand;” And now it seems that
Brient did not understand either. (And we see
that Heracles’exploit in killing the nine-headed
hydra was far more glorious than Perseus’; for
Medusa had but one head, however terrible.)
“ But if the worst comes to the worst,” said 8., “I
can always earn my salt as a reporter.” And again,
‘ ‘ I had three years in London, and when we came to
measure up—and this is not vanity—l found very
few men better than me.” But the years have
gone since then ; and at 45 (Brient’s age) one does
not find London the same as at 30. However:
good luck to him ! He made the Telegraph a
dashing, unscrupulous, clever journal; and re-
member this in his favour, that he was compelled
to feed the hydra which finally devoured him.
The vocal O’Sullivan, by the grace of Gab a,
“leading politician” in N. S. Wales, has had a
list of “the most beautiful aboriginal names”
prepared to confer upon new dredges and vessels
of that ilk. There are Wurtamurtah, Dooribang,
Timbrebunga, and a crowd of cacophonous
examples—salted with the remark that “the
aboriginals suffered at times from a disease very
like smallpox, called by them Galgala.” Appa-
rently the aboriginal disease is to be perpetuated
with the others—and we wonder We wonder
whether in a thousand years a new race will come
and build upon the ruins of our aboriginal civilisa-
tion, and discover that we suffered from a disease
called O’Sullivan, and declare it a “ most beautiful
name,” and call a flying-punt by the name. Even
that may be ; for sounding brass is more lasting
than many a monument.

Furniss’s recent book of reminiscences quotes a
candid opinion—Alfred Harmsworth’s, apparently I
— re the lost enterprise of Lika Joko.
One of the youngest and most successful newspaper
proprietors called me a fool. I wrote and asked hipi why.
We had an interview. He said frankly, “ You are a fool,
in my opinion, for producing too good an article for the
money. Your staff is too good ; your printing is too
good ; your paper is too good. lam a success because 11
know where to buy paper cheap, and sell it for a profit. I■
have thirty publications, but their names, their contents,
writing, or art, I never think about, nor does the public
either. We ink something on the paper; and sell it at so
much a pound profit.”
“To ink something on the paper and sell it at so
much a pound profit ” —Journalism Up-to-Date.
(Historians please note.)
d*
In Edward Hyson’s The Gold-Seekers: a Story
of Waddy (Lond., Longmans), he cultivates once
more the garden of youthful reminiscence. The
story is simple and smoothly written, and will
please boyish readers as well as others. On the
whole, it hardly rises to the level of Dyson’s
excellent capacity ; but as a first novel it is not
without promise.

Phil May's Annual might be stereotyped, so
little variety does he give us from year to year.
His work in the new number is not of his best;
and the literary contents are feeble. Have we
heard before the story of the “ fearfully groggy
old woman” who called on M. with the quff?tion :
“ Do you want any morals ? ”
J*
The Saturday Review —“ magni nominis umbra,”
as Mr. Lucan says—apparently endeavours to
revive its old tradition of “slashing articles.”
John Davidson is the last victim. We read that
the “ Ballad of a Nun ” was
perhaps the worst poem that ever won notoriety. It
was unreal and- unpleasant, it was medical rather than
poetic; a vulgarly nasty contribution to a singularly
jaundiced periodical of the decadents.
John Farrell, we remember, swore by the Nun
for at least three months. Then ? But the
merit of The Saturday's slashing hey-day Is, in
this: that the rhetoric was exaggerated, round a
heap of sound sense.
J*
Yet on another page the S.R. is pleased to print
reflections on Venice by Arthur Symons, one of
the chief of “the decadents.” He closes not
unimpressively, remembering his Pater—
Why is it then that the melancholy of Venice is the
most exquisite and alluring and the least distressing
thing in the world? It is because that melancholy is
no nearer to one’s heart than the melancholy in the face
of a portrait. It is the tender and gracious sadness of
that beautiful woman who leans her face on her hand in a
famous picture in the Accademia. The feast is over, the
wine still flushes the glass on the table, the little negro
strikes his lute, she listens to the song, her husband sits
beside her, proudly : something not in this world, a vague
thought, a memory, a forgetfulness, has possessed her
for the moment, setting those pensive lines about her
lips, which have just smiled, and which will smile again
when she has lifted her eyelids.
A.G.S.

WOMEN AND
WARNER’S
SAFE CURE.
The health of women is of supremo im-
portance. The daughter of to-day becomes
the wife of to-morrow, and it is abso-
lutely essential to the welfare of the race
that good health should be hers; good
health which brings happiness and en-
sures vigour in posterity. A woman’s
nervous system is very highly strung.
Her mechanism is far more delicate and
complex than that of a man, and there
is little to wonder at when it breaks down
under the continuous high pressure of
modern life.
Most women require some aid to enable
them to support the daily stress and
strain, and this aid is found in WARNER’S
SAFE CURE — a medicine wonderfully
adapted to their delicate organisation-
which has for twenty years secured the
grateful endorsement of women in all
walks of life in all parts of the civilised
world.
Read the following Letters:
“ Charlton,” George Street,
Norwood, S.A.
October 1, 1900.
Dear Sirs,— . ,
About seven years ago I contracted a severe
internal complaint, and was in a very critical
state. The doctor attending me told me that if I
lived'l should be a sufferer all my lifetime. His
treatment was quite useless in giving me relief.
After two or three years I got tired of paying
doctors’ fees for no benefit, and hearingof Warner’s
Safe Cure I gave it a trial. I took, in all, five
bottles, and am thankful to state I am perfectly
cured. The medical man was genuinely surprised
at such a wonderful result. lam now as well as
it is possible to be, and give Warner’s Safe Cure
the whole credit for accomplishing what doctors
deemed impossible. Hoping that ladies, suffer-
ing as I did, will profit, I am writing to you now.
My mother considered that her life was saved by
the use of Warner’s Safe Cure in a somewhat
similar complaint. } ANNIE HARRIS.

June 26th, 1901.
The morning of life is the time of
abundance, profusion, strength, vigor,
growth. When the sun begins to sink,
when the midday of life is past, then
the hair begins to fade and the silvery
gray tells of approaching age.
Sunrise or sunset ? Which shall
your mirror say ? If the former, then
it is rich and dark hair, long and heavy
hair; if the latter, it is short and fall-
ing hair, thin and gray hair.
The choice is yours,—for
Ayer’s Hair Vigor
always restores color to gray hair,
stops falling of the hair, and makes
the hair grow long and heavy.
This is something you have been
looking for, isn’t it ? And it is some-
thing you can have confidence in, for
it is no experiment; people have been
using it for half a century. We do not
claim it will do everything, but we do
claim it is the best hair preparation
ever made.
Prepared by Dr. J. C. Ayer Co., Lowell, Mass., U. S. A.

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Rolling In.”
See the gray Dawn breaking slowly
With a sullen sky above her,
And a white mist hanging lowly
Over sodden leaves a-drip ;
And the creek’s bed running over
Where the yellow flood is rolling
With the ten-knot current bowling
Underneath the Timber Tip !
And a-glint with rain-flecks shining
Where the laggard light is breaking—
See, five hundred logs reclining
All in stately rank a-row ;
And behind —their stations taking —
Twenty sodden souls a-shiver,
With their pulses all a-quiver
And their score of hearts aglow !
Pass the word—twelve feet, and rising !
Stand by—knock the chocks from under
Heave ! —and twenty shoulders prising
At the signal, “ Let her go ! ”
Break the level lines asunder
And five hundred logs a-flashing
Hurtling through the air—and crashing
Cleave the seething flood below !
Oh ! there’s music in the ringing
Of the yellow pines a-clashing,
And a merry lilt a-singing
In the lapping of the tide,
When the rain in torrents dashing
Scuds before the gale a-blowing
With a roaring flood a-flowing
And a golden freight a-ride !
N.s.w.
A. CAR-B.
To the Girls of the Bush.
The sun on the sliprails has faded,
But still in sweet memory bide
The ready brown hands that have aided,
And lips that have lured us aside.
Oh ! girls from the North of the Murray !
Oh ! girls from the Bogan and Bland !
Oh ! sun-caressed maids of Macquarie 1
The laughter and light of the land !
Light-footed you led our bush dances,
Light-hearted you rode with our best,
And wove with a smile the sweet fancies
That robbed us of reason and rest !
Your “coo-ee” dies down on the ranges,
Your musical step on the floor.
And Life, with its chances and changes,
Has parted our pathways once more !
Unspoiled of rich raiment arid jewel
For dames of the city designed,
You learned, all the same, to be cruel
In beauty that meant to be kind !
Bash maidens ! Demure little misses,
And heart-stirring hoydens and bold !
Bush maidens ! I blow you warm kisses
From lips that are lonely and cold !
u.k.
WILL OGILVIE.
On tie Night-Shift.
All the world is hushed in slumber
Save the stampers’ monotone,
And the stars that none can number
Smile serenely on their throne.
Now the moon, three-quarters missing,
Peeps above the “ Harveys ” far,
While the Southern Cross is kissing
Farewells to the evening star.
Curlews scream from oak and yarran,
Fireflies flash bright amethyst;
Mountains bleak and ridges barren
Hide their heads in fleecy mist.
Hear celestial music calling,
Siren-voiced, o’er oceans deep !
And the thudding meteors falling
Guard the vault where heroes sleep.
When night-fancies sweet come o’er us,
Bringing ease from constant pain,
Would we wake to cares that bore us—
Wake and never dream again ?
Build your towers in old Hispania,
Let the caustic cynics sneer ;
What though churlish churchmen ban you,
Have your joys and pleasures here !
What of future can they tell us—
Can they penetrate the skies ?
All the ills that e’er befell us
Are as nothing to their lies.
When a stone’s thrown in the river
Ripples spread and disappear,
Bubbles shine, and then for ever
Vanish into atmosphere.
Life is short; but long is Sorrow,
Cunningly she welds her chain ;
Drain the goblet! for to-morrow
There may be no draught to drain.
Gay the fledgling magpie flutters,
Minahs peep from pine and gum,
And the raucous jackass stutters
Warning that the Day has come !
KELPIE.

SATURDAY, JANUARY 25, 1902.
The German Fury.
This House takes its first opportunity, in view of the
despatch of the Federal contingent to South Africa, to
express its indignation at the baseless charges made
abroad against the honor of the people, and the humanity
and valor of the soldiers of the Empire.
This House affirms the readiness of Australia to give
all requisite aid to the Mother Country in order to bring
the present war to an end. —Resolution •passed by Federal
House of Representatives.
Last week the Federal Parliament rose in
a moment of excitement and passed the
above resolutions by a large majority. In
the course of the debate the reason of this
temporary burst of indignation was fully
explained. The German press has recently
been saying things. It called the British
army in South Africa a “gang of thieves,”
or long German words to that effect, and
cast other reflections upon it, including
charges of rape and wholesale barbarity,
and in the German Reichstag Joseph
Chamberlain —he of the eyeglass and the
orchid —was alluded to as a villain of the
worst order. Also, some German paper or
papers of no great importance expressed the
opinion that “the British colonies” were
tired of the war, and weary of contingenting
generally. Germany, certainly, with the
record of its doings in Poland still on view,
and with its hideous methods of maintaining
military discipline even in time of peace, is
a country which has no need to be excited
in the cause of humanity, and the Prussian
junker, at all events, has a tendency to be
an uncivil German at all times, whatever
other Germans may be like. Chancellor
Von Bulow threw a limited quantity of oil
on the troubled waters, and in the Reichstag
he urged his agitated countrymen to
moderate their language; but his apology
was apparently not sufficiently abject, and
his perfunctory way of dealing with the
matter roused Britain, if the cablegrams are
to be credited, to temporary hysteria. Aus-
tralia took the hysteria at second-hand, and
as a result this country is sending away a
new contingent of 1000 men, and its Federal
Parliament has “ resolved ” as above.
* * * *
So far Australia appears to be needlessly
harassed over this matter. There is wild
talk, for example, of a general boycott of
German goods by way of hitting the empire
of William the Astonishing a deadly blow in
the eye, and bringing the Prussian junker to
reason. If such a thing does happen, and
the boycott is serious enough to make
William’s empire really angry, the probable
result will be a boycott or customs prohi-
bition of our goods in Germany by way of
retaliation. And as Germany buys much
more from the seven States of Australasia
than they buy from Germany, the balance in
our favor being some £700,000 a year, the
loss would be all on our side. There is
nothing which this country supplies to Ger-
many which our restless rival, Argentina, can’t
furnish just as well. Australasia has to ex-
port vastly more than it imports in order
that the surplus may cover the interest on
its foreign debts, and it can’t afford to
kick one of its few good customers
very hard, merely because that customer’s
opinion of the British army is low, and
of Joseph Chamberlain still lower. And
Great Britain can’t do any serious damage to
Germany by a trade boycott either. In 1899
—the latest year for which the writer has
full details available—Britain bought a little
over £30,000,000 worth of goods from Ger-
many (German goods and other goods which
reached it via Germany, all included). And
Britain sold to Germany (including British
and Irish produce, and foreign produce
bought by British merchants and re-sold)
nearly £38,000,000 worth. India also buys
about £1,000,000 worth of goods annually
from Germany and sells to Germany
about £5,000,000 worth. A boycott of
German goods by Britain, or by Australasia,
and a retaliatory measure by Germany,
would leave the British Empire a heavy
loser. Germany is one of the props of
British commerce and industry, and the prop
can’t be kicked away with impunity. And
while the hostile German helps Britain to
the extent of £8,000,000 a year, and India
to the extent of £4,000,000 a year, and Aus-
tralasia to the extent of £700,000 a year, the
friendly American takes £90,000,000 a year
away from Britain by selling much to her
and buying practically nothing. The
American speaks kindly about Joe Cham-
berlain —but it is an awful price to pay for
a few kindly remarks about Joe Chamber-
lain.
• • * *
And there are excuses to be made, even
for the German. The language he used
about Britain may have been bad enough,
but, after all, it is hardly any worse than the
language that Reid and M‘Millan and Joe
Cook and other N.S.W. politicians, also a
multitude of N.S.W. papers, have been
using for years and years past about Victoria
—also, to a less extent, about the other Aus-
tralian States. Victoria is part of the
Empire just as much as England is, and the
mean grudging abuse which the dominant
importing gang of Sydney has heaped upon
it so consistently is just the same kind of
offence that the German is charged with.
Britain is hated by many foreign States
by reason of its wealth. Victoria, ever
since that mere fly-speck on the map
of Australia made such astonishing progress
that its industries are far ahead of
those of the adjacent State—a State which is
twice its age and four times its size, and far
better supplied with natural resources—
has been, in the mouths of Sydney poli-
ticians, a place whose very name was
hardly to be mentioned without a sneer or a
curse. A Federal Ministry which is, in the
main, a N.S.W. Ministry, should not be so
grievously shocked at strong language
directed against one portion of the Empire,
considering what their own State has done
in the same direction. And if the language
of one or two German papers has been
violent, to say the least, the Federal
Ministry, being largely a Sydney Ministry,
should recollect the kind of remarks a
German may occasionally find in the gentle
columns of Truth. Chancellor von Bulow
has not yet made Truth an international
question, but even that may come.
* • * *
No person in his senses imagines that the
British army—the alleged “ gang of thieves ”
—is worse than the average of armies
indeed it is better than most. It is not
at all like the awful semi-British army with
which John Company looted India in days of
old. But, at the same time, the British Jingo
bard of the moment, Kipling, who is so
much quoted just now that even the Germans
must be well acquainted with him, describes
the British army as a gang of thieves. He
puts into his soldiers’ mouths, over and over
again, the prideful statement that they are
going after loot and women, and Kipling’s
soldiers don’t profess to be too particular
.about how they get either the loot or the
women. The Australian contingents have
formed part of the British army through
most of the present war, and almost every
Australian paper has published letters from
contingenters in which they talked of
the loot they stole from farm - houses
occupied mostly by defenceless women.
Some of them went further, and declared
with pride that they and their com-
rades habitually mutilated their dead
enemies, and were gathering teeth and
other fragments as souvenirs. The hor-
rible tale told by one contingenter, who
said that he and his fellow-soldiers of
the British forces had cut off the
fingers of Boers killed in battle, and stuck
biscuits in their mouths by way of ghoulish
mockery, was published as solemn fact in an
Australian paper; and an Australian civic
functionary publicly exhibited alleged bits of
dead Boer in a frame, and made a speech
about them with unconcealed pride. Who
can expect a German to have a high opinion
of an army in which, according to the
accounts of its own soldiers, not only is
thieving a matter of everyday occurrence,
but dead enemies are dishonored and
mangled ? And when the German read that
this was done in a force commanded by
British officers, what view was he likely to
take of the British forces in general ? Also
what view was he likely to take of Cham-
berlain, who is always presented before the
world as the man who is running the war —
this kind of war.
* * » *
In all human probability, many of the
loathsome statements made by Australian
contingenters about themselves and their
comrades in the British service were mere
Australian “blow” of a particularly nasty
order, and had no foundation in fact. But
the German can’t be expected to comprehend
the intricacies of the Australian joke. There
are many Germans in Australia, and it is
impossible to suppose that they didn’t send
back to their old friends in the Fatherland
some of the dreadful published letters of the
hilarious Australian contingenter. These
friends probably thought that, when the sol-
diers not only admitted to wholesale thievery,
but also to barbarous mutilation of the dead,
and spoke of it almost with pride, as if it
was a thing rather to be esteemed than
otherwise in a force commanded by British
officers, and when no Australian Parliament
or politician and not one Australian daily
paper repudiated the gruesome thing as a
gross slander on the army and on
the character of the Empire—well, they
probably thought that even worse -lay behind,
for the bitterest cynic seldom tells the whole
unpleasant truth of himself. Nothing the
Germans have said against the British army
in South Africa exceeds in grossness the
things that were said about it, or a part of
it, in Australian contingenters’ letters to
their relatives ; and the relatives handed the
letters to the papers as things they were
proud of ; and the papers published them in
the same spirit. And as Premier Barton
and his colleagues uttered no word of repro-
bation when these statements were made
about a British force by their own people, it
is childish to grow so furious when the Ger-
mans say things that are really no worse.
The Bulletin can’t believe that half these
stories were true, but that makes no dif-
ference in the situation. When Australia
made no attempt to refute such hideous
calumnies on a British army the stories
passed for true. Australia is the last place
on earth which has a right to complain of
any other community speaking in slanderous
terms of a British army. Almost the worst
slanders certainly the earliest—started
here, and were allowed to grow here.
* * * *
And . for the rest, the world probably
still thinks that Australia, at all events, is
dead tired of the war. If it knows the cir-
cumstances, it will probably have a suspicion
that it was more or less tired all along. Aus-
tralia raised its little force only by paying
the men better wages than the bulk of them
can now earn in ordinary pursuits —much
better, in many cases, when the rations and
the loot (if the contingenters’ letters home
spoke the truth about the loot) are con-
sidered. The British Government could
have hired good serviceable mercenaries, who
wouldn’t write disastrous letters home, for
less money, and when the man who is
devoted to the empire wants (or, at least,
receives) as much money to induce him to
fight for the empire as would almost suffice
to hire, for the same fighting purpose, two
men who don’t care a straw for the
empire, it is a curious proof of loyal
enthusiasm, to say the least. And amid
all the alleged devotion to the empire and
readiness to make sacrifices on its behalf,
not one Australian Premier—not even the
one who was moA desperately pressed for
money—dared, for the sake of his precious
billet, propose to levy even a small tax to
pay for the campaign. In almost every
State the cost was added, directly or in-
directly, to the floating debt, and left for
posterity to pay. If Australia and Tasmania
had made, in proportion to their population,
the same effort that Britain did, they would
have sent nearly 25,000 men to the war ;
they would have procured these men at about
2s. or 2s. 6d. a day; and they would have
added 6d. in the £ to the income-tax to pay
the account. If anything like that had been
done then there would be little doubt that
Australia was not tired of the war ; as it is,
the question is still quite an open one. And
certainly the aspersion of weariness in ill-
doing is not cleared away by the decision of
the Federal Government that another 1000
men may go if they get better pay than they
could earn by not going, and if Britain finds
the money. There may be real enthusiasm
behind, but the tired and perfunctory per-
mitting of somebody else to go at somebody
else’s expense can hardly be called an out-
ward manifestation of it.
Landlordism in Australia.
That the blight of landlordism is rapidly spread*
ing in Australia, and reproducing here the same
conditions which have depopulated the rural dis*
tricts of Ireland, Scotland, and England, is plain
to even the unobservant travellerin thecountry. To
those who dwell for any length of time in the
agricultural centres, the conviction is forced home
that, without strenuous and prompt remedies,
whole areas must soon pass completely out of cul-
tivation. The causes which drove the crofters
out of Scotland, the peasant farmers out of Eng*
land and Ireland, are actively at work in all the
States of Australia. Rack-renting of the familiar
Irish type is to be met with in almost every
district, and, with it, its inevitable consequences—
the impoverishment of the soil, the driving of the
farming population to the towns, and the loss to
the community of that most valuable element, the
yeoman farmer.
* * * *
In the early days—the days of the convicts, and
of Government anxiety to promote “ free settle-
ment” —large areas of agricultural land in the
vicinity of the big water-courses and of probable
cities were granted for little or nothing to soldiers,
sailors, convict-floggers, officials of all sorts, to
private immigrants from Europe, to public
servants who had taken part in the work of ex-
ploring and surveying the new continent, to
syndicates eager to grasp for a low price, or for no
price at all, areas of fertile land equal in extent to
principalities. Thus, “ the eyes were picked out ”
of Australia.
* * ■ * *
In some cases these granted areas passed out of
the hands of the original holders and were
gradually split up. More often they passed from
father to son, or remained in the hands of the
syndicate, and to-day are locked up against set-
tlement or worked by tenant farmers, who are
bond slaves to their landlords, who toil merely for
leave to live, and who exhaust both the land and
their own lives to enrich the owner of the soil —in
many cases an absentee. That is the history in
brief, and in general terms, of the agricultural
districts around Bathurst, and Goulburn, and
Tamworth, on the South Coast, and in the Hunter
River district in N.S.W., and it is the history,
too, of the rural settlements in many parts of
Victoria, Tasmania, and Queensland.
* * ♦ *
The incidents vary; the main facts are unvarying.
In one case the accident that a man happened to
be Deputy-Governor of a colony for a few days
gave him a claim to a huge land grant (some
200,000 acres), which his descendants hold to this
day. In other cases a syndicate, for a few pounds,
acquired a territory rather than an area, which
it now holds in insolent defiance of the public
weal, locked up against all settlement, resolute to
exact a bribe of the noblest magnitude before
allowing the plough to turn a single sod. In yet
other instances the construction of some little
public work earned for a man a great expanse of
land whiten his heirs now hold and let out on rack-
renting principles. These are the variants of the
origin of the evil of landlordism ; the results are
always identical. Either the land is held abso-
lutely against settlement, or it is let to tenants on
short leases, and on such terms of rental as devote
almost the whole profit of farming to the
landlord and leave to the tenant but a
bare living. In this drought-cursed conti-
nent, the lot of even the freehold farmer is
hard enough. It is with difficulty that he wrests
her grudged gifts from the reluctant earth.
Drought, bush-fires, rabbits, dust-storms, insect-
pests besiege him as he works through the flaming
hours, and unlucky Chance is always ready to
snatch away the fruit of his labors. The case of
the tenant farmer in a district where the land-
lords work on “ commercial principles ” is abso-
lutely hopeless. A poor season puts him in debt;
a rare good season only means that he can manage
to pay up his landlord and partially clear his
store account. When, finally, the plough-handle
drops from his gnarled hands, and he sinks down
to enrich the soil which he has tilled, he may
have won wealth for others but never anything
but the privilege to labor for himself. His place
is. taken by another victim of landlordism, and
his children scatter to the towns, where there is
some hope of a life which is not all labor.
» * * a
The effect of the system on the individual is
accompanied by a parallel effect on the soil. It
soon shares the hunger and the harsh misery of
the tenant. Since the rent must be found, and
the rent is graded on the highest productive power
of the soil, that crop which is likely to pay best
for the season is sown. Tlius around Bathurst
wheat has been cropped from the same fields year
after year until they are almost exhausted and will
produce a crop only under absolutely favorable
climatic conditions. Soon, if present circum-
stances continue, they will produce no crop and
pass out of cultivation, used up by the greed of
the owner and the forced greed of the tenant.
Bathurst landlordism has in its development pro-
duced the Chinese tenant. Fields which in Euro-
pean hands could not pay the rent and allow a
tenant to live, even in the most miserly fashion,
are cultivated by Chinese whose standard of living
enables them to meet the exactions of the
proprietor. *
* * « » •
There is no sense in blaming individual land-
lords alone for this state of affairs. They do but as
their neighbors do, and as they have been taught by
Irish, English, and Scotch examples. They act
in what they consider to be the best business
manner to secure the best dividends out of their
property. That their policy, even from a business
point of view, is short-sighted and ultimately
disastrous they cannot perceive, no more than the
sweating slop-manufacturer can see how great an
evil to the community is his desire for the biggest
dividend in the shortest possible time. But the
evil is so great as to demand immediate remedy. It
affects a very large proportion of the agricultural
lands which are in easy communication with the
big towns, and it threatens at an early date to
drive those areas out of cultivation altogether.
The following are some facts as to landlordism
in the South Coast (N.S.W.) district, to illustrate
not an unusually harsh system, but one which is
in many respects better than those ruling in other
parts of Australia. Other districts may be dealt
with later on.
* » * •
The vast Berry estate on the South Coast of
N.S.W. was originally a land grant in return for
services rendered, and once embraced an area
stretching along the coast some 15 miles and in-
land a considerable distance. The towns of Berry
Nowra, Gerringong, Toolijooa, and Jasper's Brush
have been built within this area, which is mostly
devoted to dairy farming. Its proximity to the
Sydney market (Berry is only 84 miles from
Sydney), and to the South Coast coal-mining
centres, .makes the area a highly favorable one
for farming. A large portion of it still remains
in the hands of one, or rather two, people—Dr.
Hay and the trustees of the Berry Estate Dr'
Hay and Mr. James Norton, M.L.C. The
original tenants of the Berry Estate found much


of their land in its “prairie” state. It had to be
fenced and cleared for grazing purposes. Home-
steads were erected, and the landlord gener-
i. • j con tributed to the cost of these —one-
third of the total sum ! The money for the other
improvements came from the tenant. And,
of course, all improvements belonged absolutely to
the landlord. No leases were given, and the land
was held on yearly tenancy. It was possible,
therefore, for a tenant, when he had finished
clearing, fencing, and building upon an area, to find
himself dispossessed almost immediately, and to
lose all the monejr and toil sunk in improvements.
But the commercial opportunities offering by such
conditions do not seem to have been taken full
advantage of by the landlord. Tenure seems to
have been seldom disturbed, and rents were fairly
moderate. When Dr. Hat came in as manager of
the estate before Mr. Berry’s death rents were
generally raised, and a system of lease for five or
seven years instituted. On Mr. Berry’s death,
the Berry Trust was formed. Rents were again
raised by some 30 per cent, in most cases and
seven years’ leases granted. Those leases having
now expired, rents are again being generally
raised, and notices to quit served on those who
refuse to agree to the increases. A typical in-
stance (proportional and not exact figures given):
Some 30 years ago a small farm was rented by one
farmer for £52 a year; the land was practically
unimproved and had no decent dwelling. A
homestead was built by the tenant and many
other improvements made, and afterwards the
rent Was raised to £IOO a year. The next in-
crease was to £144 a year, and now it is sought to
increase the rent to £166 a year. There is an option
of purchase but the price asked is such that the
interest on the purchase money would very nearly
equal the increased rent.
* * * *
The effect of the policy is that around Gerring’ong
the tenant-farmer works without pleasure in the
present or hope in the future. He wrests a mere
laborer’s wage, or less, out of the land, by toiling
from dawn to dark. The land suffers as it is over-
worked and overstocked. Trades-people suffer
because the over-burdened tenant is often unable
to pay his debts, and, when disaster comes to him,
the landlord has the first claim on his possessions.
About Berry, where Dr. Hay is sole landlord, con-
ditions are a little better; but there exists there
the same state of things in the main. Short
leases, no tenant right in improvements, and con-
stant reappraisement of rents, leave the tenant
absolutely at the mercy of the landlord.
* * * *
Recent increases in the rentals have brought the
matter of this South Coast estate prominently
before the public; and some of the tenants are
besieging the Pacific Ocean with their complaints.
But what avail grumblings against the landlord,
or pleadings to him for consideration? Facts
which will be published in regard to other
districts will show the Gerringong farmer that his
lot is the lot of many others, and will perhaps
convince him and the Government of the State
that it is from legislation that relief must be
sought.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Desolated Capitalist.
This remark, and it is a remark worth noting,
appeared recently in the financial column of
Sydney Telegraph :
Whatever may really be the trade outlook for the United
Kingdom, or for Australia, it cannot be denied that there
is a lot of pessimism about in both countries. We heard
a Sydney business man say, in effect, yesterday: “ X am
sending home my capital which can be spared, because
there is no profitable opening here, and what with social-
istic troubles and political pandering to the workmen
there is not likely to be any outlook for it.”
The Bulletin doesn’t profess to know whether
that Sydney business man really had any
existence, or really had any spare capital
or not—very few of them have nowadays —
but if he had he probably experienced all the
difficulty he describes in finding a promising out-
let for it. Supposing, at any time within the last
few years, there were a number of men in
Sydney with a lot of money which they wanted to
invest what could they have done with that
money 1 They wouldn’t be likely to start a new
fire insurance office ; the existing Australian fire
offices have been dying ou,t one by one for many
years, and the ground is so completely taken up
by about fifty or sixty old, solid, wealthy
British and foreign offices that there is hardly
a ghost of a show for a new local institution.
They wouldn’t be likely to start a new bank.
That business is already overdone, largely through
the fact that four English banks have so massive
a hold on things here. The only big new mining
field that has been foundin Australia inlateyearsis
practically in the possession of English companies.
Hardly anybody but a lunatic would put his
money into the establishment of a new importing
business; the English and German importing
firms are already swarming in Sydney like the
locusts that ate up Egypt. The pastoralist’s
business isn’t making bread and cheese for most
of those already in it; it has broken almost all
pastoral companies and half the banks, and it is
probably returning less than 2 per cent, all round.
As regards agriculture on a large scale, the State
already grows enough of most things for its own
requirements; it can’t absorb much more food
unless a large manufacturing population grows
up to consume it, and there is no money in
growing wheat to dump into the glutted English
market, in competition with countries that get
twice as much per acre and pay less than half the
freight. An American, under such conditions,
would organise a big manufacturing industry, but
N.S.W. has never had even one big manufacturing
industry, thanks to Ereetrade and foreign im-
ports. Its industries have been growing of late
years, especially since the advent of the allegedly
nefarious Labor party, but even now the bulk of
them are small jobbing and repairing businesses,
and the great competition of the importers pre-
vented them rising to anything better. They
average about 17 hands apiece, and there are very
few that return decent interest on the money
invested. It is no use trying to establish a new
shipping co.; the existing co.’s are laying up
vessels because the cheaply-manned British and
foreign cargo-tank is allowed to pervade our coasts
and swallow up the business. There is no real
shipbuilding in Australia; our ships are imported
from England under Ereetrade conditions, and
the local shipyards are mainly places where un-
avoidable repairs are done to British and foreign
vessels. As regards coal-mining, the biggest coal-
property in Australia is English-owned, and the
coal supply is already in excess of the demand.
Australia might put its capital into building its
own railways and public works by taking up State
loans, but most of the loans are offered in England,
and either the Australian isn’t allowed. to invest
at all, or is only allowed to invest provided he is
willing to accept less interest than the Britisher.
This insane policy has been followed for the last
four years. In the old days, the Australian with
money to dispose of went into land speculations,
but the artificial fever of the land-boom is dead,
and there is no money now in jobbing in city
allotments. Or he took up pastoral country, but
all the good and bad country has been taken up,
and the latter has ruined most of those
who took it. Or he went into y gold-mining,
but the supply of new goldfields appears
to have run out, and the absentee Bull
has largely taken possession of that industry
also. Mostly by reason of the insane policy
of the rich-man class ip encouraging foreign
capital to come in and take possession of every
industry, there has been for some years, in New
South Wales especially, hardly such a thing as a
promising opening for a Cornstalk citizen to invest
a large amount of capital in his own country.
If the foreign importer is shut out by a pro-
hibitive tariff, and the foreign steamer is totally
ejected from our coastal trade, and there is a 40
per cent, duty on imported vessels, and the ab-
sentee coal and gold mine-owners, money-lenders,
fire insurance offices, banks, etc., are discouraged
off the premises by a heavy absentee tax, then
there may be a prospect for that Cornstalk citizen.
He will have openings then in manufactures, ship-
building, shipowning, in mining, in mortgage loans,
in establishingn.ew banks and fire insurance offices,
and mapy other lines. As it is, and especially
as it has been, he simply sat down on his pile to
little profit, and charged the consequences of his
own folly on the Labor party and the eight-hour
movement. It is a curious situation for a country
which has so little capital of its own to be
hardly able to find a promising in its own
country for even that little, but the policy of
selling almost every promising industry to the
absentee Britisher (commonly described as the
influx of foreign capital to develop the industries
of this great country) has brought that state of
things to pass.

An Excited M.L.C.
The recent violent and boorish attitude of a gutter
section of the German press towards Britain has
been the cause of some weird correspondence in
the Sydney papers. The weirdest correspondent
of the crowd was probably Dr. Creed, M.L.C.,
who proposed that Britain should levy a special
20 per cent, duty on German products, and there-
by wipe that stiff and bloated empire off the face
of the commercial globe. Presumably Germany
would retaliate with a similar duty, and then
Britain’s best customer—the one that buys more
from her than any other State on earth, and al-
most the only one'which buys an immense amount
more from Britain than Britain buys from her —
would be gone, and the sympathetic American
would take Britain’s place in the German
markets. The same excited personage assures
Australia that it can burst up Germany by
stopping supplies, for Germany’s imports from
this country “are solely of raw material
which she must have for her workers under any
circumstances.” Unfortunately, almost the only
thing we export to Germany is wool, and Argen-
tina, which has now more sheep than all Australia
put together, is just longing for a chance to take
over the trade, and sell to Germany all the wool it
can ask for. There was a time when Australia
might have made foreign States sit up by
threatening to cut off their wool supply, but
that is a thing of the past. Also, Creed,
M.L.C., proposes that Germany, which
helps to furnish Great Britain with flour,
sugar, and other articles of food, should be
starved into submission by Britain cutting off its
food supply by sea, and assures Australia that
“the earnings of theGermanworkerswould thereby
be destroyed almost entirely.” Orbed, M.L.C.,
is actually of opinion that Germany, which im-
ports very little food, and which has the inex-
haustible food supplies of Russia and Austria
at its back-door, could be reduced to hunger by a
sea blockade. Of course, such a blockade would
distress Germany greatly, but it wouldn’t cut off
any food supply, and it might make some
articles of food cheaper by preventing their
export by sea. It wouldn’t cut off quite half
Germany’s commerce, for the country’s land
frontiers couldn’t be blockaded. It wouldn’t
even cut off its wool supply, for Argentine wool
could reach it through France or Austria. And
meanwhile Australia, at war with its third best cus-
tomer, and Britain with its best customer, and India
with its fourth-best customer, would be suffer-
ing also. Creed, M.L.C., also proposes that it
should be enacted that no goods or passengers be
carried between any of the ports of the empire
“except in vessels under the British flag, owned
and officered by British subjects.” That would
be an ordinance directed at all the outside world,
and if the outer world retaliated then Britain,
whose ships are wandering on half the coasts
of the globe, would have a bad time. Aus-
tralia, which has no ships on foreign coasts,
might enact such a sweeping law, but if
Britain did it would open the way for
some surprising experiences. Three-fourths of
Britain’s shipping trade is with countries outside
the empire, and if it proceeded thus_ to stoush all
the extraneous parts of the planet, it would very
probably provoke them to make counter-move-
ments against British shipping. It is all very
good for irresponsible and excited M’sL.C. to
plunge around in their declining years after this
fashion, but Chamberlain has his cold glazed eye
fixed on many things—the main chance among the
rest.

The Slaughter of the Children.
The killing of the Boer children in the British
concentration camps still goes on, and with
scarcely-diminished speed. The just-cabled returns
show that, of a total of 60,588 adults in the cjtfnps
during December, 613 died ; of a total of 56,424
children 1767 died ; so that out of 117,012 persons
2380 died —a death-rate of 244 per 1000 per annum,
or about twenty times the normal Australasian
death-rate. The children died at the rate of
377 per 1000 per annum ; —that is, the death-
rate of the Boer children up to 1% years of age is
thirteen times the death-rate of .N.S.W. children
up to five years of age—the period most fatal of
all to infant life. Here is a record of the killing
for six months :
White White as,,!*- Chib
adults in children in j fV dren’s Total,
camps. camps. ' deaths.
July .. 47,574 46,366 288 1,124 1,412
Aug. .. 55,389 52,036 368 1,510 1,878
Sept. .. 55,092 54,326 447 1,964 2,411
Oct. .. Not stated Not stated! 1 059 4 904 5 963
Nov. .. Not stated Not stated / ’
Dec. .. 60,588 56,424 613 1,767 2,380
Total for six months .. 2,775 11,269 14,044
In two years and two months of war, to Dec. 31st
1901, the total of British killed in South Africa
was 18,964. In six months of the prison-camps
the total of Boer non-combatants killed, chiefly
women and children, was 14,044. British soldiers,
exposed to all the risks of war, have been killed at
the rate of 730 per month. Boer non-combatants,
exposed only to the risks of the prison-camps,
have been killed at the rate of 2340 per
month. It is nearly three times as deadly to
live in a British prison-camp as it is to be
exposed to Boer hostilities ! No excuses, no
apologies, can palliate this monstrous holo-
caust of women’s and children’s lives. And
there is no calumny here; for these are
official figures. Now, Lord Ketchener offers,
with colossal irony, to allow the Boer women and
children to return to their homes! To their
homes ! To the waste and blackened veldt! To
the ruined farms, the burnt crops, the houses
smouldering in ashes ! Ketchener asks the Boer
commandant, harried from place to place by British
troops, to become responsible for the 100,000 women
and children that have been drafted into the
prison-ca*ips like sheep for the slaughter! Yet
we talk of “ German calumnies.” What calumnies
could surpass the fact? To the end of time,
those thousands of African graves will cry
disgrace upon the British nation which counten-
anced and permitted the slaughter—ay ! and upon
Australia, which uttered no protest.

A Manufacturer’s Deputy.
The following gem is taken from the Federal Par-
liamentary reports of last week :
Mr. Conroy: I decline to accept instructions FROM
ANY MANUFACTURERS’ DEPUTY. Go and get your
instructions from the manufacturers, but don’t pass them
on to me.
This was CONROY, the Foreign-trader, addressing
Mauger, the Protectionist. Mauger, it may be
explained, is a manufacturers’ deputy without any
doubt. He has long been the secretary of an
organisation formed for the purpose of urging
local industries ahead, and until he ran against
the M ‘Millan and Conroy push he probably
never dreamt that anyone could expect him to be
ashamed of the fact. He knows better now. Mauger
seldom speaks without some Foreign-trader or
other asking, with scorn and loathing, if the
House is to be diotated to by a deputy of the
manufacturers. Mauger has the misfortune to be
distinctively the representative of an Australian
class which provides work and wages for thou-
sands of Australian artisans, and spends its profits
in Australia, and is content, on the average,
so far as can be gathered from the balance-
sheets of manufacturing companies, to scrape
an 8 per cent, dividend for itself out of the
business. And the Conroy crowd, which acts
as agent and flunkey for the rich importing gang
that mostly has its headquarters in England, and
spends its 10 and 15 per cent, dividends there,
can’t conceal its tremendous disgust at a man who
merely represents Australian industries getting
between the wind and its Calico Jimmyism. The
crowd which acts as an aggregate political pimp
for the wealthy absentee merchants mentions the
fact that Mauger represents Australian industries
as a matter which is so obviously disgraceful that
it only needs to be mentioned to cover him with
shame. Quite recently the same member was
charged with “living on the manufacturers,” or
words to that effect, and that was also mentioned
as if it were an unburied corpse being brought to
light. Yet the anti-Australian gang of importers
was even then passing round the eleemosynary
hat for a certain Freetrade politician, in order that
he might be better able to maintain the battle of
Calico Jimmy against Australian manufacturers.
AndM‘Mii.LAN, the man who scoffs at “wretched”
Australian industries, lives directly on the impor-
ters. He represents the absentee M‘ Arthurs, and
liis scorn for the men that have sunk so low as to
represent Australian manufacturers, who spend
their profits in the country and employ Australian
labor, is also something lofty and tremendous.
Australia pays men like Conroy and M‘Mti,lan
to jeer at its “wretched” industries, and to
point out indignantly the baseness of anybody
who represents Australian manufactures in an
Australian Legislature. And N.S.W. supplies
these men. And N.S.W. has also, for many
years, sacrificed about £lOO,OOO a-year to protect
the Freetrade papers which boost wp and adver-
tise these men. It isn’t many countries which
would do as much.
A Mental Reservation.
Premier Barton’s speech on the “patriotic
motion,” which he ac,cep ted at the dictation of
Mr. Reid, and about his broken 'jfromise not to
send away a contingent without first consulting
Parliament, was a masterly defence of the doctrine
of “ mental reservation” :
I want to make it clear that all that was in my mind
when I answered the question in the House and made the
promise not to act without giving an opportunity for dis-
cussion was that the matter was not seething in the
public mind at the time, and we had not then made up
our minds to make an offer.
So in future, when Premier Barton makes a
promise, we are not to believe what he says, but
to try and find out what he “has in his mind.”
Meanwhile, it is worth while for Australia to
make a note of this: There is no lie, no mean-
ness, no treachery, no broken promise, no aban-
donment of principle, no selling of truth or
honor, no crawling-down or backing-out, no wile,
dodge, or betrayal that a man can’t be guilty of, if
he once fully persuades himself that an alleged
mental reservation when he gave his word is
sufficient reason why he need not 'hold his word
sacred. Hereafter, when Premier Barton pro-
mises anything it will be essential to ask him if it
is a reliable promise, which he means to keep, or a
promise that is nullified by something he has, or
is going to have, in his mind. If Parliament
treated Premier "Barton as Premier Barton has
treated Parliament,-and promised him his salary
with a mental reservation, his remarks on the
subject would probably be worth listening to.

In a New Character.
George Reid—he of the glazed eye and the Dry
Dog—having kept to his tents for some time,
waiting for the great Freetrade Party to send
for him, or possibly send something to him,
and not being sent for, has evidently turned his
thoughts in another direction. The High Com-
missionership of Australia, with its salary of
£5OOO. a year, which public opinion has generally
marked out for B. R. Wise, is still an “open
billet ”; and George Reid has evidently con-
cluded-that there are worse stages on which to
lag moderately superfluous than London, where
he can be the Commonwealth’s representative.
There or thereabouts, it is commonly reported, lies
the reason for his sudden access of friendliness
towards the Barton Ministry. He helped Pre-
mier Barton out of the large hole and the
great difficulty in which his vacillation," his
Eolitical treachery, and his gross breach of faith
ad involved him. George Reid ,is not the man
who does salvage work of this kind for nothing ;
he doesn’t even rescue his friend out of the hole
gratis, let alone his enemy. If Barton had been
allowed to meet Parliament and face the direct
issue of his broken faith, the debate would have
been damaging, if not absolutely disastrous. But
Reid, in “afriendly letter,” suggested the clever
move of drawing the German herring across the
trail, and Parliament met to Barton’s
broken compact, but the rude language of the
German press. No member, whatever his
opinions, cared to take the responsibility of ap-
pearing in the public eye as the champion of Teu-
tonic boorishness and Prussian junkerism ; and an
incident which threatened danger was passed over
with eclat and “cheers for the Empire.” At the
same time, George REiD’took the opportunity to
Sut himself right with the Chamberlain party, in
Ingland by a perfervid speech, which should wipe
out all memory of his action in pigeon-holing the
first request for Australian troops for service in
Africa. All this has a purpose. George Reid is
not likely to have reconsidered his war attitude
simply for the amusement of political gymnastics.
He used to do such things, but he grows too old
and heavy for the frivolous boundings of a few
years ago. All things point to the conclusion that
the High Commissionership lias attracted him,
and that he regards it as probable that Premier
Barton will gladly seize the chance to get rid of
a dangerous opponent. A friend promoted is, in
practical politics, a friend lost; an enemy pro-
moted is an enemy lost, and enemies are more
easily spared. Even if he is missed when he is
gone a new enemy can always be found to take his
place.
M'MikLAN’s Sydney speech to importers and
Freetraders was stuffed as full of stupidities as an
egg is full of meat. Ho wanted to know, for
example, “What is the good of Australia pre-
dominating in the Pacific if she has got no trade
in the Pacific ? ” The- Bulletin wants to know
what is the good of M‘Millan predominating in
his own family if he can’t sell things to his wife
and children ? The tariff has killed the Fiji
banana trade and the Hebrides coffee trade, has
it? All the better, since it has.given fresh life to
coffee and banana production'in Queensland. The
skin is closer than the coat pand one tropical agri-
culturist in Australia, employing white labor, is
worth to us more than 100 in Fiji, employing black
labor. But it isn’t so good for the Foreign-trade
middleman, and that is where M‘Millan’s trouble
comes in. The Queensland coffee and bana,na
production is still far short of the Australian
demand, but it will catch up. And when Aus-
tralia produces those, and other things, for ithelf
it won’t have to send tribute to England to keep
M‘Millan’s absentee bosses in luxury.
M’Millan complained bitterly to the Freetraders,
in his recent Sydney speech, that the Federal
tariff practically destroyed Australia’s imports
of Maoriland bacon, ham and cheese. All the
better for Australia. Australia doesn’t intend to
live by paying M.L. farmers to produce things
that its own farmers can produce. When the
article imported is one we can grow ourselves,
then “trade” is a mere useless and costly
humping of goods from one place to another.
All buying of goods and carrying of them
round the corner is trade; it is only when
the article is carried over a certain line
marked on the map that a record is kept of
it —but it is all trade, none the less. M‘Millan,
by having his breakfast cooked at home instead of
having it bought at the hotel and carried to his
house on a tray through a dust-storm, is destroy-
ing the commerce in cooked victuals—the export
of them from the hotel in the next street, and the
import of them to his own house, and the impor-
tant trade in carrying the tray round the corner.
The middleman’s reverence for the art of carrying
things round the corner is a holy emotion, but he
fails at times to live up to his Joss.
That Protest from Balmain.
At a meeting of the Balmain branch of the Australian
Natives’ Association, held in the local Town Hall,
the following resolution was carried That the
Balmain branch of the Australian Natives’ Association
strongly protests against the utterances of the German
Imperial Chancellor (Count VON Bulow) in practically
supporting what he must know are base canards in re-
gard to the British conduct of the South African war, in
view of which this branch of the A.N.A. urges concerted
action on the part of all patriotic Australians in protest-
ing against such utterances on the part of a so-called
friendly Power. Also, that this resolution be forwarded
to the board of directors for transmission by them through
the proper channels to the British Government.”
The Chancellor von Bulow
Sat in his State Bureau,
Inscrutable and cold
As some grim knight of old.
Upon his right hand stood
His Secretary good :
Said he—“ Herr von Bulow
All England’s in a glow.
“ With anger and amaze
Its Press is all ablaze.
Alas, Herr von Bulow,
You have the world for foe ! ”
The Chancellor von Bulow,
Inscrutable and slow,
Said—“ What are they to me,
Or our Great Germany ? ”
Then, clanking through the door,
Strode in the Emperor.
He threw his naked sword
Upon the Council board.
“ My Chancellor,” said he,
“ This touches you and me.
Our honor is at stake ;
Let Germany awake!
“ Observe this paper here —
It’s haughty flaunt and jeer ;
Our imperial course is plain—
Make war against Balmain.”
He flung his helmet tall.
Against the office wall,
And swung his Sword of Dread
Three times around his head.
Then quickly down below
His table dropped Bulow ;
The Secretary, he
Lay flat and silently.
The Kaiser’s wrath passed by.
Yet scorn was in his eye ;
Then from his posture low
Rose Chancellor Bulow.
The Secretary, too,
Arose, with eye askew—
“ 0 Kaiser, Sovereign Lord,
Put up that dreadful sword.
“ ’Tis not Balmain alone
That rages round your Throne ;
Its threat is backed to-day
By all the A.N.A.”
The Kaiser, Sovereign Lord,
Put up his dreadful sword—
“ Lie low, Bulow,” said he,
“There’s peace in Germany.”

There is a wild row jusfcaiow in the Scotch
medical community of Sydney. When Hector
Macdonald visited the city its Scotch citizens, and
more particularly its Scotch doctors, decided to
g've him all the haggis that the heart of man
could desire, and some of them cheerfully guaran-
teed to put up any haggis money that was
required, assured, seemingly, that the guarantee
would never have to be honored. But after
Hector had had his haggis there was a wee
deefeecit, ye ken—some £5O or so—and a very
solemn gathering of Scotch doctors assembled in
consultation as to ways and means of removing
the growth. Then one young man (a very young
man who didn’t know his Sydney or his brother
doctors well), airily remarked that the £5O could be
easily managed; such and such a big gun had
guaranteed £lO, another £lO, another £5 and so
on. A frozen silence held those doctors, and you
might have heard a claymore drop. Afterwards,
it is rumored, the young man was threatened with
very dire consequences indeed by one big gun.
The threats are regarded in other quarters as
“most injudicious”—perhaps as calculated to
warn a person who should have been silently
extirpated— and the shadow of Hector’s haggis
is dark over the medical quarters of Sydney.
