“The Professor’s Love Story” (a comedy
by J. M. Barrie, produced at Sydney Palace last
Saturday) shows literary rather than dramatic
skill; yet the development of the slender tale
should have made but slight demands on the
resources of a play-wright with a fair knowledge
of the technique of his profession. But then IVlr.
Barrie is not a playwright, but a novelist. His
choice of dramatic expedients in this piece shows
the amateur: the eavesdropping scene of the
third act, for example, is strained and un-
natural, verging on the altogether improbable.
And, to be of the first class, a comedy must
develope on the lines of absolute naturalness.
Apart from its errors of technique, “ A Professor’s
Love Story ”is of rare literary flavor—a graceful
little play, full of quaint humor and tender pathos.
It boasts no sensations, no fitful flash of glaring
epigram, but, in soft grey and rose, the story of a
dawning love is unfolded, through its merry fun
and its brief trial, to its happy ending. To some
of the characters the author has been very kind.
Professor Good willie and Dr. Cosens, for instance,
are drawn firmly and finely. The women of the
play are not so well treated ; Lucy White, the
heroine, is rather colorless, and is given two
distinct characters—one for the afternoon (act II.),
the other for the evening (act III.); and a woman
can’t change her character as she changes her
hat. In act I. Lucy White is secretary to
the absent-minded, old-young Professor who,
without knowing it, has fallen in love with her,
and finds as the chief symptom of the sickness a
disinclination for work. The situation is quite
clear to her, however; and it is put beyond all
doubt by an accusation from Dr. Cosens that she
is trying to trap the Professor into marriage. Yet
she follows the Professor to Scotland, and, by a
trick, gets him to propose : then, in bitter sorrow
at the trick, wants to rush away from the nup-
tials, and is recovered, under rather unfinished
literary circumstances, to be happily kissed by a
rejuvenated man of learning. Now the girl in Act
I. wouldn’t feel any bitter sorrow in Act 111. for
Act II.: she would be rather cogitating on her
trousseau. But the story is pretty ; it is prettily
told ; it is adorned with much subtle humor ; it is
charmingly stage-pictured, and that makes it more
than good enough. V
• * * *
As for the acting of “ The Professor’s
Love Story ” it is of the highest order of merit.
Charles Arnold as the Professor (especially in
Act I.) plays with sympathy and power, holding
the audience every moment that he is on the
stage. Mr. Arnold has never before done any-
thing so good, even in the palmy young days of
“ Hans the Boatman.” Mr. Thomas supplies a
very effective character cketch of a consulting
doctor, and Mr. George Willougby of an M.P.
with somewhat under the political average of
brains. Two pawky Scotch laborers are also
capitally acted by Messrs. Denton and Sharp.
The women characters have not been so well
treated by the author. Miss Ada Lee has the
best playing part (Effie Proctor) and does it every
justice. Miss Dot Frederics as the heroine gives
full expression to what charm and grace that
inconsistent young person possesses. The audi-
ence on the opening night was very large, but not
markedly enthusiastic. It seemed to nave been
expecting something of the “Jones ” brand, and
its frame of mind was that of a bear who looked
for a bun and found a rose. But either the origi-
nal bear will rise to higher things, or the kind of
bear that really wants a rose, hearing that there
is one available at the Palace, will hasten there on
all his four feet.
** » *
At Sydney Royal “ Alice in Wonderland ”
still appeals mainly to the juvenile section of the
community. Certain variety items introduced
into the second edition caused a brief spasm of
adult interest, but the items had been seen so
often elsewhere that it was only a brief
spasm. If a third edition is contemplated, The
Bulletin suggests some really good, well-dressed,
well-staged ballets. There is not a decent big
ballet in the show, and so the men nearing their
second childhood stay away, and are not brought
along in their Bath chairs as they should be. A
pantomime can never really succeed without the
unanimous support of the men in their second
childhood, or thereabouts.
«* * *
Verdi’s “Otello” flashed across Her
Majesty’s stage (Sydney) one night last week, and,
although it showed up the cracks and seams in
Williamson’s 00., received a tremendous ovation.
’Tis a marvellous composition: its strains glitter
through the memory like jewels seen through wine.
The tumultuous anger of Otello in the third act is
infectious; it spreads to the audience, and when
the last angry echo has died away it leaves a sense
of wrong for which only death can atone. Many
a man, after seeing “ Otello,” would gladly go
away and slay all his creditors on the spot.
Bozzoli’s impersonation of lago, the damned, was
rather wooden, and the claw-like action of his
hand was incessant. Sapio’s renderings of the
“Ave Maria” and heart-breaking “Willow Song”
were things to be remembered. Larizza, as the
Moor, was the best of the cast, and even he didn’t
strike one as being equal to the splendors of the
opportunity. Larizza is 40, which age is a bit of
a drawback in the upper voice-register. He is a
lawyer, and practises m his ain countree—whether
in the higher courts, Or in defence of Giovanni, the
drunk, when he has used bad language on the
Campagna, writer knows not.
** * *
Operas trod close on each other’s heels at
Her Majesty’s, last week. ‘‘ Feodora ” had a good
run. Bassich as a tragedienne, and Dani’s fine,
restrained acting of Loris Ipanoff, are attractions
that outweigh even the drawbacks of the music.
Giaconda ” was repeated on Saturday night. A
group of the just-arrived “ Ben-Hur”-ites were in
the manager’s box. The chief operatic attraction
of this week was “ Ernani.”
** * *
The Empire calls the people of Sydney to
its pantomime as per usual, and many contingents
have been despatched in that direction. But the
days of Robinson, like the hairs of our head, are
numbered, and when this paper is published
Robinson’s days will be only two. The hairs above-
mentioned are, in many cases, just about as fre-
quent nowadays. The Empire, when it has lost
its Robinson, will go back to the variety business,
and trusts, by civility and prompt attention to
business and a reasonable display of legs, to pro-
sper as aforetime.
* * *
At Sydney Criterion “ For the Sake of a
Woman ” goes off this week to make room for the
Executioner’s Daughter.” Miss Roxy Barton,
who took the part of the heroine for the last week
or so, acts in a rather vigorous and wide-awake
manner considering what a mild, simple-
minded young person she impersonates. Miss
Barton has a good voice, which she does not quite
know how to manipulate, and the little gasp she
gives every now and then somewhat detracts
from what is, for so young an actress, a good per-
formance. Miss Helene Burdette keeps up to her
usual high standard, her denunciation of the hero
shortly before her murder being a specially fine
bit of declamation.
** * *
On the coming Saturday “For a Child’s
Sake ” is to be staged at Sydney Lyceum. The
new play is by Henry Herman, part author of
Silver King,” which may mean a good deal, or
again it may not. When two writers collaborate fora
time, and then give up collaborating, it generally
means that one of them has found the other out,
and if Herman was the man who found out Jones!
and not the one who was found out himself, then his
new play should be worth considering. It
is produced “ by arrangement with W. J. Hollo-
way, Esq.” The Bulletin notes with some satis-
faction that not nearly so many things happen by
arrangement with W. J. Holloway, Esq., as for-
merly. There was a time when the solar system
was only saved from general collision by arrange-
ment with W. J. Holloway, Esq.
«* * #
Sydney Tivoli found no reason to change
its programme last week, and thus gives a hurried
critic opportunity to mention some of the smaller
lights in the general illumination. Val Vousden,
a female impersonator, who reads a letter from
Dan O’Connor nightly, is a sufficiently humorous
person to be always welcome. The Williams
Sisters, four lithe-limbed Australian girls, who
juggle on bicyles, also provide a good turn ; and
Miss Ada Delroy, in addition to her flame-dance,
contributes a pleasant bell-duet, being assisted by
a male person who bears the same name as the
implement. “High and Low” is the first song
of Miss Lizzie Collins now, and it exactly describes
the voice of that gorgeously-gowned lady. One
moment it is lowest contraito, the next highest
?ad she been caught young and trained
Miss Lizzie might have been the world’s contralto
now.
*« * #
Business seems to have slowed down at
all the Melbourne theatres, though the pantomime
matinee crowds continue to be great and jubilant
As regards “San Toy,” at Her Majesty’s, the
drop m the attendance amounts almost to a slump
Public opinion has decided that “San Toy,” even
on the demerits of its book, doesn’t go as gaily as
it might have gone. There are few positive and
a lot of negative faults in the performance, and
where these faults are musical the orches-
tra is to blame. Composer Jones is a pretty
melodist .as a rule, but his tunes are
not strikingly original or remarkably various
therefore the orchestra should show them
all the delicate consideration possible instead of
making too muchee noise. A duet between Miss
Young and Bathurst is a rather effective piece of
operatic shoddy, in which the two fine voices seem
to be kept under, rather than brought out by the
accompaniment. The full strength of the lady’s
glorious top-note is required to score a success for
that duet. Miss Young’s warble being the most
admired item of “San Toy,” it has been re-
marked, with sorrow and surprise, that she gets
no chance to shake up the second act, wherein
Miss Palotta invites a stern reproof from this
journal by talking a silly song about Rhoda, who
ran a, Pagoda. Now that Miss P.’s foreign accent
has lost its novelty, a good many people feel hurt
at her conversational singing, except when she
can give point to the refrain by picking up her
in the best Palotta manner. In “San
Toy ” she hasn’t the assistance of a long skirt
consequently the story of Pagoda Rhoda comes
very painful.
** * *
The three acrobats who go to Africa to be
walked upon are still stretching themselves across
a chasm at Melbourne Royal. A small weekly
journal with a Freetrade mission lately alluded
to the aforesaid “Living Bridge” as a master-
piece of John Brunton’s art, but in more enlight-
ened circles the Horley Bros, are recognised as
things apart from the scenery. Some nights
more than others, the middleman of the Bridge
looks an unsafe plank in Mrs. Holt’s platform,
and Mrs. H. seems to tread very lightly on his
innards. Nevertheless, she has made about 25
escapes from the Arabs with complete success,
and the health of “ The Span of Life” appears to
be satisfactory.
** * *
<l The Colleen Bawn,” at the Melbourne
Gaiety Theatre, is an Irish drama, produced under
distressful circumstances. The Gaiety, in fact, is
not a theatre, but a hall, and a small-staged hall
at that, whereas Maggie Moore is a well-grown
colleen, who needs elbow-room for her emotion
and plenty of lake to fall into when Danny Mann
attempts to drown her marriage - certificate.
Also, the drama itself seems unable to
breathe an air of romance when the proscenium
comes close to the orchestra-reserve and the
front row of stalls is within easy distance of the
back cloth. “Colleen Bawn,” in its present
domicile, is not a persuasive fiction. The com-
pany, for the most part, does well, or fairly well;
but the solemn exhortations of Father Tom seem
rather short weight, and Hardress Cregan fails to
account for the interest that. Eily and Danny and
other people take in his somewhat wooden Career.
Hardress, although not a noble character, is ex-
pected to be a more imposing devil of a fellow than
Vincent Scully makes him.
** * *
Wallace Brownlow is claiming £lOOO
damages from the Sandringham (Melb.) Coffee
Palace for injuries received through stepping out
of a door that led to a fall of 25 feet, with hard
stuff at the end of the distance. Newspaper
paragraphs state the result of the accident was a
sprained ankle,” but, as a matter of fact, the
doctors don’t know how much the beauteous
baritone may have hurt himself. Of late he has
lived at a private hospital, coming out for an hour
m the evening to sing at Melb. Bijou, and all the
time he seems to be getting worse instead of
better.
** * #
Aladdin, at Melbourne Princess’s,
shows sundry changes in Nellie Stewart’s cos-
tumes, one of which is a gorgeous yellow robe,
elaborately trimmed with pantomime rubies and
other valuables. This is the raiment Aladdin
wears when he drops into the Emperor’s Court at
Pekin to notifv Princess Maud Harrington of
his intention to marry her. The surface
value of Aladdin would be about two and
a-half millions sterling on a jeweller’s rough
calculation, but dear little Maud shakes her head
sadly at the young man, and says she will never
be able to get the Imperial consent to her mar-
riage with a pauper. Then Nellie Aladdin ex-
plains that he is no longer sunk in deepest
poverty, but has become a multi - billionaire,
and the Princess is expected to give a light
soprano cry of astonishment. Subsequently, the
other smart boy (Miss Rose Hamilton) come along
with the other nice sympathetic girl (Miss Ada
Willoughby) and they all sing their love for one
anot her in a sweet and feeling strain. Musgrove’s
extravaganza has all sorts of charms for all kinds
of onlookers. The onlooker who is now speaking
finds a particular charm in the intelligence of the
performers. There are no flies on the co. this
year.
** * *
At Melbourne Bijou (where the names of
the World’s Entertainers are politely painted on
an electric-lighted board for the information of the
outside world) everything is proceeding as usual
except, perhaps, the Lottos, who have been
announced to proceed somewhere else. To-night
(Ihursday) is ‘a special American night ” at the
Bijou. On this occasion Henry Lee produces
himself as a series of famous Americans, ranging
from Croker to Roosevelt, and the Star-Spangled
Banner will probably sing “Yankee Doodle”
with variations.
** * *
The Opera House is seeing the last week
of Peggy Pryde, for the present, and, in place of
Beg., somebody from New York or Sydney may
be expected next Saturday. Otherwise, last
week’s programme remains up to date. The
Jackson Family is growing on the affections of a
moderately musical populace, because the Jacksons
are bright and unassuming entertainers as well as
clever musicians. In fact, they promise to be a
very valuable engagement to Rickards, who has
never previously staged a musical “ turn ” of the
same class as the J acksons’.
The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Owing to the language of some irresponsible
German papers, a multitude of foolish people-meiudimr
people who from their positions, might be expected to
know something of trade statistics—have written to the
papers, asking why Britain and Australasia should go on
buying from the objectionable Teuton and thereby keep
that person out of the Insolvency Court with our
good red gold. A general boycott of German goods in
Britain and Australasia has even been proposed. Unfor-
tunately, this is how the trade statistics really go
according to the latest published returns : ’
Sold to Bought from Balance in
Germany Germany Britain’s favor
. (year). (year). (vear)
Britain .. £37,9*8,267 .. £30,123,058 .. £7,855,199
Balance in Aus-
tralasia’s favor
Australasia 2,901,363 .. 2,244,154 ..
Germany may be darned uncivil, but if Britain and Aus-
tralasia start an effective boycott of Germany’s goods,
and Germany retaliates in like fashion, both Britain and
Australasia will be the losers. India will also be an
enormous loser. It isn’t so much a case of us supporting
the German by buying his goods, as of the German sup-
porting us by buying our goods—this despite all the
jokes about Made in Germany.”
Tti® trouble about the trade-boycott idea is that
the countries which curse and ridicule and abuse the
empire most are also those which do most to keep us out
of bankruptcy. France and Germany may be hostile,
beastly and objectionable, but they buv from Australasia
IP ° ne year, according to latest available statistics,
£6,794,669 worth of goods, and sell to us only £2,811,805
worth. Australasia has to export and turn into cash
every year about £14,000,000 worth more goods than it
imports in order to pay interest on its public and
private debts, and we make out of these two unfriendh'
countries almost £4,000,000 of this amount. If it was
possible to mark our displeasure at French and German
caricatures by shutting up the trade with these two
countries, then Australasia would probably be bankrupt.
The United States, Scandinavia and Japan are friendly
enough, but we buy from them over £2,400,000 worth
more than we sell to them, and, if the trade with those
three States was closed up by a boycott, they would lose—
not we.
Britain is in almost exactly the same situation
as Australasia. France and Germany taken together are,
m one sense, our best customers —better even than Great
Britain. That is to say our trade with them shows a bigger
average surplus in our favor than our trade with Britain
does, and it is largely the gold they pay us to make
good the difference which enables us to pacify
our British creditors. France is the better sup-
port of the two —in fact, it isn’t uncommon for us to
make a bigger profit on our trade with France alone than
on our trade with Britain ; and the friendly United
States are, in a trade sense, our very worst loss. Britain
in like manner finds Germany its best customer, and the
United States its very' worst. Germany buys more from
Britain than does any other State ; even all Australasia or
all India isn’t so good a customer. The trade with Ger-
many shows a bigger margin in favour of Britain than
does the trade of any' other State or any British depen-
dency, and the gold that Germany sends in payment for
the extra goods she buys from Britain is one of the things
which enables Britain to pay for the goods the Yankee
sends her, seeing that the Yankee (who is Britain’s bit-
terest commercial enemy, just as Germany is almost the
only commercial friend Britain possesses) won’t take
goods in return. Germany, in fact, pay's Brita n nearly
£8,000,000 a year for the privilege of calling her bad
names. And the United States gets nearly £90,000,000 a
year from Britain as the price of its friendship.

The Hannans Oroya Gold Mining Co., Kal-
goorlie, have lately placed an order with the Austral
Otis Company, South Melbourne, for a double drum first
motion winding engine. The cylinders are 18 inch
diameter by 36 inch stroke, and are fitted with balanced
piston valves. The drums are Bft. in diameter and fitted
with indicators. The engine is completely controlled
from the driver’s platform. In all respects is similar to
the engines working so satisfactorily at the Golden Horse-
shoe Mine, Boulder, Long Tunnel, Victoria, and the Cobar
Chesney Mine, N.S.W. *

Vanning Tables.— The very newest and what
gives exceedingly fair promise of superseding all other
tables is the machine known as the Wesle.v-Baird Vanning
Table. The table is of South Australian invention, and
has given excellent results on Broken Hill ores and ores
containing pyrites, carbonate of copper and tin. Like all
tables, the best work is done on “ fines,” and more espe-
cially when the product is properly classified. The
Central Mining and Tramway Co., of 424 Little Collins-
street, Melbourne, and 40 rfunter-sbreet, Sydney, are the
sole agents in the Eastern States. The table has a capa-
city up to 30 tons per day.*

June, 1900.. 27,447 15,447 3000 6000 15,000
Dec., 1900.. 27,713 15,713 3000 6000 18,000
June, 1901.. 28,350 16,350 3000 6000 21,000
Dec., 1901.. 28,745 16,745 3000 6000 24,000
The nominal net profit (£12,000, after writing £16,745 off
losses) is equal to £5 10s. 7d. per cent, per annum on the
shareholders’ funds (capital and reserves) as at the open-
ing of the half-year. The gross profits represent
£l3 4s. lid. percent. These are respectable figures, but
when a bank gets so much of its deposits at something
like £2 12s. 6d. per cent., and has the bulk of its deposits
so securely fixed that it isn’t necessary to keep any
cash reserve against them, the fact that the above rates
are earned on the very small shareholders’ funds now
remaining (capital £412,985 and reserve at opening of
half-year, £21,000) is in no way remarkable.
The balance-sheet, as showing the real position
of the Queensland National Bank, is of about as little
value as usual. When the great investigation took place
at the end of 1896 the deficit was estimated at about
£2,432,000. Against this there was a paid-up capital of
about £887,000, and some accumulated profits. Some-
thing approaching £500,000 of the loss was covered
by writing off some capital and all accumulated profits.
This apparently left the bank with a deficit of about
£1,900,000, against which there was some £450,000 of
capital remaining. This deficit of £1,900,000 was partly pro-
vided for on paper by the bank professing to write off one-
fourth (ss. in the £) of its reconstructed deposits, but
as it pledged itself to repay out of profits, when profits
were available, the sums allegedly written off, it can’t
fairly be said to be any more free of this liability than it
was before. The rest of the deficit, whatever it might
prove to be, remained as an asset in the balance-sheet,
to be gradually written off as profit allowed. All that is
definitely known, therefore, is that there was an apparent
deficit, some four or five years ago, of about £1,900,000,
with £450,000 of capital to set against it. And since
June, 1897, £219,160 has been earned, and the shortage is
reduced by that amount, if no further losses have been
made or discovered in the interim. Also, it may have
been further reduced by the sale of securities at more
than their written-down value—or again, it may not.
The Bcudetin is still of opinion that this indefinite way
of presenting a balance-sheet is a mockery. A balance-
sheet ought'.to show the exact position of the concern,
however disastrous, and should reveal the whole deficit.
Even if a great part of that deficit is only liable to be
liquidated out of profits it is still some kind of a liability,
and should be shown as such. ,
The best features about the Queensland National
Bank are that the gross profits are increasing, a certain
amount of progress is being made at reducing the deficit,
and a most unpromising asset (called “ Private ledger
accounts —debts in suspense pending realisation of
securities ”) which presumably includes most of the
deficit which wasn’t nominally provided for by the
writing off of deposits, is being steadily reduced. In
December, 1897, it stood at £742,835. It is now down to
£588,317, a reduction of £154,518, the amount cleared off
each half-year being :
June, 1898 .. £ll,BlO June, 1900 .. £17,925
December, 1898 20,013 December, 1900 21,166
June, 1899 .. 16,000 June, 1901 .. 23,036
December, 1899 14,987 December, 1901 29,581
Since December, 1897, also, the amount of the recon-
structed deposits, as shown in the balance-sheet, have
been reduced by a trifle—about £171,000 out of about
£4,950,000. The reserve of cash and liquid securities
(£1,780,350) is very solid indeed in proportion to liabilities.
But the new deposits are not increasing—they remain
stationary at a little over £1,000,000. There is an uneasy
feeling, too, that very bad times are coming in debt-laden
Queensland, and even with moderately good times the
Q.N. Bank has a very long way to go before it gets on
to solid ground again.

pay 4 per cent, and make better progress at the rebuild-
ing of the reserve funds. And the co. still reckons its
£14,000 vvorth (nominal) of Australian Deposit and Mort-
gage Bank deposits at £14,000. though their present
market value is only about £12,000. Taking everything
into account, however, the outlook is much better than
it was between ’95 and ’9B, and something has been done
in the way of maV.ng good past losses.

Sydney Harbor Collieries Co. (not having found
the main seam) is, it is announced, going to work that
little 2ft. 6in. seam. But it wasn’t to work 2ft. 6in. of
coal that the co. sank its magnificent 3000 ft. shaft. Yet
when this paper suggested at the beginning that the
right thing to do was to test the ground by bore instead
of by shaft, the co. was very angry indeed.
* * * *
Great Cobar Copper Mine, N.S.W.: The
determination of the tributor-purchasers to submit the
question of the rescission, or otherwise, of the purchase
to a vote of the shareholders of the co., independently of
their own votes (which amount to three-fourths of the
whole), must have come as a lyddite shock to the host of
lawyers concerned in the case shortly expected to come
before the Equity Court. Nine years ago the famous
Great Cobar was a mining derelict, having been closed
down for nearly four years, and shares were unsaleable
at a shilling. On the failure of some London negotiations
for a tribute of the mine, a party of men, two of them
coal-miners, and the other a country medical practitioner,
attempted to solve the problem of producing copper at a
profit when the price of the metal was even lower than
it is to-day. Fortune favored them in two ways—by
their discovery that certain gold and silver values hitherto
unknown were in the ore, and by an exceptional rise in
copper value. In course of time the public went bald-
headed for the shares till they reached 85s. Neverthe-
less a section of shareholders made a decided stand
against the tributors, who, it must be admitted, were
the sole means of so enormously increasing the value of
the-property. Some 12 months ago the tributors pur-
chased the mine for £500,000, less an allowance for the
remainder of their tribute, which would have given a
cash distribution of about 90s. a share. Just as the
matter approached completion a gentleman claiming to
be a representative of powerful English capitalists, told
some of the dissatisfied section that he could easily
get something like a million and a quarter for the
property, and they started law proceedings. Some
arrangement was made to satisfy everybody in the event
of the representative of millions of waste money in
London getting over a million for the mine, but he failed
to make a deal in the Big Smoke. With the delays of
litigation has come the present copper slump, and the
dissatisfied shareholders have now the opportunity of
saying whether they will accept the distribution of half-
a-million, or keep their shares, and remain without
dividends until copper takes a substantial rise. The
conditions of copper-production at Cobar are entirely
different from what they were nine years ago. As in the
case of all other copper mines, the value of the
ore has decreased with the depth. It is not
too much to say that if the ore-bodies worked
out years ago existed in the mine to-day copper
could be produced at under £2O per ton, but the ore
has gone down so much in value that it is doubtful if it
can be made under £5O. Some of the furnaces have
already been shut down, and but for existing contracts it
is probable the whole mining and smelting plant would
close, and some 2000 men be thrown out of. work.
Should no agreement be arrived at, lawyers alone will find
steady employment at the Great Cobar.
• • * •
Sulphide Corporation’s report is better than it
looked through the cable. The year’s profit of £73,049
(the co.’s year closes with June) was made after allowing
£ll,OOO for depreciation, and £5OOO for interest on deben-
tures. The amount carried forward, £93,993 is big, the
directors admit; hut they wanted to be prepared for a
Sossible further fall in metal prices. They regard the
ontinental depression as mainly responsible for the lead
slump ; and as that depression can hardly go on
indefinitely they look for a recovery in lead before very
long. Curtailment of production through many mines
becoming unremunerative will also help to restore the
price, they argue; though as to that, metal statistics
show that a fall in price is often closely followed by in-
creased production, the result, no doubt, of the few big
producers straining to level up profits. Certainly both
the B.H. Prop, and the Sulphide Corporation have been
doing this. The most satisfactory item in the report is
the announcement that the zinc-concentrating plant—a
German patent—lately erected at the mine, is doing good
work, making a profit with spelter at even £l6. This
plant, which works only on hitherto-valueless middlings
and tailings, has been producing about 600 tons of zinc
concentrates a month ; but the capacity is now to be in-
creased to 1200 tons. Very shortly, also, a zinc-distilling
plant will be started at Cockle Creek.
• • • •
The steady fall in the lead market last year hit
the Barrier in two places—-it shrunk the quantity of
metal exported, and also the value of what did go out.
High ocean freights also practically wiped out the pro-
mising zinc industry ; from 15,204 tons zinc concentrates
in 1900 the exports dropped to 204 tons in 1901. The
district’s whole yield for 1901 was valued at £1,872,411
net, as against £2,642,700 in 1900, and £1,998,615 in 1899.
And the shrinkages were nearly all in the latter half of
the year.

Broken Hill mines’ timber bill last year
amounted to £llO,OO0 — only just 10 per cent, of what it
was a few years back, when the square-set system of
mine-timbering was wholly relied on.
* • • •
Lead, which got down to within a few pence of
£lO in the last days of ’Ol, improved back to £lO 10s. be-
fore the middle of January. The share market responded
eagerly to the rise; though, seeing that the fall from
which this rise was a recovery was probably due to the
Xmas stagnation, there was not muoh to justify the idea
that the oorner had been turned at £lO, and that things
were permanently on the mend again. Most of the mar-
ket transactions appear to have been in the interests of
operators who had bought at big prices and wanted to
“ average,” and who jumped at the first upward turn as
indicating the touching of the bottom. There certainly
has been, during the last week or two, a much more hope-
ful feeling at Broken Hill itself—though Broken Hill’s in-
formation regarding the future of the metal market is
quite likely no better than Wooloomooloo’s.
* * * *
Commercially, Adelaide is deadly dull. The
depression at the Barrier hits it severely ; and, added to
that, the S.A. wheat yield is in the aggregate dis-
appointing. Very large quantities of hay have been cut,
fanners fearing to leave their crops for wheat in so un-
certain a season ; and hay is very cheap, the expected
demand from South Africa having hung fire. The
lately-busy machinery-making centre of Gawler is duller
even than Adelaide. The brightest spot ahead of the
State seems to be in connection with the wine-grape
yield, which promises exceedingly well.
* * * •
The copper slump has, for the timebeing, knocked
out the Parramatta, the French venture on Vorke’s
Peninsula, S.A., which, it seems, has latejy depended on
calls. The re-treatment of the Burra slag has also been
stopped, and the projected re-opening of the Burra mine
hung up. The wage-reduction on the big Moonta mines
has been effected without trouble, though scores of the
miners are now making for the West. The Hamley and
other mines have followed suit with a 10 per cent, cut—
and wages were humble enough even before the cut

London and Globe Finance. Cabled from Lon-
don that the “notorious” company-promoter, Whittaker
Wright (up to a few months ago he was the “ famous,”
etc.) had given evidence in the Bankruptcy Court. He
accused the engineers employed by him of treachery, they
having misled him as head of the L. and G. re the value
of the Lake View Consols mine. The war, according to
Wright, completed the collapse of the co. His examina-
tion extended over two days ; on the second he admitted
having allotted blocks of shares to press-representatives,
including those of the Financial News, Financial Times,
Troth, Daily Mail, Citizen, Australian Mail and the
Empire. The next man to get on the “ stand” should be
the mining engineer against whom Wright’s accusation
was probably chiefly directed. What a tale he could un-
fold of the time when he received instructions to make a
sudden departure from the mine to meet Wright at Port
Said, having carefully barricaded the rich part of the
mine before going, also under instructions. lie met
Wright, and returned to the mine under the impres-
sion that the meeting had taken place with the know-
ledge and consent of the other members of the Board,
whereas it was unknown to any of these latter, and the
engineer was not made aware of the fact till long after-
wards. Also on that memorable occasion Wright agreed to
“ carry ” him for 2000 shares bought at £lO each and sold
at over £2O. The next meeting of the tw r o was
in London where the engineer went for the purpose
of getting his money, which he only screwed out of
Wright at the point of the pistol. Wright has for years
been bribing the London financial press, the general
rottenness of which is unequalled on earth- or probably
in hell. And that same press speaks quite severely of
Continental journals, which are said to take bribes from
Leyds.
• * * •
Mr. Charles Kaufmann, in expressing surprise a,t
the wonderful progress made by Kalgoorlie field since
last visit, said that he regards the big mines on the belt
as the greatest in the world. Not so very wonderful
after all, for that’s where Kaufmann’s interests are. They
have lifted him from American obscurity to the world-
fame of a mining millionaire. Of all the boomsters
Westralia has had, the astute Kaufmann is the
only one left that can spread his tail. After
all, he has been saved by two successes, the
Lake View mine, which he secured for working capital at
a time when neither he nor the sellers of it had the
faintest conception of its real value ; and by the Ivanhoe,
which he got on the basis of half-a-million, £250,000 of
which was cash. He floated a good number of W.A.
“cats” on which an immense amount of money was
wasted before they were abandoned, in fact some are
eking out an existence to-day. Kaufmann is silent on
this subject. His was a clever move to get his then boss,
Wright, to put all the “wild cats” together and allow
them to slowly die under the name of the Standard
Exploration Co. ; a cleverer move still to separate himself
from Wright before the latter’s downfall. By these moves
Kaufmann must have made at least a million and saved his
reputation.
• * * •
Merton’s Reward (W.A.): The sale of this
mine is again announced. Nothing is explained as to
why the previous alleged sales did not come off. It has
been sold a dozen times over, according to the news-
papers, which have a playful habit of selling mines on
rumor and never contradicting the statements. Wires
relating to W.A. mines are evidently received by the
Eastern dailies from only two sources, one communicat-
ing with the Register in Adelaide, the Argus in Mel-
bourne, and the Herald in Sydney, the other with the
S.A. Advertiser, Melb. Age, and Sydney Daily Tele-
graph. In respect to this sale one set of newspapers gave
it out that'After a long period of quiescence British
capitalists are again showing faith in W. A. mines. A very
big deal has just been accomplished, resulting in Mer-
ton’s Reward mine changing hands at a large figure. The
capitalist is Charles Kaufmann, and the figure is £300,000,
of which it is said that a preliminary payment has already
been made. F. Morgan, formerly underground manager
of Lake View Consols, has been appointed manager, and
has gone to the property with Kauftnann to take posses-
sion. No company is to be formed for some modths, as it
is intended to first thoroughly open up the mine in a
systematic manner before placing it on the market for
flotation.” Quite an unusual circumstance in W.A., where
the custom has been to float first and open up or close up
afterwards. As this mine has been worked by Merton
himself with a 20-head battery for some years, it should
be fairly well opened up already. The other set of news-
papers tell a somewhat different story—that Kaufmann
has seeured an option on Merton’s Reward, and is leaving,
accompanied by Messrs. Hamilton and Morgan,' to
thoroughly examine the mine, and that, if he is
satisfied with his inspection, Kaufmann will com-
plete the purchase before the end of the week, and
he and Merton will then probably leave for England
within the next 10 or 12 days. There is something wrong
here, too. Kaufmann is not exactly the man to buy a
mine for £300,000 and complete the purchase within a
week. He is very rich and given to throwing money
away at poker at times, but he is not quite so bad
as this. If questioned on the point he would probably
reply, “ Don’t you see me vink 1 ” Merton has been
prepared to sell his mine for the last two years, but
always wanted more than anybody was prepared to
give. Up to date, he has had many magnificent
promises, but few genuine offers. That he has taken
many thousands of ounces of gold out of the mine
is also vvell knowrn, and had he offered it when it
was nothing more than a surface show, but when the
boom was raging, he would have probably got a better
price than to-day, when the mine is equipped with a
plant and has been worked in many places. There are no
buyers of mines when there is no boom in London, but
if there is one man left who can handle this mine in the
absence of any London boom it is Kaufmann, who is pro-
bably impressed with that idea himself. It would be in
keeping with Kaufmann’s marvellous run of luck if, after
he bought it, Merton’s Reward turned out the biggest
mine in Westralia.
* » • *
Lake View Consols : Wired of chairman Govertt
that, if he has the slightest pretensions to mining know-
ledge, he cannot fail to be impressed with the equipment
of the mine and the amount of development work going
on. Unfortunately, it wants it all. Several levels in this
mine have been proved of little or no value. Much
diamond-drill boring has been done, but nothing much
has been found. It was a great mine, but its greatness
has departed. It is not alone in that respect. The
Associated near it seems to be pretty well worked out.
The little 6-acre block owned by the Central and West
Boulder was a fair surface show, hut the lense of ore
petered out in depth. The Kalgurli, on the same line as
the Associated, seems to have a consistent pipe of ore,
having considerable width but no length, and if it gives
out in depth, all is up with the mine. At £6 10s. a share
the L.V. Consols is an anomaly, and when the bears have
covered or are squeezed out, scrip will fall to £4 or so,
and unless something extraordinary is found in the mine,
that figure will be much above their value.

Perseverance (W.A.): These shares have been
consistently rising for some months, and took an extra-
ordinary jump the other day from £lO cum div. to £l2 ex
div. —a rise equal to £2los. a share, or of £350,000. There is
no boom in the district or anything in the mine to war-
rant it. It is quite clear that it is a fight between Frank
Gardner and the bears, who put out thousands of these
shares at about half their present price, Frank
has been laying his lines for a bear-squeeze for
many months, and it looks as if he has suc-
ceeded. ’Tis said that in filling his own sale contracts
he made arrangements to pay heavy backwardation rather
than let the scrip get out. The rumor went through the
Australian market a few days ago that no scrip was avail-
able anywhere, that it now remains with Gardner to
put the shares to any figure he likes, and that it is not
unlikely they will be £l5 within the next week or two,
and may reach £2O ere he finishes the game. It is well
known that on the merits of the mine, which is rotten in
the bottom, they are not worth £5 a share—a price they
are bound to see after the bear squeeze is over.

Chillagoe : These shares more than doubled in
value the ether day. Tijey jumped from lOd. to Is. lOd.
and then stopped, just when the newspapers were saying
they were likely to go much higher. It has “ leaked
opt ” from information received from London that capi-
talists there were prepared to take up all the shares that
remained unallotted under the reconstruction scheme.
What magnanimity ! But isn’t it possible that this is a
move on the part of the debenture-holders? It is nob
likely that at the present moment they want to take
over the concern for the debenture debt. They prefer
to collect 6 per cent, interest instead, and if they are
forced to take it over a-t all they would rather wait until
another £100,009 or more has been spent on the mines
under the reconstruction scheme. Possibly they will not
object to another reconstruction scheme after this. It
will need more than one to make the Chillagoe mines pay
with copper under £5O a ton.

Talisman Consolidated, M.L. : “Moses Moss,”
one of the men whom Whittaker Wright swore he had
bribed with blocks of shares, devotes an article in London
Truth to this mine. The few weeks it has taken his
paper to arrive in Australia are quite sufficient time to
prove Moses wrong, as usual. He said of the mine
that it would yield at least £3 per ton after all the initial
difficulties had been overcome, and that this would give
a net profit of nearly £lOO,OOO a year. Some 4000 tons a
month, he said, were being treated, and it would be easy
to treat 5000 tons a month. “ Here, then, is a mine
which is at presenteaming 15 percent., and will probably
earn 30 per cent, in the near future, the shares m which
have been knocked down by those who do not
understand the figures and possibilities to £1 ss.
This is an absurd price. Anyone buying _ Talis-
mans now has the prospect of very large dividends.
Those who have bought at higher prices should average
at once.” That is the Moses side of the picture; here is the
true one: The first crushing averaged 265. 6d., and, as
usual, it was put down to the mullock that went through
first (somehow, mullock always seems to do that). The
next crushing was 275. 6d. per ton, and the last 21s. The
quantity crushed averages only 2500 tons a month. In-
stead of going up from 255., as tipped by “ M.M.," they
have gone back to 15s. If “ M.M.” acted on his own
advice, he would be ruined many times a year. Of
course, if he gets his shares as Whittaker Wright swears
he does, he can afford to sell them cheaply. One could
hardly expect anyone with such a name as “ Moses Moss”
to be a mining philanthropist, but it is remarkable that,
in the face of Moses’ many—er —mistakes, that he should
be allowed to keep going.
* * * *
A farmer in the fire-swept Young district had
his standing crop of 1500 acres of wheat insured. After
he had it stripped and bagged, a fire burnt the lot, and
the insurance company refused to pay on the plea that
the policy was effected on the standing, not the harvested
crop—a legally correct position. Moral is obvious —
Insure from paddock to market 1
» * • •
The Gold Coast (W. Africa) : The Governor of
that colony reports that the output of gold from the
settlement has been decreasing for some time, that of the
hundreds of concessions registered during the year a
large proportion are not worth the paper they are written
on, and that many of the alleged mines had been salted.
An up-to-date nigger was caught in the act of salting, and
his method struck the Governor as remarkably clever.
He had a blow-pipe, and, just as the native jewellers blow
little beads of gold in their ornamental be;ul-work, this
little nigger blew little beads of gold into little crevices
of barren quartz. He then produced samples, and was
prepared to show where they came from for so mush spot
cash. This method of salting may be quite new. to the
Gold Coast Governor, but is as old as the hills in Aus-
tralia ; and the writer has no doubt that even on the
West Coast it is not necessary to employ a nigger to salt
a mine. He may do a little on his own account to work
off a concession—but after all, the prospectus is salted a
great deal oftener than the mine.

BRANDY
Manly, the Sydney suburb of never return,
was treated to a specially grim exhibition last
week. A young lady bather, caught in the under
current, screamed for help, and a young man
immediately plunged in to save her. He, too, was
caught in the under tow, and a rush was made for
the life-line, and then the thing that always
happens took place. The rope was defective.
Some said it was rotten and broke, others said it
was not tied properly at the sections and cameun-
j-lc‘d anyhow, it parted. The young woman and
her would-be rescuer were drowned, and those
who tried to rescue them were recovered too
exhausted to stand. Now the dailies are teeming
with .suggestions how to prevent such happenings
m future. Meanwhile the first question is to find
out who was responsible for that life-line, and do
something to him. All the inspection and sub-
mspection in the world to very little so
Jong as it is an understood thing that neglect
brings no penalty upon either the inspector or the
inspected.
The Manly tragedy, happening towards the
end of a week, was a great boon to the ferry
company which carries the public d'own to the
waterside village. Saturday and Sunday, whilst
the police were dragging for one of the missing
bodies, thousands lined Manly beach, eager to get
a glimpse of a swollen, discolored corpse, and
speculating as to whether it would be much eaten
by the fish or not. The loathsomely morbid
wretches—men, women and children—in some
cases camped about the place all day, and only
dragged themselves away when the police ceased
work. Public decency would be served—even if
no reform of individuals was secured—by the
wholesale arrest of such ghouls, on a charge of
obstructing the beach, or being found in a public
place for an unwholesome and diseased purpose.
A Sydney paper is raising a subscription to
erect a memorial to the man who lost his life in
trying to rescue the woman victim of Manly surf-
bathing. The man was married and had a family.
Perhaps his widow and children would be the
better of something more practical than a stone
over their dead bread-winue»*

"Adventurous voyagers from the north
bank of the Yarra at Spottiswoode (Vic.), to the
southbankat Port Melb., have had
their minds set at rest on one point.
The Crown Solicitor has issued an
opinion covering several sheets of
foolscap, that the punt is not a
steamship within the meaning of the
Act, aqd that it is not
necessary that the man who
pulls the lever should hold
a marine engineer’s certifi-
cate. A steamship, ac-
cording to the definition
of the Act, is any ship
propelled by steam; a
ship is interpreted to
mean any vessel used
in navigation and not
propelled by oars. As
the punt in question is
noi propelled by oars,
there is still a doubt
if it isn’t legally a
three-masted ship on
the high seas, and re-
quires, accordingly,
an anchor, a bos’n
and a midshipmite,
but, doubtless, the
law will settle this
point also if the
foolscap holds out.
There are very few
points the law can’t
settle if the foolscap
holds out.
A much-aggrieved
man hits out in the Ayr
Chronicle (N.Q. ) in this
fashion: “I, the under-
signed, wish to inform the public that the person
who claims to be my wife has neither right nor
title to any such claim.” Signed, Thos. P.

Sydney’s new railway station involves the
destruction of one historic church, the Pitt-street
which was opened by Dr. Fullerton
m 1838. Ihe last service in the church was held
this month, and those who put nothing else in the
plate put in a tear. As the congregation will get
fron? £40,000 to £60,000 for the site, its sorrow at
the loss of the old tabernacle has a solid cash
alleviation.

The Australian girl, in her early years,
before she can fix up a yarn about her honor
(Grippsland Times quote)•
Two little girls went to Sunday school, and heard an
address about the infant child Jesus, and how He was
eventually crucified. Next day they got the two-year-old
brother and called him the infant Jesus. One of the girls
came into the house and got a hammer and nails, and the
mother, wondering what the youngsters were up to, went
out to investigate, and found the boy stretched out on
the floor and his sisters in the act of nailing him down.
They intended crucifying him.
A Queenslander, well-known in the
sugar business, telegraphed lately to a
shipping agency to secure a berth in a
boat bound for a southern port. The
telegram, after the manner of its kind
made it appear that Mrs. A. wanted the
berth, m place of Mr. A., who, all-un-
suspecting, went aboard just before the
ship sailed, made a bee-line for the cabin
marked on his ticket, and fearing mal de
mer quickly- put himself to bye-bye in the
top berth. Some hours later he was
awakened by a sound that froze the very
marrow in his bones—an unmistakably
female cough in the bunk below. It was
followed by the lament of a lady who
sam she was sure she was going to die.
Ihe cabin was pitch dark, and there was
nothing to be done save to lie still and
pray for rescue. Sleep was impossible,
especially as day dawned and showed
garments trimmed with “invisible lace”
and nic-nacs all about the cabin. At
last came the stewardess, in answer to
the feeble wail from the berth below,
lhe male in the upper premises made
mute and wild gesticulations for this
angel of mercy to come near while he
whispered in her ear, but she only gave a
little shriek and vanished, returning in a
few minutes followed by the husband of
the occupant of the lower berth. He
grasped the situation, helped the Queens-
lander to escape, disguised in the bed-
clothes, and it was explained to the occu-
pant of the lower bunk that the lady up
above had been removed owing to severe
illness—alcoholism, complicated by in-
growing toenail, or something of the sort.

The Chinese gambling den flourishes
apa,ce m North Queensland, and the Cau-
casian youth of Townsville, Cairns, and
Charters Towers are corrupted accordingly. The
other day, a Townsville newspaper printed
letter from a well-known citizen, wherein
the latter told how he visited certain local
fan-tan places and noticed policemen among
the habitues. One member of the “ foorce ”
took a hand in the gamble, and explained to the
citizen aforesaid that, inasmuch as the authorities
disregarded the reports (re Chinese dens) sent in
by the police, the latter considered that the
gambling dens had received official sanction, and
were to be regarded as legalised institutions. The
.North is, indeed, a festive land.
“I 1 .” ; Some of the members of the Vie.
society for the prevention of cruelty
to superannuated painters recently
elected for life six “Policemen
Art, 5 called academicians.
1 hey have the power to elect or
reject associates and to add to
their own number up to 12.
They still remain six—Pater-
son, Mather, M'Cubbin, Lou-
reiro, Withers and Richard-
son. According to the regu-
lations it takes four acade-
micians to elect an associate,
so when one of the guardians
is away, two men, by" working
in union, could block any future
rival. It is always foolish to
erect artificial barriers of this
sort, for other, and perhaps
better, painters may come along
to seek admission in vain. This
would harm the society rather
than the particular painter, for
no true artist can benefit by
carrying round his neck such a
label as “Academician, Associate,or
Honorable Mention.” His own merit must be
his only passport to success.
N.S.W. military officers are just now
agitated about a “Cape cart.” A gallant officer
in South Africa informed his parents that he was
forwarding a Cape cart as a Christmas present for
his father, a brother warrior kindly undertaking
delivery. On arrival in the State this latter pro-
ceeded to cut a dash with the girls, driving them
round in his Cape cart. A demand for instant
delivery by the consignees only annoyed him, and
he drove the girls round more than ever. Pressure
from headquarters was brought to bear on the
officer, who then stated that it was really his own
vehicle, that he had lent it to the thoughtful
son at the front, and that young man had no right
to promise it to his father or anyone else. A
violent controversy on the subject was suddenly
ended by the Imperial people, who got to hear of
the row and impounded the Cape cart on the
general principle that if it wasn’t theirs it ought
to be.

is bad for the throat? ”
LITTLE BILLY : “ I reckon it is. I'm simply hoarse

Both the Adelaide dailies (which do noi
often get anything like a sensational murder)
boomed the Towitta case in the most aggressive
way, keeping special relays of cyclists and horse-
men on the bad and frightfully hilly track between
the scene and the nearest telegraph-office. A
motor-car was tried, but broke down at once. By
flaring head-lines and posters, and announcements
of the preparations made for getting news away,
public interest (not specially keen to begin with)
was at last really worked up.
THE SONG OF THE SKIRT.
[F OR THE BULLETIN. ]
With Angers daintily gloved,
A woman, serene and fair,
Is clutching the train of her white delaine
As she haughtily takes the air—
Hitch ! hitch ! hitch !
She feigns to avoid the dirt,
But the track of her feet on the germ-strewn street
Is swept by a trailing skirt.
Drag ! drag 1 drag !
Wnilst the dust-Aend Airts around.
Drag ! drag ! drag !
When the mud befouls the ground.
'Twire better she were a slave
Of a rational-costumed Turk,
To drowsily dream in a, clean hareem,
If this be Christian work.
Drag ! drag ! drag !
Through a mile of powdered grime.
Drag ! drag ! drag !
Through slushes of evil slime.
Tram-car, street, and shop,
Shop-floor, asphalt, and tram,
The long skirt sweeps where the microbe creeps—
In the wake of la belle madame.
0 ! women wi£h kindred dear !
0 ! mothers, daughters and wives !
It is not gowns you are,knocking out,
But human creatures’ lives.
Hitch ! hitch I hitch I
While scientists shrill assert
That the elegant tail you partially trail
Is a shroud as well as a skirt.
Drag ! drag ! drag !
As fisher-folk drag the seas,
You trail your dress for the typhoid mess
And the relics of lung disease.
Hitch ! hitch ! hitch 1
At furbelow, frill, or tuck ;
Your figure looks sweet as you cross the street,
Though your title is Lady Muck.
With fingers daintily gloved
A woman serene and fair
Is clutching the train of her white delaine
As she walks with a queenly air.
Drag ! drag ! drag !
’Tis little she heeds the dirt;
But still in a tone of regret sincere,
Would that the woman inclined her ear !
We sing this song of the skirt.
LOVE ME TO-DAY,
[F OR THE BULLETIN. ]
Love me to-day—for this ambrosial space
Take thou my hands and smile into my face ;
Give me those kisses thou hast held in store
So many empty days ! Nay, I implore,
Be prodigal—a spendthrift of thy grace.
Oh ! but to-morrow is a distant place,
And yesterday a barren land of yore ;
To-day I live who knew not life before—
Love me to-day 1
MAGGIE SULLIVAN.

IN DOUBT.
MR. SAWKINS (uncertain of his address, rings up his housekeeper): “Ish this number twenty-
shia?” HOUSEKEEPER : " Of course it is, sir! ”
MR. S.: “ Well, come out and look at me and see if I’m the man that lives here.”
The rumor has been revived of a Protec-
tionist morning paper in Sydney—this time, on
the authority of “a gentleman high up in the
political world.” But the fact that the cable
syndicate agreement between the Australian
papers has just been renewed for two years
severely discounts the probability of a new morn-
ing paper being published. The various Austral-
asian papers—from the Age, Argus, Herald, and
Telegraph, right down to the smallest Maoriland
sheet—“ pool” the London cable news and pay a
share of its cost, proportioned to their importance.
So, if £30,000 a-year is spent, the share of a
Sydney morning daily would be only, say, £3OOO.
A new morning paper would not be admitted to
the syndicate, and (allowing a 50 per cent, dis-
count for the stupidity of the present cable ser-
vice) it. would cost it £15,000 to give as good a
column of European news as that now provided.
Difference between £3OOO and £15,000 a-year
would cripple any new paper. .
« * * v' ’ *
A Melbourne man writes about a new re-
ligion—so new that the paint isn’t dry on it yet:
On Sunday night I went to Parson Dowie’s up-to-date
propaganda in this city. As there’s no polygamy about
it the enthusiasm is not vast. Small attendance, in the
Victoria Hall, which architect, the Hon. Billy Pitt, has
just decorated in a charming manner. Six deyotees on
stage, half of them ladies. Two Dowie-ites have come
as evangelists, called Overseers. The one I heard is a
dull fellow, but the other is said to be a trifle superior to
General Booth. But there is no permanent hope for
Dowieism, or Christian Science, or Mormonism, except
as vulgar temporary money-making devices. They don’t
come from Asia. Asia is the only place where they build
successful religions Christianity, Mohammedanism,
Buddhism, Brahminism ; all the world’s religions are
Asiatic. Creeds are Asia’s chief exports. .The man who
tries to start a completely new faith —one that begins
with a European or American revelation or prophet—has
a hopeless contract on hand.
* » * •
Maoriland is the place of romantic suicides.
Also, the romantic suicide is generally absurdly
foolish. Some time ago a young couple, who had
got married and spent more money than they
bargained for on the honeymoon, passed through
the veil because they had less than they, had
hoped for with which to buy the furniture.
Now comes the case of a young Maori bride whose
husband went off to a temporary job, and got two
jobs where he had only expected one. Meanwhile
his pretty wahine, for whom he was making cash,
came to the conclusion that he couldn’t stay away
from her for three whole days unless there was
some other cat to keep him, so she shot herself in
the chest and died. Romance is a deadly thing !
“Melb.”: Salvation Army had an extra-
ordinary shivoo in Bevan’s church, January 6.
One hundred ladies and gentlemen were com-
missioned as officers, to start forth at once. They
come from all parts of Australasia, and go to all
parts. Six months’ training in the new college,
next to the Fire Brigade, has furnished them with
all necessary (qualifications. One hundred more
collegians go in at once, and will also be turned
out, like pickled salmon or canned lobster, strictly
to order, next July. All this is very fine, but
it is like pouring water into a cullender. Your
theatre-ticket is taken by an ex-Salvation Army
officer. He takes your dust-bin, humps your
portmanteau from the wharf, cries “Ser-veep!”
(grimly suggestive of his old friend Lucifer, son of
the morning), and you find him driving an omni-
bus or taking tram fares. It is even alleged that
an ex-major and an ex-lady captain run wine
shop? There is no trade so bad that it won’t pay
the sweated Salvationist to take to it, rather than
go on salvationing.
The latest woman-attempted-assault-tale
comes from" Victoria. The victim is a 53-year-old
maiden lady; she says that for four hours she
successfully resisted her assailant, who, “seeing
•that day was breaking,” relinquished his attempt.
This Homeric conflict took place in the garden.

The latest development in connection with
the sensational Ariadne case, in which Caradoc
Kerry, Eric Freke and George Mumford have to
stand trial for casting a vessel away on the Maori-
land coast, is the re-arrest of Mumford forendeavor-
ing to suborn a witness, Annie Downing, a Sydney
barmaid, who gave most damagingevidence at the
lower court inquiry. She allegedly heard Kerry
and Mumford make the compact in her bar for the
destruction of the yacht. Mumford, who appears
to be charged with a fatal disposition to open his
mouth too wide, met and dined with this hostile
witness, and, according to her account, offered
her something handsome if she contradicted her
previous story. The pair next met by appoint-
ment in a hotel, two ’tecs, being hidden behind
the curtains. They were discovered by Mumford,
however, before the conversation had proceeded
very far, and the eavesdroppers then arrested him
for offering refreshment to a witness for wrongful
purposes, or some such charge.
* * * *
Melbourne papers are wailing again with
increased bitterness over the failure to get the
Fever Hospital into working order. This was
one of the institutions intended as a monument to
the memory and greater glory of the late Queen.
It still stands, a brick shell with the vagrant
winds blowing through its empty wards, an elo-
quent memorial which should bear this inscrip-
tion :— ■
Sacred to the Memory of the alleged Love and Devotion
of a Great Population which imagined itself distracted
by the Death of its Queen, and wasn’t.
Both morning dailies urge the State Premier to
remove the shame from Melbourne’s. reputation
by supplying money to finish the hospital. Mean-
while, the spirit of the deceased monarch is sup-
posed to be staring reproachfully out of the un-
glazed windows at a callous people.

Victorian Board of Health has been pro-
viding some interesting “ copy.” on the
subject of trailing skirts. At its last
meeting Councillor Wood wanted
the Board to instruct its secretary
to write to the Countess of Hope-
toun, Lady Syd. Clarke, the
ladies of the Austral Salon, etc.
urging them to set the example
of wearing short skirts. Other
members refused to take the
proposal seriously. Probably
they all know, how useless
and ridiculous it is to reason
with a woman about herdress.
That old warrior, Coun-
cillor Shoppee, of Ballarat,
put the matter in a very
few words : “Lord Hope-
toun has already expressed
himself publicly in favor of
shorter skirts, and, if his
influence is not sufficient to
get Her Excellency to lead
the fashion in short skirts,
I’d like to know what
chance the Board of
Health has ? ” Councillor
Wood, seeing that the
Board was against him,
remarked that when
women went through the
streets holding up their
trailing skirts “ they
looked half-way be-
tween stuffed, bags and
prop-sticks with clothes
on.” Therein Wood struck
a far brighter idea than his
original one. If he can persuade an influential
section of the male community to shudder ob-
viously when the long-skirted woman, goes by—to
shudder at her dirty skirt-tail when it is down
and at her bunched and bundled shape when
the tail is up—a reform will begin to set in.
A Queensland ruffian was
recently fined for tying up his 10-
year-old son in a chaff-bag, and
keeping him there for a week
at a time—only letting him out
for food. He also admitted
bruising the child with kicks, and
with blows of a heavy stick. The fine imposed
amounted to the magnificent sum of 10s. Pre-
sumably this is another outbreak of the J.P.
system. The great J.P. idea, whereby a store-
keeper deals out justice to his best customer, is a
blot on Australia.
* * * *
The appearance of a shoal of nautilus in
Hobson’s Bay recently started a new and profit-
able industry. The fish were the paper nautilus,
and the shells were in great demand at ss. apiece,
consequently every unemployed rushed the sands
from Port Melbourne to Black Rock, hunting the
shellfish. Hundreds were secured and found ready
buyers in the city. An ex-fisherman earned £l2
in two days. A demand for nautilus shells for
Earlor ornaments at once set in amongst suburban
ousewives, and was met by enterprising young
men who hawked shells from door to door, and
did well at the game, till it was discovered that
their wares were papier-mache imitations “made
in Germany.”
Premier Barton, of Australia, and Premier
Seddon, of Maoriland, are invited to the corona-
tion of Edward VII. as the guests of the British
nation, with all expenses paid. The Australian State
Premiers are invited to attend and pay their own
ex’s. As the cable puts it ; “ Their expenses will
not be paid, but their visit will be made agree-
able.” London will be a dreadfully expensive
place at coronation time, and already Peacock and
Philp say they aren’t going, and See and Jenkins
are doubtful. If the British Government knew any-
thing about the price of victuals, it would select
Peacock, of Victoria, and Lewis, of Tasmania, as
the two men to be put on the free list, and make
all others pay. A great number of large, healthy
men with sound appetites will be living at
Britain’s expense next June, while pale, dyspeptio
statesmen who could be boarded economically are
paying their own bills.

latest Australian industry—“floating a.
man.” The man must be able to do something,
however indifferently—paint, draw, write, sing,
whistle, or tell stories. The floater, who must
know people and be able to pull political strings,
takes nim in hand, and, for a commission, places
his work, secures for him orders, and so on. Since
there are few rich fools in Australia, it is the State
cow that is generally milked by the “ floater.”

FORETHOUGHT.
THE ANCIENT (who wedlocked a young bride in
his sixties, and is now getting his hand in):
“Ah, I can see youngsters are a bit of a
trouble in infancy, but I’ll bring mine up in
the way they should go that they may be a
comfort to me in my old age. ."
Pascoe’s fresh book on Adelaide gives the
history of the Register and ’Tiser. One cannot
exactly make out which is the True-Blue Liberal,
and Caesar and Pompey are not more alike than
these two. Indeed, it is a fault of the rival dailies
in all Australian cities to be too much of a tran-
script of each other. If two boys go looking for a
lost coin, they will keep together, each afraid lest
the other should find it. It is often the same with
two papers looking for the public’s penny.
TE DEUM LAUDAMUS.
“Deaths in the concentration camps for
September and October, nearly GOOD —say 3000 a
month—or, roughly speaking, a hundred daily! ”
’Mic| the sound of many curses,
Comes a tale of tender mercies
From the blood-red veldt and valley where they chase
the stubborn Boer:
For, though burgher bows to no man,
Yet the dear ones of our foeman
Lo ! They die, a hundred daily, in the blood-red land of
War!
So the godly British people
Bing the bells in spire and steeple,
Lift the strain of wild rejoicing as their fathers did of
yore;
And the sound of song and psalter
Tells in joy at God’s high altar :
“Lo ! They die—a hundred daily—in the blood-red land
of War ! ”
And they chant, both young and hoary,
“ Unto Thee, 0 Lord, the glory
Of our foemen, bent and broken by a means untried
before ;
Of their women fair who perish,
Of the children whom they cherish,
And who die —a hundred daily—in the blood-red land of
War! ”

A WOMANS LETTER.
Sydney, January 21, 1902.
My Dear Arini.—
Several marriages are coming off presently.
Mr. Lindemann, of wine fame, and Miss Dight,
of Albury, are going to do the wedding march!
So are Mabel, a daughter of Mr. George Watson,
and Mr. Stewart Osborne, of Moss Vale. But if
certain persons are going into matrimony others
are trying to get out. There are alarms and ex-
cursions in a certain very high-class, water-front
suburb, and among military feminine sets, over a
divorce case alleged to be pending.
Miss Florence Greaves, of “St. Helen’s,” Bur-
wood, a pretty blonde artist-girl who has exhi-
bited some good work at the Soc. of Artists’
shows, is engaged to be married to Mr. W. Motf-
lin, of “ Tokann,” Strathfield.
Miss Lillian Digges, the childish Little Alice of
Wonderland,” doesn’t look more than 12 in her
pink frock and pinafore, but she is 20, and has
been married a few years. Her husband is Lieu-
tenant Deacon and is on one of the fleet vessels
stationed at Gibraltar. Captain Thompson, of the
commissariat department at Garden Island, is her
brother-in-law, he having married, a sister of
Lieut. Deacon.
A new claimant has arisen as the discoverer of
Amy Castles—or, truly speaking, an English
paper has invented a discoverer. The paper
prints a portrait of Amy, looking dreamy-eyed
out of a cloud of hair, as “ the little Irish-Austra-
lian who was discovered by Lady Brassey.”
Last Sunday an important decree was read
from B.C. altars regarding mixed marriages. The
v 4iurch breaks out at intervals very heavily
against the practice of R.C.’s marrying into fancy
religions. It does a regular southerly buster of
censure against the practice, and then lies, or
rather blows, low again. But it never ethically
padlocks the gate between Catholics and noii-
Catholics and then throws away the key.
Ihe gate is kept on the latch, so to
speak, and, however reluctantly, it is opened
occasionally. Same with divorce. That is
allowed or disallowed in a rather movable-
least manner. Also, the re-marriage of divorced
persons. In Melbourne, a short time back, a
well-known man divorced his wife (she didn’t
want him, and had been out of the State for
years). Then he sought to marry, in her own
church, a devout R.C. girl. The difference of
creed could be arranged for, but not so his divorce.
Church wouldn’t marry them, so they took the
alternative of civil marriage. About the same
time a medical man in the same city was divorced
jy his wife (to his great joy), and he immediately
married an R.C. damsel, in her own church, with
considerable glitter and “The Voice that breathed
oer Eden,” etc.
I have been trying to imagine wnat difference a
variety of creed would have made to a love such as
that of Professor Goodwillie and his Lucy, in
Barrie’s play, “The Professor's Love-Story.” A
sweeter play has never been put on the Sydney
stage, using the saccharine word in its wide sense
of healthy human charm. There isn’t much out-
right laughter in the Prof.’s 1.-s., save when the
antithesis of his love-making—that of Effie Proctor
and the two Bobbie Burns' laborers—takes place.
Smiles are there all the time, and tears not so
very far off as you might think. It is
an idyll, but people who want their
plays to jump about will long for “What
Happened to Jones.” Mr. Arnold, as the young
old professor with the heart of a child, is great
and simple. Miss Dot Frederics (she must take
ones in shoes) as the secretary is an attractive
piece of fluff, just the kind that gets into the cog-
wheels of a brainy intellect like the Prof’s. A little
widow is a dangerous thing. There’s one in this
story, and Miss Bensusan embodies her so beauti-
fully, so raspingly, that you think how glad the
dear departed must have been to get disembodied.
Miss Hope Mayne, who always reminds me of the
young person in Scripture who mashed a king and
demanded the head of John the Baptist on a
charger, takes the role of wife to a big, good-look-
lng, silly M.P., and doesn’t run him through the
solar plexus with a hat-pin when he pulls her hat
P le r < LL t ? s , ee how ifc ’ s trimmed. Mr. Sharp as
Pete, Effie s lover, had evidentlv rolled himself in
a yellow clay duck-pond, and then- dried himself
in the sun. But he and the other spade-bearded
lover make the heartiest laughter of the piece.
Miss Agnes Knights, who strolls with fine cyni-
hrough her part as the Professor’s sister in
the Love Story,” wears a very thoroughbred-
looking frock of black and white checked silk.
On one shoulder is a black chiffon rosette from
which fall two long ends of chiffon held in bondage
by her belt. This fashionlet of the chiffon inside
the belt is “ coming in,” so my modiste tells me.
There was no Cinderella dressing in W.A
during the Gov.-General’s visit. Published
accounts of the garden party at Govt House read
like a description of the gowning at a Melb Cup
meeting. The State Governor, Lady Lawley, and
Hopetoun received under the shade of a beautiful
magnolia tree in full flower, which sounds cowl
sensuous scenty, and appropriate. It is to be
hoped that the G.-G. was not bagpiped on the
occasion. Bagpipes would breakeven the spell
cast by a blossoming magnolia. Lady Lawley’s
gown was of white net, with full pleated flounces
on the skirt, edged with narrow black lace. Lady
borrest was in white silk, over a foundation of
mauve silk, with rare lace on the bodice. Mrs.
Yelverton O’Conor, in a gown of white and corn-
flower-blue silk, fitted well into the midsummer
landscape. The G.-G’s “holiday” programme
makes dizzy reading. After the water supply
scheme had been explained to him, lie frisked
through inspections of public buildings, museums.
an old chapel, and aboriginal weapons, listened
to addresses and bagpipes, attended concerts and
conversaziones, fled hither and thither by rail
and otherwise to mining centres, cavorted amongst
machinery, drove spikes for new railways, de-
scended into mines, lifted his hat to vulgar little
sandgropers who saluted him as “Hopev,” and
in replying to addresses spoke of the “ holiday ”
he was having. It suggests Atlas carrying the
sun round on his shoulders for a rest after being
relieved of the world.
Probably the most superb pearl the world has
ever seen.” This large statement is made re a
pearl just fished up at Broome, in the Nor’-West.
Its lucky owner, one Buddivent, has been boat-
building and pearling for the last 15 years—until
now without any special success. The pearl is
oblong, of a perfect lustre, and its length is Din.
Pearlers put values of from £9OOO to .£15,000 on
it. A few days ago it was in Perth, en route to
.London, vvhere it may have a front setting among
the great jewels at the Coronation.

mother in Ben Hur,” is a Melbourne girl, but an
absentee m London for nine years. She is a tall,
nne-hgured girl, with the ravenest of hair, the
brunettest of complexions, and big eyes that don’t
quite fit her face in coloring, they being a slatey
grey. lhe countour of her face is very like
that of Maud M'Carthy. The beautiful evil genius
female m “ Ben Hur” will be Miss Mackay, an

of eyes Swinburne describes in “Felice.”
Miss Mackay was playing lead, the lady detec-
tive, in “lhe Wrong Mr. Wright,” before she
became a Ben Hurite.

Concerning servant-girls who work in town for
12 months, then spend their savings entertaining
their friends: A correspondent writes that she
legularly employs three sisters —excellent servants
they are—who work under assumed names. Every
1" months they take a five or six weeks’ holiday
and go back to their native town with new dresses
and jewellery, and announce that they have been
staying with noli friends in, say, Hobart or M.L.
I hey assume “airs” and entertain to the full
extent of their savings, then come back “broke”
only to repeat the “break” again after another
year s labor. The psychological significance at
tue back ot tins line of action is worth some
sealch. Vanity and improvidence apparently,
though the sense of the blessedness of giving over
that of receiving may be a side cause. The shearer
knocks down his cheque in drink, the working
girl knocks down hers quite as flatly, but less
coarsely A serving damsel came to our house-
hold awhile ago, destitute—not a tram-fare. Been
frolicking up-country. Good references, though
She drew her 12s. in advance. Did she buy
aprons and a suitable frock ? No ; she bought a
3s. diamond-ring, a bottle of scent, a pair of open-
work stockings, a bag of lollies, and went to the
py at night. “Greed for Gold” was the

In Minister O’Sullivan’s registry office scheme
will servants have to give their mistresses a char-
acter ? Of course, they always do give a verbal one
outside their employer’s house; but will a written
one have to be produced before the employer
may engage another Helma ? (“ What Happened
to Jones has given us a new name for a slavey.)
If so, then will be the time for someone to write
111 „ a tragedy, “What Happened to
Obullivan. In Chicago some mistresses are
trying the eight-hour system and proving it a
failure, and, of course, very expensive. The
morning shift of cooks and housemaids comes on
duty at 7 a.m. and leaves at 3 sharp ; but the
relays are not so sharp in taking up the running.
Often a long interval occurs before the second
cook-lady is on hand to get dinner, or the second
housemaid to bring up the afternoon tea.
The recent presentation of a purse of sovereigns
and a bangle to the wife of a high military official
has left a waspish feeling in woinen-o’-war circles.
Mrs. Merino Bigwool, who was put at the head of
the collecting movement, intimated in her note to
the wife of a colonel that, as the military set was
not a rich one, any small sum, even a shilling
might be forwarded, and none but the executive
would be any the wiser. A most sane and kindly
suggestion, I think, but the woinen-o’-war’s point
of view was different. They might be Mesdanies
Job m poverty, but it wasn’t for Mrs. Bigwool to
remind them of the fact. They retired, declining
to gi\e battle or a shilling'. Mrs. Merino merely
shrugged her fine shoulders, saying : “Then I’ll
make the collection among the brewers’ and store-
keepers’ wives. _ They don’t know Mrs. , but
that’s a detail. ’ And the brewers’ and store-
keepers wives came down handsomely.
Modiste is a good word, and it reminds me of a
storyette I’ve had in a letter from London. An
actress, playing in the “ Wrong Mr. Wright ” de-
signed a frock for herself—a real picture done in
browns (the fashionable color is all brown you
know)—cigar brown, cocoa brown, roebuck brown,
and sparrow brown. It was as much poem as
picture, and directly it was finished she destroyed
the design. She had already made the modiste
promise not to do a replica. Well, before the
Right Mrs. Wright, or whatever her role was, could
wear the picture frock she fell sick, and her part
was taken by an understudy. But she got
instantly better, and broke things, when she
learned that the understudy wore another brown-
study the “dead-image” of the original design.
The modiste was just as angry as her customer,
for the copyright of the frock had not been in-
fringed in her establishment. A girl from a rival
house was arrested for stealing the design and
patterns of the brown materials. Of course, it all
boiled down to a case of petty larceny, but the
stealer described herself on the charge-sheet as a
“ modiste,” although she did nothing but take out
basting threads ; and the magistrate asked if “ the
removal of the basting threads was —ah—an im-
portant point in—ah—costuming.” And the real
modiste could do nicely with the heads of that
girl and that magistrate on a charger.
\ ery few persons not made in Germany know
of the long catalogue of Royal German divorces
which have taken place within memory of the
present generation. Late Queen Victoria steadily
refused to receive ladies who had divorced their
lords, or who had been divorced, yet two of her
grand-daughters are now in that put-asunder con-
dition, and her own mother-in-law was a divorcee.
I Ins poor lady, in order to free herself from the
brutality of her husband, the Grand Duke of
oaxe-Coburg-Gotha, eloped with a young cavalry
lieutenant. When her lawful Bluebeard divorced
her she married the lieutenant, got him a title,
and lived happily in Paris. When she died she
bequeathed him a considerable income on condi-
tion that he would never part with her corpse,
bo the poor man carried the mummy of Queen Vic-
torians mother-in-law about, even long after his
marriage to an another woman. Then enemies
stole it, hoping to thus do away with his income.
But they didn’t succeed ; it was decided that as he
hadn t voluntarily lost, dropped, or thrown away
Ins late wife, he was still entitled to the salary.
