The Pat Man Divided Against Himself
A “Taxpayers’ Union” has been formed in
Sydney, a slab from its preliminary circular being
as follows:
Believing that the predominance of the Labor and
Socialistic party represents an accidental, and not the
true, position of parties in our Federal and other Parlia-
ments, we are of opinion that the time has come for a
concerted general and earnest political opposition to the
present unfortunate situation. Little hut socialistic
legislation has been passed in the last decade, and tax-
payers interests have been practically neglected, if not
openly derided, and further legislation on the same lines
is to be anticipated if parties are allowed to drift on in
the present unsatisfactory manner. From interviews
with leading business men in Sydney and elsewhere, we
believe that an organisation to oppose further socialistic
legislation, further increases in land and income taxes
now vvithin measurable distance, and to maintain the
necessity for private enterprise and non-interference by
Government—such as building Government docks at
Newcastle, &c.—would be widely supported throughout
the Commonwealth.
What a handy scapegoat the “ Labor and Social-
istic Party” is when a crowd of inept Tories has
blundered into a hole. The Labor party doesn’t
predominate numerically in the Federal Parlia-
ment; it is only a fraction. It might be a power-
less fraction but for the fact that the Sydney
business men and importers and wire-pullers, and
their newspapers, knowing that a Protectionist
majority was practically certain in the Federal
Parliament, still insisted on sending in Reid,
M ‘Mill,an, Conroy, B. Smith, S. Smith, and others
of that ilk, and thereby so reduced the Govern-
ment majority as to make the comparatively small
Labor vote an influence that is often liable to turn
the scale. If the Sydney business men and their
daily papers would fall in line with the rest of
Australia, and would spread themselves to send in
a solid body of Protectionists, then the Govern-
ment would be independent of the Labor Party,
and that body wouldn’t “predominate.” If the
same crowd had stood by Dibbs, in N.S.W. State
polities, in ’94, instead of looking solely after its
importing interests, and had sent Dibbs back
with a big majority that made him in-
dependent of the Labor Party, he would
probably have told the Laborists, in his
wonted gentle fashion, to go to perdition,
and there would have been no land or income tax.
There would probably have been no One Man One
Vote either, if the Sydney crowd had stood by
Dibbs when he was Premier, instead of forcing
him to depend on the Labor Party for his precari-
ous majority. There would almost certainly have
been no old age pensions if the Sydney crowd had
made Lyne independent of Labor support.
Almost everything Labor has gained in N.S.W.
politics and in Federal politics has been gained
because the importers didn’t know when to come
in out of the wet. And they don’t know when to
come in out of the wet now, which is a blessed
thing for Australia. A Fat Man divided against
himself shall not stand.

Here is a specially-excellent sample from the
recent speech of William M‘Millan in the Pro-
testant Hall, Sydney: -
I understand that probably 75 per cent, of the clothing
of the people of this State is now manufactured within
its borders, and, indeed, has been for some time, without
any duty whatever ; yet in spite of this fact they have
put 25 per cent, upon the clothing of the whole of the
people of Australia, and the worst of it is this—and it is
the evil of the whole system—that although there may
be no necessity for the duty, the imposition of it gives an
opportunity for increasing the prices.
Also, though the M‘Billion couldn’t be expected
to mention that, it gives an opportunity for in-
creasing the wages. About the Worst disgrace
that ever fell on Sydney was embodied in the re-
velations made before a recent committee of
inquiry about the hideous sweating of Sydney sew-
ing women—the slaves who had to work 14 hours
a day making pants at 4d. a pair, because they
had to produce their work as cheaply as the sew-
ing women of London, or else got no work at all.
But as soon as the much-abused tariff saw the
light of day the tailoresses banded together and
demanded front the commercial philanthropists
of York-street advances in their rates of pay
amounting to from 50 to 100 per cent. And
because the Federal tariff reduced the competition
of the London Sisterhood of the Shirt, the local
grinders of blood and bone had to submit, at least
to .some extent. The philanthropic M‘Billion,
demanding a return to the old sweating horrors
which he and his fellow-Freetraders never lifted a
finger to alleviate, and demanding it in the name
of the poor, beats Satan reproving sin, in one
innings on a fiery wicket.

The barbering profession seems to be in
the ascendant just now. One hair artist repre-
sents a metropolitan constituency in N.S.W.
Assembly, and another, from Bourke, has just got
a State billet at £lOOO a year.
Lieutenant-Governor Owen, of N.S.W., is
68 years of age, and will be 15 years on the Bench
m October next. He has been 42 years in N.S.W.
He quitted the Bar for a while, taking the office
of Principal Undersecretary to Premier Charles
Cowper.
Francis Kenna, editor of the Brisbane
Worker, proposes trying to knock out fossil
Smith for the Bowen (N.Q.) State electorate.
Smith has a fair hold of the sleepy Bowenites, but
any change from Smith (the six-foot man with the
six-inch voice) should be a relief.
Dr. Koettlitz, senior scientist of the
Discovery Antarctic expedition, was on the Wind-
ward with the Jackson-Harmsworth party when
Nansen was found “up North.” Koettbtz might
have had the luck to be the actual finder of
Nansen, but he was washing-up at the time.

The niceties of etiquette : In the big cities
Maclaren tossed personally with the captain of
the Australian team. When he met the Stawell
district men he sent a deputy to toss. It is
understood that the captain of an English Eleven
would lose caste if he personally skied the copper
with the boss of a country team.

A memorial, privately subscribed for, is
to be erected over the grave of Madame Sara
Flower, the well-known operatic and ballad singer
of the fifties, who died in 1865, and was buried in
the Devonshire-street R. C. cemetery. The remains
have been removed to La Perouse, with those of
Rosalie Durand, wife of Fred Lyster (of the
Lyster opera troupe), and William Andrews,
comedian, of the old Victoria Theatre, Sydney.

Sydney daily-paper news-headings :
“The German Fete. Cordiality towards Eng-
land. Germans sing ‘ God Save the King !’ ”
Why not ? The King is more German than
English. He is even more German than his
mother. Victoria’s father was born a British sub-
ject, whilst Edward’s father was born and bred a
German.
Lumley Hill, of Barcoo fame a quarter of
a century ago, is up again for a place at the
coming Queensland elections. Lumley is now
“agin” the Government. The rich wool-kings of
the past gravitated to the Tory class as a matter
of instinct, but the droughts have mostly made
them wool-mendicants, and in the battle for
existence they begin to acquire new instincts.

A W Pearse, the secretary of the newly-
formed Taxpayers’ Union of N.S.W., is a Conser-
tive with decidedly pessimistic views. He looks
with horror on “labor” legislation, islaff ectionately
disposed towards black labor, and predicts all
kinds of financial disasters as a result of the want
of “ encouragement to private enterprise. He is a
fussy, little man, an incessant smoker and a
hustler, and is part owner of the Pastoralists
Review.
One of the most pathetic sights in Mel-
bourne lately was to see H. H. Champion led two
or three times a week to meetings of the Christian
Scientists. The once bright, alert Champion
shows distressing signs of nerve trouble, from
which he has suffered these 30 years, off and on
Christian Scientists’ meetings are attended almost
entirely by women of the credulous type so easily
recognised, people to whom some spiritual or
religious fad has become a necessity. Ihe
majority of them have belonged at different tunes
to various religions, and have gone through the
Spiritualistic and Theosophic crazes. They can
be recognised at a glance by the skilled m
physiognomy—“foolish rabbit faces. Neverthe-
less, Champion has derived the benefit of at least
a temporary belief that the C.S. treatment im-

A lately-deceased very old N.S.W. iden-
tity, who, it was printed, had left a large fortune
(one paper mentioned a very vast sum), turns out
to have been actually insolvent when he died. In
one thing, however, the much-respected old
gentleman was rich —illegitimate children. He
had no less than three left-handed families.
Stories of the tottering veteran’s latter-day frisk i-
ness were current in his lifetime, but they seemed
so improbable that most people set them down as
legends of his gay and long-departed salad days.

Lambert, the Towitta (S.A.) policeman,
hailed by the Sehippan boy to shoo away the
alleged man who murdered Bertha Sehippan,
missed the opportunity of his professional life-
time. Lambert left his bed reluctantly, and,
armed only with a bludgeon, struck out on a black
night across a howling wilderness. He approached
the Sehippan domicile cautiously, reminding the
boys that they would have to stand by him.
When he sighted the body from the bedroom door
he voted it dead ; then retreated to his home. Had
he got to work intelligently on the spot he might
have found something. The constable who wants
to rise in the world needs to hang on to the mys-
tery and the dead body for all he is worth.
Apropos, it is alleged that the officer who first
viewed the result of a big S.A. family tragedy
some years back went stark, staring mad.
When the A.N.A. arose in its loyal fury
at Melbourne, the other day, and upheld the im-
perial dignity by refusing to allow “Jack”
Murray, M.L.A., to lecture it on the “Der-r-ink
Our-rse” because of his anti-war opinions, one
Aye-’u’-Nayite particularly distinguished himself.
Wild-eyed and gesticulating as if jabbing bayonets
into the vitals of the globe generally, this patriot
spoke:
His speeches, and speeches of others like him, were
responsible in great part for the continuation of the war.
(Cheers.) They encouraged the Boers in their hopeless
resistance and caused the loss of many splendid lives.
(Hear, hear.) This conduct had become criminal, and
should be treated so. (Loud cheers.) One could wish
that the days of Cromwell were back, when they did not
argue with traitors, but hanged them or cut off their
heads. (Loud cheers.) If Mr. Murray had come there
that night his reception would have made him sorry.
(Hear, hear.)
And, Oh, Erin ! this rabid Jingo’s name was
Molloy.
Mary Sehippan, at present in Adelaide
gaol, accused of the murder of her sister Bertha,
at Towitta (S. A.), is, whether innocent or .guilty,
a young woman of wonderful self-control. All
through the investigation Mary, neat, scrupu-
lously particular as to the crimping of her front
hair, amiable, prepossessing, and quite collected,
attended to the wants of the officers and to her
ordinary duties. She showed no leaning to ner-
vousness or hysteria. At the inquest she gave
her testimony and heard the verdict, looking all
the time just the same quiet Mary as usual. The
coroner and some others dropped tears, but Mary
showed no grief. She only gave way to tears
when her mother threw her arms about her neck
to weep good-bye. Then she said, “Mother, I
did not do it; ” and got for answer, “ I know you
did not, my child.” But she quickly rallied, and
attending once more to the exact lay of her front
hair and the set of her dress, started on the
journey to gaol. Mary Sehippan won the re-
porters of the big dailies at sight, and they were
her friends to a man.
Abdul Wade, the colored
gentleman with 200 camels, pro-
mises soon to have a monopoly
of the carrying trade at Mt.
Garnet (Queensland). He has
bought out all the teams, and
is now contracting to carry
all kinds of goods at prices
with which no white man
can compete. One great
secret of Abdul’s success
lies in the fact that he
engages black brethren
in Asia at wages which
look respectable as prices
go there, but which, with
Australian rates and
prices, would barely pay
for the feed of one dog,
and not a large dog at
that. With this cheap
and wretched labor he
can undersell the white
carrier everywhere. The
law in the back country
exists largely for thepurpose
of putting Abdul Wade’s in-
dignant employees in gaol for
breach of contract, their usual plea
being disappointment, starvation, and
general misery. Twenty of them struck
recently, and the Queensland statutes sent them
to gaol for six months. The mighty camel-driver
and his drove of indentured niggers are institutions
which the Federal law can’t be too swift to take
cognisance of. The dark and skinny hand of
Abdul is now beginning to gather in much
property, and many white men up F orth know
that paw well as the one which is attended for
tlie rent at regular intervals.

Towards the end of his career, old District
Court Judge Blake, of Queensland, got very-
shaky, but, for all that, it was hard to catch him
napping. He had a habit of going to sleep as
soon as the evidence on both sides had been given
in a civil case ; but he had trained himself to nod
his head and give an occasional assenting grunt
while advocates were speaking, and he always
woke up as the barrister or solicitor who had
addressed him sat down. Then he would bow
politely and doze off again as the other fellow com-
menced. This aggravated the bar considerably ;
and once up at Cooktown it thought it had
his ’Onah, but it hadn’t. A civil case was being
tried without jury, and four reporters, in the free
and easy style of the sweltering North, took
possession of the jury-box, which, being under the
window, was cooler than their own seat. The old
Judge settled himself to a nap while counsel for
plaintiff addressed the court. When that luminary
had finished Blake woke up, smiled, afid bowed,
and then snoozed off again while the advocate for
the defence hurled his eloquence around. When Ac
had finished his Honor awoke with his usual bow,
and, seeing the pressmen in the jury-box, he forth-
with began a long summing-up to what he believed
was a jury of four. Then the bar sniggered and
settled down to enjoy the joke ; but when half-
way through his address the ancient, recognising
the pressmen, saw his mistake. Nevertheless, he
hung on manfully and finished a masterly sum-
ming-up. Then he mildly remarked: “This
being a case of unusual importance I have ex-
plained it at this length in order that the press
may have an opportunity of reporting the matter
in full. I find for the defendant.”

The Bulletin was recently knocked flat
by a machine called the Burroughs Adding and
Registering Machine, of which the Commercial
Banking Co., of Sydney, use seven. It has a
keyboard of figures as a typewriter has letters ;
and these are arranged in numerical order
from one half-penny up to £1,000,000. To add on
the machine, you print off the amounts as you
strike off the words on the typewriter, then you
press a key and the machine thereupon prints the
exact total. The machine is infallibly correct.
Users, after a little practise, can manipulate the
machine at the rate of two thousand cheques or
dockets an hour, and have the total thrown in,
while with the pen they could not write the
amounts, let alone add them. The United Type-
writer and Supplies Co., of Sydney, Melbourne
and Perth, is the concern responsible for intro-
ducing the benefits of this machine to Australia.
They state that a large number of banks, and
many of the leading drapery establishments in
Sydney and Melbourne, such as David Jones and
Co., Ball and Welch, Craig, Williamson and
Thomas, have them in use for listing and adding
up the daily dockets.*
There is one place in Sydney where the
business of money-lending is carried on in an
equitable manner and where those in need of
financial accommodation can rely on being
decently treated. The sensible man who wishes
to get rid of his monetary worries at a minimum
of cost and trouble to himself goes to the offices of
the Australian Equitable Investment Co., Ltd.
The co. has a large capital for investment at low
interest, and is always open to advance money on
risks which the banks would decline; and acts
generally as a medium between the banks and
ordinary money-lenders. Men in business can
obtain assistance for their venture at a figure
which will leave them a fair profit for themselves.
The co. advances money to any extent on deeds of
land, bond warrants, merchandise, stock in trade,
scrip, interests under wills, furniture and all classes
of security. Press notices which have been pub-
lished from time to time express the favorable
opinion which generally exists regarding the co.
Mr. J. E. O’Brien is the Managing Director, and
the co.’s offices are at 45 Elizabeth-street, between
King and Hunter-streets.*

THE FESTIVE SEASON.
THE BOSS: "What, starving with a paunch
like that?"
THE WANDERER : “ Well, boss, I think yer
might overlook that , seein' it's Noo Year."
Towitta (S.A.), the scene of the Scliippan
tragedy, is one of the western flats of the river
Murray. This country never knows exactly where
it is. For a week or two, it is blown bodily
towards the Murray ; then, for a like period, it is
blown back again. This oscillation goes on pretty
well all the year round. The wind there does not
blow in puffs or squalls or storms. It blows with
a long, strong, changeless pull, making hideous,
howling music through every chink and round
every corner. A tornado or a giant whirl-
wind creates as much diversion as would a
first-class circus ; it shakes up the dregs of
sorrow’s cup, as Henry Lawson puts it.
The dust is highly charged with limestone par-
ticles, whence blight, granulation, and all the
other eye troubles. These flats have not raised a
crop for years. The farmers remain only because
they lack the means to get away. They don’t
look forward with glee to a good harvest. They
would not handle it. The creditors would swallow
all. They live chiefly on rabbits and parrots, and
many of them are clothed in sack-cloth. Their
only hope at present is in the swelling of the
subscription lists now passing round Adelaide on
their behalf —the result of the efforts of Jenkin
Coles, who has the misfortune to know the
country. And the-man who knows that country
knows Gehenna.

M‘Cooey on “ Werimee ” :
Re 11 Werimee " (8., 11/l/’O2). Neither kangaroos nor
wallabies lap their drink, and no tommy-rot or distorting
of the inane human visage in 14 opposite directions at one
and the same time, nor even a senseless repetition of the
absurd saying, “ I’ve seen them do so.” will ever convince
any intelligent school-boy that they do, oi ever did. It
is an action quite foreign to the life histo y of each and
every member of the vast order Herbivora.and would have
infallibly been recorded, did such a thing occur, 100 years
ago. Whatever “ Werimee ” may know about swag craft,
about kangaroos he knows nothing.
The dilly-bag of the Australian aborigine
is a structure far in advance of her white sister’s
perishable reticule of commonplace silk or satin.
No self-respecting lubra would part with her
dilly-bag or allow it to fall into holes. It is an
heirloom probably, and, as time disintegrates it,
the weak parts are renovated till the original
structure is quite done away with. A few hairs
taken from the head of a fiiend are woven in, or a
feather from the wing of the black cockatoo. A
little wild hemp, or the fibrous root of the supple-
jack strengthens a seam, and a bit of cloth is
purloined from an enemy on the same principle
that David annexed a morsel of Saul’s night-shirt.
The contents of black Mary’s bag are far more
useful and practical than white Maude’s puff and
powder or her silly little mirror in which she can
only see a section of her face at once. Among them
is, perhaps, a scratching thorn, and the comfort of
a nice scratching thorn deserves to be known more
widely than it is—few white ladies of rank have
ever scratched themselves all over with a thorn;
and the shark’s tooth for use as a toothpick lias
many advantages. The black sister’s dilly is at
once portmanteau, medicine chest and pantry.
The present fashion of carrying a potato filled
with eyes as a cure for rheumatism is a set off
against the toe nail of an enemy carried by Mary
to keep off evil spirits, or the loose bone from the
head of the dugong which is supposed to be an
antidote for almost every disease.

At a farm in Gippsland (Vic.), the other
day, the lightning cut a clean tunnel, no broader
than a man’s hand, through the centre of a hay-
stack. The ends of the sheaves were shorn off as if
with a sharp knife. Lightning is a frivolous cuss.

Dear Bulletin. —Re trees lifting weights.
I will ask “ Jaek-a-Clubs ” (30/11/’01),
also “ H.P.” (9/11/’01) the following : If
a bullet is fired into a growing tree—say
six inches in depth—will that bullet be
carried upwards with the growth ? Be-
cause a scar always remains on the bark
and wood at the original height from the
ground. If they are right, and lam
right, then in, say, ten years time the
bullet would not be found opposite the
scar, but some distance higher up, for,
according to their theoi’y, the tree would
lift the weight as it grew. I could prove
at the present time that a gum, which,
some ten or twelve years ago, was used
as a training-post in a wire fence, has not
lifted the wires an inch, although they
are buried almost out of sight beneath
the bark, and the tree has grown in
height perceptibly. That scars do not
grow upwards with the tree can be
proved by any surveyor. Writer has
cut out scores of old marks in a survey
line 50 years old for verification, arid
found the original marks perfect to
a depth of 15 inches, and in no
case has the scar exceeded in height
an ordinary man’s elbows when in the act of
marking. There is, or was, in the Survey Office,
Hobart, a perfect, solid triangle which was re-
moved from a growing swamp gum at a depth of
12in. The old scar was not more than 3ft. from
the ground, and the survey line was 40 years old.
I cannot doubt “ H.P’s ” assertion that the
Kanaka could not reach the nail he hammered
into a tree seven years previously ; but I think
the subject of the shrinkage of Kanakas requires
investigation. Also, is it dead certain that it was
the same Kanaka? The explanation of the whole
matter is that the top of a tree grows faster in
height than its other portions, the speed diminish-
ing towards the root, where the growth is so slow
as to be imperceptible. Therefore, if the Kanaka
had reached Bft., the nail might have advanced
the 32nd part of an inch—but I doubt even that.
—C.H.R.
Latest cattle-“ lifting ” trick in Rangi-
tikei (M.L.) district. A number of owners of
young steers are known to only visit their stock
once a week or so. “ Duffer” takes them to first
sale-yard and sells. Purchaser paddocks them,
for the night; “duffer” takes them away again,
and puts them where he got them. Rightful
owners swear that they haven’t sold, and that the
beasts have never been out of paddock. Case
badly mixed ; police have no clue ; purchaser left
lamenting ; air full of big D.

“ P.Q.R.” : lie rabbits dying in East
Riverina (Bulletin, 11/l/’O2), I don’t think it is
want of green food. In Wyalong district, where
there is no green food any summer, bunny is
dying off in thousands this year. It is some
disease, perhaps of the mangy order. The fur if
rubbed the wrong way comes out easily. What
show for a scientist to diagnose the complaint and
spread it, and win a big reward for bunny ex-
termination ?

W. M‘Leod writes: He “Scars” par of
11/l/’O2 about two rows of shearers working on
one “board.” This is a common style of wool-
shed. Some of the largest ones are built on
that plan—for instance, “Big” Burrawang and
Yeralia. The shearers on the outside, or “pack-
horse” side as shearers call it, catch out of the
same pens as the inside row of shearers. The
inside row let their shorn sheep go down “shoots”
alongside the catching-pen doors. The shearer is
not considered much in the building of some sheds.

Dear Bulletin. “ Werri-
mee’s ” idea re an “ Aboriginal
Gazetteer ” (8., 11/l/’O2) is not
new. About twelve months ago a
circular was issued from the Lands
Department, Sydney, to every
staff surveyor in the State, request-
ing him to forward the meanings,
with any noteworthy remarks
thereon, of the aboriginal names
of towns, rivers, and prominent
features within his district. He
was also asked to forward to the
Department of Agriculture any
seeds of indigenous trees and
plants he might come across during
liis travels. But the surveyor has
his hands full with professional
work. He is generally under orders
to carry out immediately surveys
at both ends and the middle of his
district. Any information respect-
ing the above-mentioned matters
he is expected to gather in his
own time. Naturally, therefore,
very little thrilling news regard-
ing aboriginal matters is received
from him. Writer knows of one
surveyor who was greatly struck
with the idea when he received
the circular, and at once put down
on the form provided a long name
with which lie was familiar, and
added a few remarks on the
meaning, &c. At the present time
there is only one name on that list.
Apropos of circulars, a curious one
was issued some time ago by the
Under-Secretary for Lands to sur-
veyors and draftsmen. In it they
were instructed that the word
which they had been in the habit
of writing on plans as ti-tree should
in future be written tea-tree. It was
stated that said tree derived its
name from the fact that Captain
Cook once made tea from the
leaves of one of that kind. Who
believes this ?— Scutum.
The mallee district of Victoria has
been struggling amid a cloud of dust to
collect a two-bushel harvest. Nobody knows
who made the mallee, but the Devil is
strongly suspected. The great irrigation
scheme still hangs fire. The alleged lakes,
which make such beautiful snail-tracks on the
map, are dry. Most of the dams have dried up
or else fallen through ; it is a porous country,
where the falling-through is almost as bad a prob-
lem as the evaporation. Fanners are carting
water 16 or 20 miles, and the water-train is once
more an institution in the land. The Bulletin
has an idea that the Falkland Group is the place
where the souls of good Australians go. It rains
.‘lOO days a year there, and if the Australian who
never got properly wet in this world doesn’t get
wet in the next, when does he get wet ?
A DAUGHTER OF THE LAND.
“ Margery," of Wyangorie Station, N.S. W. Died recently at the reputed
age of over 100 years, and looks it.
Told to The Bulletin by a doctor that
Croydon (Q.) hospital has no nurse at all on duty
during the night. One typhoid patient, accord-
ingly, was allowed to wander about the wards in
a delirious state at nights until he died. The
whole nursing staff consists of the wardsman and
his wife, the latter having two young children of
her own to look after. Though a qualified medical
man is available, powerful drugs are prescribed
in an off-hand fashion by a layman. Croydon
hospital does not seem to take its patients very
seriously until they are dead.

N. W. Queensland, having battled through
a three years’ drought, a few thunderstorms
are just commencing to cheer up that weary land.
Also every square inch of some districts is sup-
porting an able-bodied grasshopper awaiting the
arrival of the first blade of new grass. Verily—
the west is a wondrous land !

A party of three

Js.P. and a third

“THE BOYS OUT-BACK.”
11 No doubt it would ‘ amuse the boys out-back,' but it
would at the same time get us ‘ six months’ hard ’ —which
no doubt would also ‘ amuse the boys out-back.’ —Bulletin
“ Answers to Correspondents
To tub Editor.
This is the way of “ the boys out-back”—
The boys on the far Barcoo—
Whose homes are the “ Run ” or the “ Droving Track,”
Where the yarns are always blue.
These are the boys who, year by year,
Keep watch on the weary West—
The hoys whose chief delights are beer
And a good strong tale—undressed.
It’s little they know, and less they care,
llow the world at Sydney wags,
While they muster, brand, and shear at Clare,
Or carry their well-worn swags.
They spend their cheques and they tell their tales —
Their tales of a sapphire hue —
(Till the dear old crimson blanky fails,
These tales will be always new).
And then, on a Sunday calm and cool,
With a pen that is bright sky-blue,
They write these yarns of the out-back school,
And send them along to you.
v.
FRANK RADOLIFFR.

Serious Biding Accident.
The friends of Mr. J. J. O’Sullivan, of Com,
will regret to hear of the very serious effect of the
fall from a horse which he sustained
“One day whilst I was riding,” explained
Mr O’Sullivan, “my horse became unmanage-
able, and I was thrown and sustained an injury
to mv back. Sometime after that X was running
to catch a horse in the paddock when my legs
crossed and I nearly fell down; from that time
the muscles of my limbs became uncontrollable,
and I was told I had locomotor ataxia. My spine
seemed quite without strength, and I lost my
appetite and fell away in general health. When
I tried to walk I used to stumble, and severe
pains ran through my limbs. Notwithstanding a
physician’s treatment, my complaint continued.
But, last October, a lady friend recommended Dr.
Williams’ pink pills for pale people, and I began
taking them. One boxful brought no noticeable
benefit, but as I further continued taking them
I began to gradually improve. After a time I
was able to control my muscles and to walk
properly. Then I regained my appetite, my spine
became strengthened, and by persevering with
these pills the symptoms of locomotor ataxia
abated, and I now enjoy very good health. My
cure is no secret for I have lived m the Cowra

“ S.S.” : The beery towns of Bananaland
cannot give many points to Nagambie, a mangy,
one-street township on the Goulburn (Vic.) with
five pubs, and five wineshops in its single
thoroughfare.
Buffalo hunter P. Cahill writes in truly
startling strain to the Northern
Territory Times re the inroads
made by leprosy among the
aborigines. Cahiil asserts that
in 1892 he employed 194
natives along the East and
South Alligator. Of that
number 6(5 have since died—
mostly from leprosy. Cahill
averts that if the Govern-
ment should start a quaran-
tine station up in the
Northern Territory it would
have a big contract on hand.
Attributes spread of disease
to the Chinese and the visits
of Malayan proas.
The ignorant but ferocious
Kanaka proved an easy victim
for a Bananaland deadbeat, who
recently set about raising_the wind
in the sugar country. With a big
empty loliie-tin propped on a tripod
of three saplings and a piece. of black rag
the d.b. set up business as an itinerant photo-
grapher. Trade boomed wonderfully, and in
three days he took the photo, of almost every
“boy” and “Mary” thereabouts, obtaining a
substantial deposit in each case, and promising
the complete picture as soon as he could develop
the negatives. When the Kanaka found that the
picture of his large black countenance wasn t
forthcoming, he became dangerous ; and the next
photo.-man who goes that way will take his life in
his hand.

A recently-created Jay-Pay in Never
Never Land held an inquest on an unfortunate
who had perished from thirst. This was the
finding:—
I find that the accused died of thirst, exhilerated by
want of water, on or about 10th December, in 'Possum
paddock, Sandyblight.station.
• * * *
Saw blown down at Evinvale
shed, the other day, a magpie’s nest, the sticks of
which were bound together by a ’possum-snare.
Crows, magpies, and hawks prefer short lengths
of wire to sticks, and at nesting time will gather
up all the pieces of wire along the wire-fences.
“ Western ” : Once had a cat that used to
sleep half the day on the back of a pet sheep. Do
any of your readers know of a similar instance of
affection of one animal for another? [A stable-
cat will sometimes take a fancy to sleep on the
back of a particular horse, the neddy generally
coming to approve of the arrangement.]

SUMMER GIRLS ARE IN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
TVS long ago since Winter-tide:
1 The Summer winds come, parched and
dried;
I take me to the shady side ;
I do not care a pin
If I do any work or no;
It’s pleasanter to watch the show
Of little feet trip to and fro,
Now Summer Girls are in!
Oh, no ! they're not dicolleUe,
For filmy lace and flowery spi-ay
Lie lightly where, as some might say,
The blushing should begin;
But still they’re rather careful, yes!
To give facility to guess
How much is girl, and how much di ess,
These Summer Girls so thin!
I wonder if it’s really wrong
To watch them as they pass along—
This softly sinuous female throng;
A nd if it’s any sin
To glance at airy skirts like those
Which fully, daringly, disclose
Each turn of hip, each style of hose,
Now Summer Girls are in 7
No dryad she, the modern maid,
To hide her charms in forest glade;
In daylight fair they’re well displayed
And admiration win;
And lovers gaze till twilight dims
The air, and wonder at her whims,
And dream of shapely, slender limbs
Of Summer Girls so thin!
Then is it naughty—let me know —
To gaze at this bewildering show,
To drop one’s eyes — well, just below
That dainty-pointing chin 7
To love these flowers with woman-face,
These stems of undulating grace,
These arum lilies lapped in lace,
Now Summer Girls are in ?
sydxxy.
ARTHUR FRY.

THE PYRAMID’S BASE.
Ah 1 nomad of the city walks,
Whose rags record you derelict.
Whose tenement the tapster stalks
Until his leer your anger’s piqued ;
Whose phantoms all are bacchanal,
And pavement lights with forms abound
That pesky be, and pesky shall,
Though present not to people round :
I wonder where’s your Talon gold, _
That clawed so well your comfort in—
That wrung the mattress from the old,
And drove the harassed young to sin T
Maybe in other hands as harsh,
Whose goblet’s full, and woman true.
And you—you're nabob in the marsh,
Where Goblet (aye I) may follow you.

LATEST FROM THE S'AFRICAN WAR, BY “THE BULLETIN’S ” OWN WAR ARTIST.
“The battle of Waterloo was won on the cricket-fields of Eton and Oxford.” —Wellington.
“The war in S’Africa is being fought in the various newspaper offices —especially in Sydney.” — UNDERSTODY.


THE LEAP IN THE DARK.
“Undeterred by all that has been said concerning the dangers of emigration to South Africa at the present time, 45 persons visited the Department for External Affairs yesterday and
obtained permits to land there. Thus far 495 persons have, since 2nd January, declared themselves possessed of £100, or able to keep themselves in South Africa if allowed to land there. Practically
this means that the community is losing £49,500 at least in cash, as well as nearly 500 able-bodied men and women.” — Melb. paper.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Queensland Labor Party vexed the soul of
the piebald Philp Ministry last day of the session
by carrying a resolution condemning the leasing
of sugar-growing lands to Chinese.
N.S.W. Assembly has voted £5OOO for
the salaries of four commercial agents to be located
in London, South Africa, China, and Japan.
There are over 200 applicants, amongst them some
members of the Legislature.
Westralia has long been thinking, with a
slow Westralian think, about putting up a fence
to stop the westward march of bunny. It has
shifted that fence in its mind several times. The
two latest reports are that a line has been decided
on, and that the rabbits are already 70 miles
beyond that line.
The Victorian Ministry has appointed a
Treasury clerk and the caretaker of some gardens
at Creswick to act as two Conservators of Forests
in the place of the late G. D. Perrin. After this,
the denuded tree country should blossom forth
with neatly-cut grass and blotting paper and other
towering timber.
The Tailoresses' Union delegates have
seceded from the Sydney Labor Council, taking
as sympathetic companions most of the socialist
delegates. There is a general feeling of relief in
the Council; throughout its history the appear-
ance of the “lady delegate” has always been the
forerunner of storms.
How long are the ignorant J.P. fatmen of
Victoria to be allowed to sit on the Bench
and sneer at the Factories Act, in addi-
tion to nullifying its provisions by
imposing ridiculously small fines ?
A case which occurred at the
Prahran Court the other day
fairly indicates the attitude
of the bulk of the honorary
magistrates towards pro-
gressive legislation. A
Chinese laundry-man was
up for permitting work
to be done during pro-
hibited hours. He
pleaded guilty, and
then the Js.P. on
the Bench —Phipps
and Gooch—pro-
ceeded to say
funny things
about the case.
Phipps remark-
ed that “ the
Chinaman ought
to be .sent to
gaol.” Gooch
then contributed
his bit of spark-
ling wit, and
said, “ Oh, dear,
no! hang the
man.” The court
could not get on
with its business
for a time by
reason of the
laughter of
Phipps and
Gooch at their
own jokes. A
fine of ss. was
i mpose d.
Will the man
who calls
himself the
father of
the Fac-
tories
Act, and
who hap-
pens now
to be Pre-
mier, ;ake
action by
enact in g that
the minimum
punishment for
breach of the Act
shall be not less
than so much, and
thereby make it im-
possible for small-beer
humorists on the Bench
to absolutely nullify the statute? Not much I
W. P. Crick, N.S.W. Minister for Lands,
has achieved more distinction—he has been burned
in effigy. Some Darling River settlers think so little
of his new Western Lands Act that they have flamed -
up in this way. The worst offence of the measure
is held to be the provision relating to the throwing
open of only one-eighth of existing pastoral leases
for closer settlement. But, after all, that marks
a pretty big advance on the previous Acts, which
aimed not at opening up even an eighth of the
leases, but at turning the open country into
leasehold.
Seddon and Ward are losing no time in
nobbling the M.L. Farmers’ Union. At a big
meeting held at Gore recently, Joseph the Wily
addressed the local members, and a vote of confi-
w? 1 ? 6 ! ' ll le Ministry was passed unanimously.
Which wasn’t at all what the originators of the
Union bargained for.
For the year ended June, 1900, the number
of unemployed who applied for work at the
N.S.W. Labor Bureau was 6003. For last year
(to June, 1901) it was 12,252. Work was found
for 9803 of these, but in 8292 cases it was Govern-
ment work paid for out of loan moneys. The
Jolly and Fat policy was a disappointment to the
very last.
Queensland pro-Kanakas still cherish a
hope, founded on the fact that under the Common-
wealth Constitution the vice-regal assent to an
Act can be withdrawn by the King, at any time
within twelve months from its being given by the
Governor-General. The Queensland State Govern-
ment is said to be now making strong representa-
tions to Chamberlain about its lost nigger.
The Dutch have organised a “ British
Boycott League,” pledged not to buy any goods
of British make or origin. But there are two
ends to a boycott, and the Dutch may have got
hold of the wrong one. Last year Britain bought
from Holland a great deal more than Holland
bought from Britain, and if Britain boycotts
Mynheer in return Mynheer will fall in badly.
Cablegram from London re the opening of
Parliament:—
Premier Salisbury also solemnly warned the Liberals of
the danger to the empire resulting from any failure, how-
ever slight, with regard to a recognition of the ABSO-
LUTE SUPREMACY OP THE KING.
The ABSOLUTE SUPREMACY OF THE
KING. Britain hasn’t heard words like these
since the time of Charles I.
“ Pukaki ” writes from Wellington :
Though recruits came forward freely for the Eighth
M.L. contingent, they were not up to the stamp of for-
mer drafts. In Wellington, despite the multitude which
presented itself, the authorities failed to find sufficient
properly qualified men to fill up the district’s section of
the contingent. Of the Auckland squad 20 didn’t turn
up when the boat left, and several more deserted on the
way down to Wellington. Police warrants cut short the
military career of more than one prospective contingenter.
The democratic members of the Federal
Parliament should make a resolute protest against
any proposal to adjourn for Edward Vll.’s corona-
tion, if that step means delaying
such pressing matters as
Federal quarantine, the es-
tablishment of a Federal
Judiciary and the Inter-
state Commission, the
selection of a
capital, &c.
ITALIANO.
“ How is it. Signor, that you Italians sing so
much better than other people?”
“ Well, it is ze maccaront dat we eat. It makes
us all organ pipa winda.”
“Nonem ” writes from the sunset:
Touching western lands in N.S.W. I do not know if
you are aware that the reductions in rents and in
purchase price of these lands, made by the local Land
Boards, have been appealed against by the Minister for
Lands, although the Western Lands Commission advised
such reductions in its report. The Government seems
always to promise to ameliorate the condition of the
western settlers, but never does it, and this latter action
only bears out past history. Surely the country can be
awakened to the fact that the western settlers are being
crushed out of existence by this fraud of a Government.
AN ADVERTISEMENT.
Pertinently asked by a Maoriland paper
why the recently-formed Farmers’ Union hasn’t
protested against the despatch of an eighth con-
tingent. Last harvest the scarcity of hands was
bad enough, but it will be far worse this season,
with another 1000 men sent away just as har-
vesting commences. Farmers will soon realise
the grim fact that they have to buy their labor in
the dearest market and sell their exported produce
in the cheapest.
Awful tales about the leaking and general
cracking-up of the pipes used in Westralia’s great
Foolhardy water scheme are in circulation. The
300-odd miles of pipes are not nearly laid yet, and
there is already talk about the necessity for relay-
ing much of the work. It seems fairly possible
that Coolgardie will be as extinct as Babylon by
the time the water arrives there, though John
Forrest still prophesies that the tardy fluid will
be on hand by the end of 1902.
Rumor drifts around to the effect that
B. R. Wise’s chance for the High Commissioner-
ship is rapidly passing. The men said to be
in it are Bigjohn Forrest, Malcolm M‘Eacharn,
Langdon Bonython, and G. H. Reid. George
can have the billet for the asking. As for Bigjohn,
report says he is a strong second favorite. The
two other knights have evidently been given some
reason to hope that the job will come their way,
and their assumption of indifference is painfully
overdone. Neither knight has any qualifications
for the position, but they are both bloated with
riches.
There was a fearsome stench in the Bana-
naland Parliament some months ago when it was
clearly proven that a Brisbane journal of no in-
fluence and little circulation was being subsidised
to the tune of £lO weekly—per advertisements.
The thing was so bad that even the spineless
Brisbane Telegkaph had to denounce it, and
Philp himself promised immediate reformation.
Just one week after the close of the session, afore-
said journal is trotted out again with 1U pages of
Govt, advts. in one issue—value about £69. And
no one in Brisbane bothers. They’re used to that
sort of thing.
Premier Seddon is not game to apply the
gag to influential anti-jingoes in M.L., but his
Government can vent its petty spite on small
public servants with safety. James B. Murray,
head teacher of the Kirikiri public school, has
been dismissed because he refused to obey the
Board of Education’s ukase to salute “ The Flag.”
Murray protested :
I cannot salute any flag—that is, personally salute it,
as I would a human being ; it is against my conscience.
I have taken occasion to ask every man I got into con-
versation with, and who had served in the British regular
army, if he had ever been asked to salute the flag when a
soldier, otherwise than by saluting the officers carrying
the flags when “ trooping colors.” I always got the same
answer that they had not.
But he was nevertheless sacked : the mean rage
of the pro-Gore party must be sated on someone.
The weakness of the permanent commis-
sion appointed to administer Western N.S.W.
lands seems to lie in the absence of a man with
financial experience—a weakness which looks all
the more serious because one of the Commission’s
most important duties, perhaps the most impor-
tant of all, is to ’secure better terms for the 'man
on the land from the man in the bank. Of the
three commissioners, two, C. J. M‘Master and
R. Macdonald, are old Lands Dept, officers ; each
has had long experience, first as district surveyor
and then as land board chairman in the Western
district; and each has shown more than average
administrative capacity. The third commissioner,
“ Hughie ” Langwell, got into N.S.W. Assembly,
as member for Bourke, after the last big shearers’
strike ; but he soon went out of politics. The
Reid Government resurrected him in the batch of
semi-Laborites “called” to the Leg. Council.
The Western Lands Commissioners’ billets are
worth £IOOO a year each.
Westralia’s adherence to fch«-
old traditions of the convict days-
got back on it badly a little while
ago. There were 46 aboriginal
prisoners in Rcebourne gaol, and
early on Xmas morning 44 of them
escaped. They carried out the
flogging apparatus, leaned it up
against the wall of the yard,
streamed up it like a row of black
ants, and evaporated down the
other side. All the officials could
do, ere going forth to spend their
Xmas racing over the country in
pursuit of their 44 lost niggers
under a scorching sun and through
a thick sand cloud, was to curse the
flogging apparatus.
Premier Philp, of Queens-
land, has been telling a Townsville
audience that “ the present posi-
tion of affairs in Queensland re-
quires old hands and men of ex-
perience at the helm.” In other
words, it requires the continued
presence in office of the gang of old
bunglers and dotards which has run
the show for twenty years or so,
and which has only the present
lamentable position of affairs to-
offer as the result of its labors.
The O’Sullivan (the N.S.W.
institution of that name) has-
broken out in a fresh place with a
a naval programme. It is true he
has only got as far as deciding on
the names which his fleet will bear
—75 “euphonious and significant
aboriginal titles,” whose English
meanings range from “small-pox”
to “ why weepest thou ? ” As mud
and garbage punts and pleasure
achts are built or acquired, each will have bestowed
on it one of the chosen gems of aboriginal euphony.
Considering the use to which Government yaehts-
are usually put in N.S.W., the O’Sullivan might
more appropriately have sought out the native-
equivalents for such phrases as “ Gold hair and
Gold top,” “Little Ducky ” and “High Jinks.”
Daily paper telegram from Lithgow
(N.S.W.):
At the ironworks theonly departments working are the
bar and guide mills, which have put in full time since -
Monday. The sheet mills remain idle. It is not defi-
nitely known when these mills will again make a start.
About 100 employees have been rendered idle by their-
stoppage.
Exactly. The Foreign-trade and Joe Cook party
got the Protective duty on certain products of these-
works amended out of the tariff, and the slump,
follows promptly. Another Australian industry
is thus sacrificed to the foreign importer.
One of the retiring Broken Hill munici-
pal councillors this year is Jabez Wright, who is
blamed for nearly all the disasters of the past two
years. He is not standing again. The bill for
the recent Equity suits over the rates levied on
the B.H. mines is believed to run to over £12,000.
The ap{ eal to the Privy Council is being pro-
ceeded with—and the mines are proceeding with
some more Equity cases. Meantime, the condition
of the town, which has practically no monev to
pay for anything, is a thing of sorrow. Men
named Jabez may mean well, but they have no
luck.
Maoriland Premier Seddon is sending an
eighth contingent, and offering more contingents
later on. Meanwhile, despite the increase in the
revenue, there are signs of serious financial em-
barrassment, and the war-bill is evidently eating,
vulture-fashion, into the State’s resources. Maori-
land, for its population, hascontingented far more-
violently than any other State, and it has the
heaviest war-bill to face. Public works are coming
to a standstill for lack of funds, and men are
being discharged in all directions. The work of
building the North Island Trunk Railway has
closed down, and Seddon confesses there is no
cash left to go on with. The Government has-
even turned dog in the matter of subsidies to local
bodies. On some technical plea, which has never
been raised before, it is refusing to pay subsidies
which were passed on the Estimates, and in
reliance on which several local bodies have entered
into large contracts. If Seddon goes ’Ome to the
Coronation he will have to take something with
him to put up the spout.
Mr. A. R. Barclay, M.H.R. for Dunedin
(M.L.), and a well-known barrister, writes to a
local paper:—
The time has come when equitable terms of peace
should be offered to the Boers. The honor of England
has been deeply stained by this war. It is a scs ndalous
and infamous war. It has excited the horror of the
civilised world, who would never have expected to see
Britain in South Africa copying the methods of warfare
adopted by General Weyler in Cuba. . . . The fact is
that this war has been unnecessary and unjust from the
start, and the people of Britain are now at last thoroughly
grasping the truth. They are rapidly coming to the con-
clusion that the time has come when we should cease
choking the life out of these ragged, wretched farmers.
. . . The irony of the thing is that all this is being done
under the cloak of sacred patriotism, and we are told we
are fighting for “ King and country” ! What a mockery 1
What a falsehood ! The truth is, we are fighting to grab
somebody else’s country in the interests of the South
African Chartered Cos. and financial magnates who coveted
the Transvaal wealth.
Will Premier Seddon now fulfill his implied pro-
mise to Mr. Chamberlain, and immure this traitor
in Wellington’s deepest dungeon —or behead him?

Extract from one daily paper version of
Captain Galloway’s account of the abandonment
of the Port Patrick, off Wilson’s Promontory
(vessel subsequently picked up by a tug, unin-
jured) :
He urged the crew to be men and stick to their vessel.
Hoping to inspire them, he sang three verses of the well-
known song, “ Stick to the Ship, Lads,” but it was of no
avail, and they stubbornly refused to accede to his
request.
This picturesque incident was denied promptly
and at great length by members of the crew ; but,
as a shrieking storm was blowing, it would have
been quite possible for the captain to sing as
represented, and for his manly voice to be lost, in
the din of the tempest. But it has also been denied
by the captain, who states that he was inter-
viewed by telephone, and that either the tele-
phone wire was of an untrustworthy and romantic
frame of mind, or else the reporter misunderstood
plain nautieal language. Further, it has been (or,
if it hasn’t, it ought to have been) pointed out
that the well-known song quoted runs—
You stick to the ship, lads,
And I’ll save my life.
and that it is not at all inspiring, but only comic.
The Bulletin sincerely regrets all these denials.
What piquant possibilities are there in the story !
Imagine the British master mariner of the future,
guaranteed to sing inspiriting songs in all the
Asiatic languages, also in Greek, German, Italian,
Turkish and Bagdad, to encourage his “ bulldogs”
in case of accident. And even then the Argentine
sailor who begins to transpire on British ships
would have to sing to himself in Spanish.

MALARIAL FEVER
CURED BY
VITADATIO
Warwick, Queensland, May 28, 1901.
Sir, —Doubtless you will be glad to receive another
testimonv to the efficacy of your valuable remedy,
VITADATIO. t
About 12 months ago I went to the Ehagoolah gold-
fields, and thence to New Guinea, full of health and hope,
but it was not long before I became a victim to that
fearful climate, which completely undermined my health.
I was treated in Northern Queensland in two hospitals
without success, finally, and with difficulty that would
take too long to detail, I reached Brisbane, when I
secured your remedy, which, 1 am glad to say, at once
produced a magical effect, and ultimately restored me to
health and vigor.
I am delighted to be able to write thus, and would
suggest that you publish this in the papers circulating in
New Guinea, where 1 am so well known.
1 am not a doctor, but I believe my trouble was a severe
disorder of the kidneys, caused by and attended with
malarial fever.—Yours truly, „„„„
(Signed) ANDREW JAMIESON.
To S. A. Palmer, Vitadatio, 28/5/1901.
For further particulars,
S- A. PALMER ,

AUDITORS’ REPORT.
We have examined the foregoing Balance Sheet, made up to 31st December, 1901, and report that we find tli
several items therein stated to conform to the Balances at Head Office, and to the Balance Sheets furnished by th
various Branches, the latter being certified to by the respective Managers and Accountants. We have counted tl
Cash and examined the Bills held by the Bank at the Head Office in Brisbane on the 31st ult., and hereby certify th i
we have found them correct. We further certify that, in our opinion, the Balance Sheet is full and fair proper
drawn up, and exhibits a true and correct view of the affairs of the Bank as shown by the Books on the 3D t
D cember, 1901.
Brisbane, 11th January, 1902.

“ J.A.C.” : A Berlin paper recently asser-
ted that the Unter den Linden, in Berlin,
is the widest street in the world (about
214ft.), and that the second widest street is the
Vienna Ringstrasse, with 187ft. Now, the second
widest street in the world, at this rate, is Sturt-
st., Ballarat, Vie. (198ft.); and, as for the widest,
what about Sackville-st., Dublin? By the way,
there is in the small township of Pahiatua (Wel-
lington province, M.L.), a thoroughfare—two rows
of buildings, with an intervening desert, which,
if you try to cross it on a wet day, appears to be
five miles wide.
Of the returned N.S.W. cohentingenters
who sought re-enlistment in the outgoing gang
50 per cent, were rejected owing to heart disease
contracted during their former term of service.
Nature is, indeed, satirical. Have farm-burners
really got hearts ?
N.S. W. Discharged Prisoners Aid Society
has set up its shingle at the house in Elizabeth-
street, Sydney, where Sheridan, since hanged,
carved up Jessie Nicholls and packed her body
away in a trunk.
Stated in a recent London police-case that
the Salvarray collected the rubbish from about
1500 well-to-do houses, and sifted out great hauls of
valuable articles, from spoons to watch-chains arid
cheques. The present manager of the dust-bin
department of the Army stated that he had given
instructions to stop the looting that had formerly
been winked at, and to return valuables to the
owners when possible. But what a sordid business
it seems —this spectacle of the Church grubbing
in 1500 dirt-boxes in quest of bones and lost
property.

Melbourne Argus writes gravely thus :
' n en tirety, the speech delivered by the Prince
or wales at the Guild Hall was in conception and ex-
pression admirable and worthy of the occasion. The
substance of it will be incorporated in British history as
a thoughtful summary of a memorable voyage.
Incorporated in British history ” is a memorable
slab of flattery. Heaven only knows how often
things like that have been said, yet the speeches
of princes and kings drop through the bottom of
history and disappear like small change dropping
into perdition. Of all the thousands of speeches
delivered by all the British kings since Egbert’s
day who remembers one that is more than a month
least of all one by a small harmless man
telling how he went round peacefully in a steamer?

SPORTING NOTIONS.
A. feature of Monday’s test-match was the fact
that Maclaren—through the absence of Barnes—had to
give the left-hander Gunn a fair chance ; and that bowler
proved the excellence of his quality by bagging 5 for 76.
i fi < ? ommence d badly, and had there been anyone else to
fall back upon, might not have been allowed to continue.
Once again in consecutive “tests” Hill struck hard luck
by getting out on the stroke that looked like bringing
him his century. On the same day Hayward followed in
the wake of Maclaren and reached his thousand for test
matches.
• * * *
Monday saw another Australian downfall, and
play ended much in favor of the Britishers, who had 165
runs to the good and only one wicket down. This was
largely attributable to the failure of the last half-dozen
on the Australian side. Two were out for 173 on
Saturday, and, though that average fell off subsequently,
things went well to the fifth wicket, which brought 260
up—94 in excess of opposing side’s total at the same
stage. Then a rot set in, and the other six only added 61,
which means a shortage of 67 compared with the English-
men s first innings. Provided nothing extraordinary
occurs, it is a fair assumption that the Britishers will
place the third “ test ” to their credit.
• • • •
Braund notched his first century in a “ test ” on
Saturday by knocking up 103, not out. Braund shows
out as the best all-rounder on his side. Always danger-
ous with the bat, there are few, if any, more promising
bowlers, and, bar perhaps Jessop, none better in the
field. He was among the few that accumulated cen-
turies against the last Eleven that went ’Ome from this
Bide.
V ' * • * •
vr -^ l arne8 ’ knee being badly wrenched will affect
Maclaren’s team seriously, as ’tis quite possible that he
may not be up to bowling in the next “ test ” at Sydney.
An accumulation of fluid at the joint rendered the use of
splints and laying up abed for two or three days abso-
lutely necessary.
* * • •
The halt and the lame were much in evidence in
latest test-match. Prior to start, Noble, Howell, Kelly
and Gregory were more fit for hospital than cricket.
Massage treatment was brought to bear freely. Reported
that Howell and Kelly, a day before beginning, “ were fit
only to sit on chairs and read the papers and talk to each
other.” Nol le “ was walking about, but it hurt him to
do it. Then, while the match progressed, the wires told
about Noble that “ there was very little of that great
dash m his delivery that was so strong a feature of the
second test-match in Melbourne;” and about Howell
that “ his leg was so bad that he could scarcely run up to
the wicket.” The close of the second over on Saturday
saw Howell limping to the pavilion to get his maimed
underpin bandaged ; and Kelly was palpably lame all the
time. Sid Gregory’s trouble, previously put down to a
hard knock, has since been ascrilied to a strain that may
assert itself any time. Question uppermost now is how
the selection committee, knowing all this, came to include
these four ? Possibility of Noble bowling well, even
though crippled, might have justified his presence ; but
what of Howell and the strong likelihood of Gregory not
being able to see a long innings out? Up to time of
writing, Kelly’s work has demonstrated that no harm was
done in giving him a place.
• • * *
“ Maclaren’s position as an amateur in English
cricket,” writes Tins Bulletin’s London correspondent
“ is being seriously attacked by the St. James’ Gazette.’
It asserts that the crack bat is ijoing* to Hampshire as
assistant treasurer, at an absurdly large salary for such a
poaition in such a club, merely because his cricketical
abilities have made it worth while to seduce him from
Lancashire. Lord Harris points out that Lacey—paid
sec. of the Marylebone O.O.—always plays as an amateur,
and, if he, why not Mac. ‘My lud ’ says he himself had
his hotel and travelling exes paid in the Australian tour,
and he thinks it proper all amateurs should be similarly
treated. The discussion has aroused so much interest
that it is not unlikely the M.C.C. may feel compelled to
clearly define ‘ what is an amateur ? ’ ”
• • • •
Rumored again that Maclaren is to remain in
N.S.W.—as a master in a leading N.S.W. R.C. college.
It was as headmaster of King’s School, Parramatta, the
last time. No doubt Mac. would gladly stay here, if the
billet was a sufficiently tempting one, especially after the
hard knocks dealt out by’Ome papers over his appoint-
ment, at a salary, as “ assistant treasurer ” of the Hamp-
shire Club. Maclaren probably can’t understand why he
should not be allowed to follow in Dr. Grace’s steps as a
“ cash amateur.”
* * • m
“Cash”: Recent par (Bulletin, 21/12/01) re
“ piffle ” sent out by cable doesn’t quite hit the point
The fact that Maclaren has been made assistant-treasurer
of Hampshire County is of interest, as showing what
amateurism is to-day. What is an assistant-treasurership,
anyway? A paid billet with no particular duties, in-
vented to assist a man who does not fancy being paid as
a pro. ? Writer is fairly certain that Maclaren held a
similar billet in Lancashire. And he is quite certain that,
when Sam Woods once talked of leaving Somerset, Palairet
said openly that they “ couldn’t afford to lose him ; ” and
S.J.W., being already captain, was solemnly appointed
“ assistant secretary.”
• • • •
Maclaren’s luck in tossing has been remarkable,
and much ahead of any other ’Ome skipper’s. Up to
date, as captain of England against Australia, he has won
the option of going to the wickets himself, or sending his
opponents there, seven out of eight times.
* * • •
“A.C.”: N. S. Wales’ defeat of S.A. was
apparently anticipated by Adelaide people, for the gate
receipts were much below the average. S.A. team was
the weakest put out by that State for years, and even
when the chance came to put in a good junior, George
Giffen’s little brother Walter was revived. As usual, this
mediocre player was a failure. The one bright feature
of the S.A. display was Kirkwood’s 67 in the second
innings. Travers, the slow bowler, did better work than
at any previous time in his career, and his name is
mentioned as a possible member of the next team to
England. “ Mary Ann ” Noble strained his side again in
the second innings of S.A. At the time this happened he
was doing as he liked with the local batsmen. This is the
third time Noble has strained himself in inter-State
cricket, and uncharitable people are saying that he saves
himself for the bigger events. Jones bowled the biggest
rubbish ever seen in Adelaide in first-class cricket. He
seems to have lost all his old length, as well as much of
his pace. It looks as if Jones should be discarded from
the team for England, though it is understood he is a
certainty for the trip.
• • • •
“ Adelaide ” ; Duff took the wickets when Kelly
was hurt, here, which at once raised the question whether
he is competent to act in the less important matches in
England. If so, M’Kenzie would be shut out, but room
would be made for another all-round man. Charlie
Gregory is much fancied by S.A. players, and the crowd
is ready to believe him quite as good a bat as Duff, while
badly wanting to know what made Clarke formidable to
Maclaren and Co.
• • * •
Noble, as sole selector, has picked the following
N.S.W. team fonthe inter-State match against Victoria,
on SaturdayS. Gregory, 0. Gregory, Duff, Hopkins,
Clarke, Trumper, Noble, Poidevin, M‘Beth, Carter, and
Kermode, with Parquhar as emergency. What a miser-
able attempt to infuse fresh blood 1 ’Twould be easy to
choose a second team that could knock spots off this one.
A“ e . inclusion of Kermode in the revised team suggests
that the selector did it the day he was very tired. Ker-
mode is a moderate bowler who, up till a few weeks ago
played on mud wickets at Moore Park, Sydney. He
trundled well in that fourth-rate grade, but has never
done anything to justify the honor ladled onto him.
there are a score of better bowlers playing in first and
second grade who should have a show before him. Marsh
the aboriginal, is left out. Is it his color that is objected
to C His averages for two seasons and a-half in first-grade
cricket are 358'2 overs, 87 maidens, 1137 runs b 105
wickets an average of 10 83. Farquhar, who is picked
W?n e , m ? rgr K nCy ’ J? a t)e «er bat than any in the team,
barring, perhaps, Gregory. He has an average of 70 odd
for competition cricket this season. He has been
emergency several times now, but, when there’s an open-
ing, he has always had to give way to someone else no
better than himself.
* * * •
A. C. ” : Jack M'Kenzie and Carter, the latter
a promising N.S. Wales “ keeper,” are the two men from
whom the second wicketkeeper of the team for England
will almost certainly be chosen. The N.S.W. players are
strongly urging Carter’s claims, but big M'Kenzie’s ex-
perience will probably secure him the position.

A much-nurtured and speedy pony, with which
some Ikeys had a little to do, was let loose at Kensington
(Sydney) recently, and hungry punters rushed it
greedily, grabbing twos as if the money onlv had to be
picked up, while Mo. laid it just as freely' and smiled
berngnly the while. The good thing fairly cantered home,
much to the undoing of “books” generally, who looked
as if a thunderbolt had struck them ail; they were
paralysed for several seconds, while jubilant backers
rubbed it in thick. There was no escape ; settling had to
°n- Most bent to the inevitable and tried to look
unruffled, but the effort proved too great. Others refused
point blank to pay out. Full strength of the thing
leaked out among a few. Those working the anticipated
scoop galloped the pony to a standstill in the morning,
li 1 "} up w,th as mueh water M h e’d take, and then
settled down to garnering the guileless punters’ stuff,
inej’ naan t reckoned on such a comparatively poor field
fining up, and the nag is such a thorough good ’un that
bar being pulled completely, the jock found it impos-
sible to stop him. True bill.

“Crank”: Squeezed into the thick, heavy
atmosphere of Sydney Golden Gate A.C. on Saturday
night, and through the thick haze came the thicker voice
of referee announcing that Turner was 9 and Billy
McKell 8.8 stone. Writer swears Turner was at least a
stone heavier. Then a little boy (Turner’s brother, thev
said) came into the ring and was affectionately hugged
by Turner aforesaid, and carefully passed out again
It was Cocker Tweedie who brought this sentimental
touch from Murka, but the coves and the blokes weren’t
touched a bit, and scoffed loudly. McKell was mostly
bone and skin, with a little muscle here and there and
some places would be better if filled up. Bob Turner
alleged bantam champion, slogged often and harried some
in every round, but failed to produce any effect till
McKell twisted his foot in failing in the 11th round, and,
being utterly crippled, the thick voice of the referee was
wafted up again to the effect that “ Turner’s de winner,
gen’l’men 1 ” The most remarkable thing about the fight
was the way. the referee sweated.
• • • •
Champion middle-weight bruiser, Snowy Stur-
geon, had another victim served up to him at Sydney
Gaiety A.C. last week. One or two stiff thumps and
Dawson thudded on the boards. Marvellous how fist-
fight followers stand this sort of thing. Dawson quaked
visibly while preliminaries were being fixed, and quaked
speakingly ever after shaping. Snowy went to work as
if engaged in a gym. spar, and got home pretty well
when and how he wanted to. A left jab on the point
completed the job inside 4|min. of actual work. Re-
ported that Sturgeon is going ’Ome, under the fatherly
aegis of a prominent Sydney shop Mo., to have a tilt at
the best in Kngland and America. If the project
materialises Snowy will give them all the fight they want,
but will have to change his methods, otherwise the
financing syndicate will be broke.

“ Fools and their money ! ” A week or two
ago Broken Hill miner Mat Mitohie licked the Irish-
Cockney pug, Denny Moloney, conclusively, at Sydney
Golden Gate. Now, somebody has posted a hundred for
Moloney against Mitchie again. Were flukes barred odds
of 6 to 1 wouldn’t tempt any judge who looked at the
previous rough up; it was nothing else. Mitchie is
strong, chockful of grit, and clever. Moloney is every
fraction as plucky, but isn’t as strong, nor nearly so
clever. On this, picking the winner is child’s play.

1 ) on Monday' night for the State lightweight

M.L. s policeman-athlete, Arthur Skinner, made
things fairly buzz at Caledonian Sports in Auckland, New
Year s Day Using one hand only, he hurled the wooden-
handled 161 b. hammer 102 ft.—a fresh Australian best, and
only an inch behind the world’s record. He also did
an exhibition throw of 167 ft., which, if done under
accepted conditions, constitutes a best on record for pros.
A 561 b. weight was cast from a 7ft. square (without follow)
40ft. sm.—truly a remarkable feat. Irish-American

unlimited run and follow, are also eclipsed. Same man
tossed the caber 47ft., and subsequently appropriated the
wrestling championship. A champion, truly, from top
hair to toe-nails. r

Second round N.S.W. Bowling Association’s
Pennant tourney finished Saturday, when Rand-
wick, Manly, Annandale and Victoria Park (last for
years past always threatened danger and looked sure
winners of the flag at times) dropped out completely.
Old rivals Balmain and City, who for long had turn arid
turn about at hoisting the pennant, met, and City con-
quered, after a hot tussle, by 6 points only. St. George
club s win by 4i over Gladstone Park previous week gave
chance against Ranclwick, but they only
pulled the game out of the fire by 4. Redfern’s defeat of
Ashfield by 119 to 96 suggests that the former has picked
up much power since last season. Strangely enough
draws for Pennant and B matches brought Waver lev’s’
two teams against Manly’s reps. ; and Waverley’s suc-
fwao 1 " °. th ’ ttl r ng the Principal match by no less
won 4 h P oln ! s ’ Leonards accounted for Annandale
by 20, but only through effecting a fine pull up in the
finishing stages. Spite of Sydney’s young>est club (St
George) running away from Gladstone Park in the first
round, latter body accumulated 20 more than Chatswood
m the second.

Amateur Sculls Handicap, at Drummoyne and
Hunter s Hill regatta, Saturday, was won by sheer grit
on the part of little Australasian champion, J. J. Dalev
Tas. crack, J. Burke, competed, with lOsec. start. After
gaining some, Daley’s lack of condition told, and the end
of a mile saw him a length behind. But he struggled on
and, with a desperate effort, pipped Burke by half-a-
length. Burke is in Sydney, learning from Chris Neilsen
His stroke is long and swinging, but his grip and finish
are faulty, and his leg-work wants remedying—he slides
too long. ‘

About the last sport the little Jap might be
expected to excel in is rowing". Yet a crew from their
battleship Mikasa beat one from H.M.S. Hazard when the
two lately found themselves in port together.
* * m m
Something like a record in homing pigeon flying
was put up in Maoriland lately, when, of 33 birds liberated
at Kaitoke, 30 reached Timaru, distance 316 miles, in 73-
hours. 3
* * * *
The Englishmen’s chance of winning another
one or more of the test matches depends largely on
Lilley. He was a sick man when they got so badly beaten
at Melbourne. Both Duff and Armstrong were missed
by Lilley before they got set, and his two batting
attempts showed him to be a lot below par. With the
wicketkeeper in his best form, the three B. bowlers in their
best form, and Gunn making himself more useful than he has
been as yet, the visitors would give the Australians a shak-
ing, if they didn’t actually take the third trick. But the
weakness of their emergency bowlers and their disposition
to lose heart if Maclaren and Hayward fail to open the
innings promisingly are points that nobody can deny.
As compared with the Australians the Britishers are no
good in time of trouble. They have fewer resources for
making a recovery. The betting looks to be 5 to i
against them before the start, and 3 to 1 if they don’t
hold their own at the end of the first three hours’ play,
whichever side may win the toss. A lot of people who
ought to know—old Australian Eleveners among them—
declare that Maclaren holds winning cards, but the
oracular declarations of experts are rarely correct when
the general opinion of the shilling crowd is opposed to
them. In cricket, as in rowing results, the outside
experts are always looking for reasons why the best team
ought not to have won.

THE N.S.W. FRESH FOOD Sc ICE CO. Ltd., Sydney
Melbourne Age is very wrath with the Demo-
cratic Rep. Higgins for casting “an unmerited
stigma on Australian loyalty and public spirit by
referring to ‘ volunteers at ss. a day.’ ” Shocking !
but still it is open to David Syme and other repre-
sentatives of Australian “ public spirit ” to refute
the base insinuation by organising a contingent
that will fight at 2s. day, or eighteenpenoC. A
stigma that is so easily removed ought to be
shifted immediately. By way of helping the
process, The Bulletin begs to make a definite
offer. If the Federal Government will send away
another 10,000 men (which will make the Austra-
lian contingent about equal, considering the
respoetive populations, to that of Britain); and
the men will accept 2s. 6d. a day and rations
(which is more than the British soldier or any
other regular soldier outside Australia gets), so
that they will really be showing their enthusiasm
and making some slight sacrifice for the Empire; and
the Federal'Government will levy a new income-
tax of 6d. in the £ to pay the bill instead of
leaving it to posterity, in order that the Jingoes
who stay at home may also make some slight
sacrifice for the empire. The Bulletin will give
ihat contingent its support, and pay its share of
the war tax. Will any Jingo, even the wildest
Jingo, do as much \ don't all speak at once !
Premier Barton says this country is ready to
send more troops, and to pay for them if necessary.
A favorable opportunity here presents itself.
Premier Barton’s “slim” policy has met its
reward ; and we see the resolutions of sympathy
with Britain—passed by the House of Representa-
tives only—accepted by the world as passed by the
Commonwealth Parliament. It is time the Senate
protested against this usurpation of its rights
and authority. When the Commonwealth Parlia-
ment speaks for Australia as a nation, the Senate
has as much right to be consulted as has the House
of Representatives. It equally represents the
people. The House of Representatives has no
business to arrogate to itself the authority of
Parliament ; and well Barton knows it.
Dear Bulletin. —Your “Plain English ” re the
SINCOCKtAN statement that N.S.W. has a larger
percentage of convicted criminals than Vic. made
no allusion to the inefficiency of the police service
in Marvellous Melbourne. Every suburb has a
recent record of highway robbery which left the
police quite in the dark as to the identity of its
perpetrators. The two or three masked men who
stuck-up a tram-car are still rejoicing at large.
The ease of the jockey who was robbed on the
Yarra bank and thrown into the river last
Caulfield Guineas night has dropped out of public
memory. Mere thefts from the back-yards and
gardens of private houses, committed by bands
of right-o’-way prowlers are so frequent that
the police scarcely trouble to take down the
particulars, and rarely report them to the press.
Melbourne is the paradise of Bill Sykes and
the Artful Dodger, owing to those right-o’-ways,
formerly a necessity in all parts of the unsewered
city, and still serving their old pestilential purpose
in many outlying districts. This fact is generally
overlooked by people who admit Melbourne to be
the easiest of places for a notorious embezzler to
get away from, or escape conviction in if he
prefers to stand his ground. Once more the
question—why have Melbourne Trinity College
councillors and the Vic. Crown Law Department
done nothing to bring to a trial the gentleman
charged with having got away with £3OOO of the
Trin. Col. funds? And why have the “respect-
able” Melbourne journals aided, by their silence,
the hush-it-up conspiracy ?— Collins Street.
♦
The average Australian may be knocked
prostrate by fierce test-match problems, aided and
abetted by perspiration, dust, flies, George Reid
and the Federal tariff. But he (the a. A.) is
never worried for a moment by any problem as to
which is the best remedy for restoring him to his
normal state of cheerfulness. Experience has
taught him to promptly resort to D.C.L. when-
ever a reviver is necessary. To be denied access
to that deleetable beverage. The Whisky of the
Wise, is about the worst kind of calamity which
can strike one in this great thirst-stricken
country.*
