By the death, at Woollahra (Sydney), of
Mr. P. J. Holdsworth, last Sunday night, Aus-
tralia lost one of its minor poets and a prose- writer
of some grace. Originally a civil servant, Mr.
Holdsworth. after retiring on a pension, devoted
himself to writing, and was a frequent contributor
to various Sydney papers.
Just as The Bulletin goes to press it is
alleged, by cable, that Chamberlain has struck the
weary and over-laden Seddon for another (the
ninth) contingent of 1000 men —this when ne
hadn’t quite finished getting the eighth contin-
gent away. Maoriland has already sent away, in
proportion to population, more troops than Britain
has; but an impression is evidently gaining ground
at the other end that Richard of Kumara is a
simple-minded seeker after a dukedom, and Cham-
berlain is running him for all he is worth. It
isn’t every day that so huge and fresh a victim
falls into the hands of the three-card man.
Seddon is now toiling harder to conquer South
Africa for Britain than Britain is to conquer South
Africa for itself.
It was what Mr. Mantalini might call
“demnition hot,” and the dinner was uneat-
able. Macgillycuddy, purple-faced, exasperated,
sprawled at the head of tne table; Mrs. Mac.,
purple-faced and snappy, sprawled at the foot;
and six little purple-faced, peevish Macs., in
various stages of undress, sprawled round it. The
only things that displayed any energy were the
flies —especially 11 of them that were trying to
wade through the oily slush that bore a distant
resemblance to butter. The meat had gone “off,”
the milk was sour, the blanc-mange was white
mud, and the water more than luke warm. Here
was all the material for a first-rate row, which
duly arrived. And all because Mac. had not
bought that 38s. 6d. lee Chest from McLean Bros,
and Rigg, Ltd., 107 Elizabeth-street, Melbourne.*
Splendid sample of human intelligence
from Australian Star (Sydney) of 20/1/’O2 :
Whatever else the pro-Boers may be deficient in, they
were not behind the door when imagination, or the gift
of making highly-imaginative statements, was served out.
The sympathisers in England and the Continent with
that holy old person, Paul Kruger, have been thrilled and
horrified' by the stories of Kitchener’s concentration
camps, the fearful barbarities practised by Tommy Atkins
upon defenceless women and children, arid the awful,
unheard-of mortality in those camps. And these state-
ments sound quite impressive until subjected to the tests
of common sense and of simple arithmetic. . . . And
here the vulgar multiplication table, which has abso-
lutely no grain of imagination, comes in. The total
population of the camps for December last was 117,017.
The total deaths have been 2380, or TWO PER CENT. 1
The population was composed as follows
Children .. ..' .. .. 56,424
Women .. .. .. .. 42,905
Men .. .. .. .. 17,688
Total 117,017
And it is only reasonable to assume that among the
seventeen thousand odd men was a large majority incap-
able of bearing arms, either through ill-health or old age.
The deaths (1767) among the children represent 3.1 per
cent.—slightly less than Sydney’s figures for 1900. These
are facts. The cheerful pro-Boer will, despite them, no
doubt continue cheerfully to lie.
This incredible being apparently doesn’t know
the difference between 3 and a fraction per cent.
per annum and the same per month, which
pans out at 37 per cent, per annum —the real mor-
tality among the children in the concentration
camps as shown by his own figures. And this
sort of person is allowed to give instruction to
the public in the editorial columns of a leading
metropolitan paper!
An excited correspondent to one of the
daily papers:
The spectacle of five members of oar Commonwealth
Parliament refusing to Join in a sympathetic message to
the land that gave them liberty is enough to make the
early colonists (the Britishers who made the country fit
for foreigners to live in) turn In their graves.
But the British authority of those days took a pre-
caution against any “ turning ” by burying many
of those early settlers in their chains.

HEALTH, STBENCTH. HAPPINESS
HEALTH
Few recognise the BLISS of being in PERFECT
HEALTH, until SUDDENLY certain symptoms are
noticed, slight at first, but gradually RECURRING,
causing annoyance and SUFFERING, the individual
being forced to admit he is unwell, and finally compelled to acknowledge that PERFECT
HEALTH is a PRECIOUS BOON.
STRENGTH
are PRICELESS to him.
What a blow to a man who discovers he is LOSING
his STRENGTH and VIGOR, and THAT perhaps al
a period when he most NEEDS ALL his physical
and menial Capabilities to battle through life. The\
He must by every means endeavor to REGAIN TH EM.
HAPPINESS
Does happiness consist in Wealth? No. Who can
be happy without HEALTH and STRENGTH?
Wealth materially assists the endurance of suffering
...... . .. .„ but PERFECT HEALTH, STRENGTH and VIGOR
should be the natural inheritance of EACH individual, and are invaluable gifts, tending to a
baTW existence and long life, THE HEALTHY, STRONG MAN CAN contend, and SUCCESS-
FULLY, with the billows of life. BUT HOW CAN THE WEAKLY AND SICKLY?
Health, Strength and Happiness
can ho ALL REGAINED.

“ 2nd Mounted Infantry ” to The Bctixe-
TtN concerning the barbarous Boer: —
1 was one of 20 who fell into the hands of Kruitzinger’s
commando. They gave us half of what they had in the
Way of tucker, water, tobacco, &c. The prisoners in our
own hands at different times were decent fellows, and
Could easily be taken for typical Western drovers. Plenty
pf men in the fighting Australian corps never fired a shot
in anger, but the rifle hung up in the ancestral hall will
probably tell for years of bloodthirsty deeds and hair-
raising bayonet charges.
From a 17-year-old N.S.W public school-
boy’s exercise
They were a hedes sight. Some had their ribs armes
and faeses, others were dorbed with red and yellow oko
and all were smied with gresce. As the boat caihe on
they branghed their spains, uttered savged, and worked
themselves into a steeit of furey. It was then that Sturt
notsed with dismay that a sand bag ran far out into the
stream. Along the blacks in a dence body rushed with
Shouts of trimfe.

At Poverty Point.
The old, lately-deceased “ first part” at Rickards’ was
a considerable Australian industry. The cost in salaries
of three first parts—Sydney, Melbourne and Adelaide—
was nearly £12,000 a year, and about 80 per cent, of the
money went to feed Australian talent. True, some of the
talent wasn’t very deserving, but Rickards took the best
he could get. The first part was a go-as-they-pleased.
A performer who pleased the audience got a longer go
than the one who didn’t. Some of the more successful
turns have been transferred to the “ vaudeville ” pro-
gramme at Rickards’, but the possibility of a school of
Australian variety artists being developed from the old
Anglicised-Yankee notion of a first part is now a shattered
dream.
“ Tyred ”: Re the Lotto Cycling Track, somebody
writes to tell you that the Lotto Act doesn’t impress
bike riders, because “ the alleged 60deg. angle at which
the four experts scour round their circular track is not a
great deal steeper than some of the sharp pinches on
several Australian cycle paths.” The Lotto track, as a
matter of fact, is considerably steeper than the sharpest
pinch of any Australian cycle path, and, whereas the
racing path goes about five laps to the mile, the basket
track goes 140 laps to the mile. When the showmen rush
around in opposite directions they are doing something
which their critics have never attempted, and, although
these same critics could doubtless all do it if they wanted
to, the public will continue to regard the Lotto perform-
ance as an exciting novelty. Granting that it looks more
dangerous than it is, one would like these out-of-the-
basket critics to state exactly how dangerous it isn’t.
“ A. 8.” writes: Henry Bracy, who stage-directs for
“ La Gioconda ” at Sydney Majesty’s, is no astronomer.
The performance of the “dance of the Hours” by 16
damsels instead of 24 may be excused on the plea of
expediency, but not so an episode in the second act.
The Soprano and the Tenor make a clandestine appoint-
ment for the evening, said appointment being thought-
fully kept by the whole chorus as well as by most of the
other principals. There is a fine setting for this scene,
its beauty being heightened by a brilliant full moon, a few
degrees above the horizon, shining on the (canvas) water.
A little later, and still during the night, that full round
moon majestically sets. Now the full moon never sets
till about 6 a.m.
Robert Henry Nicholls, known as Robert Henry,
theatrical manager, having purged himself of his con-
tempt of Court over “ What Happened to Jones,” has
been released from Brisbane gaol.
The Pro-Boer has a lot to answer for. Wilson Barrett’s
“Sign of the Cross” was recently very ill-received at
Salzburg, in Austria, and the daily papers put this down
also to the pro-Boer feeling which prevails in Germany.
The Bulletin's raucous old friend, Antoinette Sterling,
is brought back to memory by the reappearance of the
Permans in Melbourne. One of the trio (brother Willie)
married Isabelle Webster, a sweet young local soprano
who supported the appalling Antoinette through her
Australian concert tour, and carried off the vocal honours
of the show wherever it went. Miss Webster scored suc-
cesses at the halls and at concerts in England. Some
returning Australians who heard her sing aboard the boat
lately say that her voice is as good as ever it was.
“ S.S.” : Re my enthusiasm over Josephine Gassman’s
velvety brown cuticle, if the proprietress of the “picks”
is not a “ roon ” of some kind, “ quad. ” or “ 0ct0.,” she
is a marvellous imitation. Saw her in Melb. Royal circle
the other night, and she was brown as a berry. It is a
surprising thing if a white American woman condescends
to masquerade as quadroon, octoroon, or any fraction of
a colored sister, but there is certainly a difference of
opinion abroad as to Josephine’s natural hue. Will some-
body appoint me a Royal Commission to take evidence ?
Is Josephine a white woman who occasionally tints her-
self brown, or a brown woman who occasionally tints her-
self white?
Adelaide’s sudden attack of circuses (Fitzgerald’s under
canvas and Wirth’s on the stage at the Royal)has departed,
and left the small city unamused, except for the Tivoli,
where Manager Marcus, back from America, produces a
strong show, with the Craggs as its leading feature.
Madame Melba says she is hopeful of finding a few good
tenors during her visit to Australia. And now how will
Australia’s million tenors lay for the diva 1
“ Melb.” : The Salvation Army is undisguisedly going
in for the show business. Concerts are given round the
suburbs by the Garrison Students, a troup of 20, men
and women, attired in imitation-surplices of yellow and
blue. At the festivities this Christmas in the Melbourne
Barracks, a captain came on in John Coleman's scarecrow
costume, and sang a comic song. Two other officers gave
an outrageously funny duet, dressed up as swagmen on
the wallaby. Also the really superior string-band of the
Salvation Army headquarters played a selection from
Gounod’s “ Faust,” including the Kermesse dance.
It is not generally suspected that several members of
Williamson’s Italian Opera Co. are quite incapable of
singing music at sight, and others can’t read music
at all. Their parts have to be drummed into
them by conductor Hazon, and in some cases they
are not apt pupils. These singers who memorise by ear,
so to speak, seem to pride themselves on their want of
musical education. They may, perhaps, be able to teach
themselves a part by fingering out the notes on a piano,
but have never dreamed of acquiring an artist’s pro-
ficiency in sight-reading. Armes Beaumont, the almost
blind man, can pick up a bit of unfamiliar music, pass it
in review before his remaining eye, and then sing it from
note memory. A veteran like Larizza gives one the
impression that he reads music quickly. But the average
Italian vocalist needs a lot of coaching, and a lot of assist-
ance from the prompter who holds a book under the
shelter of that unsightly canoe arrangement.
Judging from the awful popularity of Josephine
Gassman’s piccaninnies, the “White Australia” Bill needs
a clause prohibiting the introduction of Kanaka babies
by showmen for future stage purposes. The training of
colored “picks” is getting to be quite an important
industry in America. White folk encourage it by accept-
ing the precocious impishness of the “ pick ” as’a matter
for surprise rather than as natural evidence of lower
animalism in the black races. Women who start by
enthusing over the antics of an infant nigger are not
unlikely to discover a charm in its big brother’s monkey
tricks.
“ J.A.C.” : Mdlle. Dolores (Trebelli), being asked by a
Sydney interviewer what was the proper diet for a singer,
very sensibly said that it would be absurd to lay down any
general rule. Her own practice was to take an
egg beaten up in a little whisky. As a matter of fact,
there is no special diet or special drink for a singer.
The celebrated Malibran pinned her faith to “ portaire,”
which she sipped at every “ wait,” and the spectacle of
such an ethereal being standing in the wings with a
pewter-pot at her rosy mouth was decidedly bizarre.
Mirate, the tenor, for whom Verdi wrote the part of the
Duke in “ Rigoletto,” used to devour macaroni between
the acts. Baucardd, the original Manrico in “Trova-
tore,” drank wine, without stint, at every opportunity
during the performance. French singers are great
believers in a special preparation of tar-water. A robust
actor of great proportions, once well-known in Australia,
who lifted up a very fine voice occasionally, used to take
a whole bottle of whisky and a raw onion. Sometimes he
took two bottles of whisky, but he never exceeded the
one onion.
Some talk of Bill Martin, with a couple of assistants,
reproducing the Lotto cycling act at Fitzgerald Bros.’
Circus. If the Fitzs provide a duplicate of the Lotto
track, William the Plugger reckons he can show them
how to ride at an angle of 60deg.


The Trials of the Sea
Poor Food and Bad Water.
DEBILITATE THE STRONGEST SYSTEM.
The Case of
Mr. H. ASWEGE.
(by a local reporter.)
It is no use blinking the fact that after a
lengthened period of privation, such as is
descriptively narrated by Mr. Harry
Aswege, of Phoebe-street, Islington, New-
castle, enough reverses had already been en-
countered, and if the final effort to dislodge
the enemy had failed, even a temporary
cessation of hostilities would not have
averted the disaster which at one time ap-
peared to be inevitable. Success having
been achieved it is permissible to hope that
caution may guide the deliberations of those
in a similar situation. When obtaining
particulars the reported asked :
“Can you fix the date on which your
enemies started operations ? ”
Mr. Aswege; “I have no difficulty in
doing that, as it was when I went on an
eighteen months’ trip on a sailing vessel
called the Monrovia in the year 1888. After
leaving Newcastle the first place to call :at
was Manila, and from thence to New York
and back to Melbourne. The only vege-
tables we' had on the voyage were a few
yams which were procured at Manila, and
when they were eaten the items which con-
sistently appeared on our bill-of-fare were
salt beef and biscuits. Living in that way
for so many months had a bad effect on my
health generally, for when I came back to
Newcastle I felt fairly broken up.”
The reporter : “ Were you suffering from
any specific complaint ? ”
Mr. Aswege: “ I was laboring under a
CO nplete derangemant of the digestive
organs, coupled with a general relaxation of
the nervous system. I looked as worn out
as though I had returned from a hard cam-
paign instead of having been on a long sea
voyage, and I felt so languid and dull that I
did not care how things went.”
The reporter : “On your return to New-
castle did you work ashore ? ”
Mr. Aswege; “ Yes. I contracted to
build a small steamer in Tigh’s Creek, but I
often had to knock off work through feeling
too drowsy and miserable to take interest in
what I was doing, and when I came home I
used to lie down on the grass suffering the
most intense pains. When my wife saw me
in such great agony she thought that some
serious internal complaint had got hold of
me, and so there had, but it was indigestion
nevertheless.”
The reporter : ‘ ‘ Why were you so sure
about that ? ”
Mr. Aswege : “ Because the pains were
always the direct result of eating. The food
seemed to go sour in the stomach almost as
soon as I had swallowed it. I used to swell
out with wind, besides feeling acute pains
like the cramps, and my chest felt so heavy
that it was troublesome and painful to get
my breath. There was a disposition to
throw the food up again, but I was even
deprived of the ease which I might have
gained it I had been able to vomit, and there
was no help for me but to endure the torture
and suffer from a wretched sense of bilious-
ness. I was depressed and tormented by
the most horrible headaches which could
possibly fall to man, and I must not omit to
mention that I had acute pains between the
shoulders. As a matter of fact, the shoulder
pains were so severe that I used to think it
was rheumatism which affected those parts,
as the bending of my back or the movement
of my arms almost crippled me. I was in a
dreadful state when I come to consider
everything, being hardly able to get about
for the aches in my loins, and with palpita-
tion of the heart coming on if I exerted my-
self in the least. Whole nights sometimes
passed without sleep coming to my relief,
and every morning my mouth was infused
with a bitter taste, which was made all the
more disagreeable by a dirty coating over
my tongue. If I dozed off for an hour I
started suddenly from some hideous dream,
and my nervousness was demonstrated by
the excessive shaking of my nerves at the
intrusion of any noise, which also excited
and irritated me. I had lost a good deal of
weight and was altogether too weak to do a
day’s work, being nothing but a mere
wreck of my former self.”
The reporter : “ What efforts did you
make to regain your health ? ”
Mr. Aswege : 11 1 employed every means
I could think of—first, medicines from a
doctor, which I discontinued because they
did not benefit me ; then I tried many kinds
of patent medicines, which were equally
useless. These reverses worried me, be-
cause I thought myself incurable, and only
for Clements Tonic I am sure I would never
have been the healthy man I am to-day.”
The reporter : “ How did you come to
take Clements Tonic ? ”
Mr. Aswege : “ Through my wife reading
about it, and advising me to take some. I
said, ‘ I am sick of medicines, and I am
positive it will not do me any good, but you
can get a bottle if you like. ’ A bottle of
Clements Tonic was purchased, and when I
had drank it I did not know whether to get
any more or not; but on calm reflection I
thought that as nothing else could cure me
I ought to give Clements Tonic a fair chance;
so I got another couple of bottles. It was
then that an improvement began, and to my
intense delight I felt that relief was being
afforded. It was at a very serious crisis
that Clements Tonic was purchased, and if I
had been again disappointed I do not know
what would have happened, but Clements
Tonic gave me a good appetite, and In ought
me restful sleep at nights. The natural
relish and desire I had for food was some-
thing extraordinary, but not more so than
my ability to digest it, which was directly
due to the influence of Clements Tonic,
which also gave power and steadiness to my
shaking nerves, and infused strength to the
whole of my system. A rich store of vitality
was thrown into my blood by Clements
Tonic, and all the flagging members of my
body lost their aches. The unbearable pains
in my shoulders, chest, and stomach were
cast out by Clements Tonic as time went on,
and I took that medicine regularly till my
complaint was thoroughly cured. Costive-
ness had been a great trouble of mine, and
was probably accountable for the blinding
headaches and giddiness from which I had
suffered, but with the continued use ol
Clements Tonic these ailments were all
remedied, and my internal organs were as
healthy and clean as they could possibly
be.”
The reporter : “Do you ever take a sea
voyage now ? ”
Mr. Aswege : “ Never. lam foreman for
the Newcastle branch for the construction
of roads and bridges, and have settled down
now; but I shall always remember that
Clements Tonic cured me when I was in
very bad straits, and you are welcome to
publish this interview in any way you
desire.”

Melbourne Southern Cross on one of
Britain’s latest annexations, now being exploited
by the firm of Cohen and Bull—largely Cohen :
The old gold-workings of Rhodesia are one of the most
interesting features of the country, and, therefore, Mr
Frank Johnson, in his paper read before the Colonial
Institute, had much to say about them. Mr. Johnson
lately examined 21 well-known properties, embracing 1000
claims, on which work had been done by the ancients.
Presuming that the lode extracted by the Himyarites at
least equalled in width and value the results obtained by
modern owners immediately below the old workings, he
iQ«‘loo that * nO ,J ess than 155 > 575 tons of ore, worth
130,8320 z. of gold, were removed from these 1000 claims
southern Rhodesia there were at present, he said,
114,000 registered claims. Assuming that the value of
,■ 114 > 000 ?laims was proportional to that of the
1000 claims examined, they had the astonishing result
tnat during the Himyaritic and Phoenician occupation of
the country, which lasted about 600 years, and ended
about 3000 years ago, 15,599,0000 z. of gold, worth at pre-
sent values more than £62,000,000, were removed from
Southern Rhodesia by these early Uitlanders. Mr. John-
son entered into further interesting investigations, sup-
ported by Biblical testimony, of the quantity of gold
which passed through the great mart of Ophir and by way
of Tarshish—-Sofala of to-day—to the treasuries of Ring
liavid and King Solomon ; but an even more interesting
inference was that the ancient miners have by no means
exhausted, perhaps hardly more than scratched, the
Rhodesian gold deposits.
Twenty-nine centuries ago the pick dropped from
the nerveless hand of Cohen, the gold-seeker—or
rather his deputy ; his kingdom went to pieces, he
lost his Red Sea ports, aud his brief dignity as
the owner of one of the world’s great empires" left
him. And now the immortal Cohen is back
among his old mines, looking for the reef he aban-
doned when he hurried home to fight Jeroboam,
the son of Nebat, away back on the far-out rim
of history. Truly things do come to the man who
can wait, and in the matter of waiting Cohen
beats the world. Th© setting sun of four great
empires has been reflected on his bell-topper since
the riches of these old Rhodesian mines went to
enrich Solomon—and now Solomon has comeback
for more. The awful permanency of Solomon and
Cohen is one of the world’s mysteries.

The Eternal Feminine.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
I.
On Ben Bolter—" Big Ben,” as he was more
familiarly called —liquor wrought a complete
metamorphosis. In his right mind, he was
kindly, gentle, and genial. He was a carrier,
living in Western Queensland, and it went to his
heart to lay a whip on one of his horses. But
with one nip, Ben awoke a craving, gnawing
devil, who instantly demanded more, and more,
and more. Then the devil demanded blood.
There was one whom this devil feared—Mrs.
Bolter. She was neither young nor handsome,
but faded and washed-out, like most of the women
whose unfortunate lines have fallen in the sunny
West. Yet, the devil was so frightened of her
that he never craved for a drink when at home in
the township where Ben resided. Mrs. Bolter
alone had never seen Ben drunk.
Many wondered at this strange influence and
could not account for it. In fact, it was an intense
affection overmastering a man of good heart, weak
head, and strong passions. He had married his
wife when she was a bonnie young girl, had
brought her out west and seen her grow sallow
and old before her time in the sun-cursed, dusty
summers. Ever haunting Ben’s memory was the
picture of the peach-cheeked, bright-eyed girl she
had been, and the dull-eyed, parchment-faced
woman she now was. This thought controlled his
mind even during the maddest drunk, until it
wrought him up to a maudlin fit of remorse, and
when Ben was seen sitting at the tail of his wag-
gon crying bitterly, every one knew that the spree
was over for the time. No matter how the devil
suffered during the process of recovery, Ben would
not gratify him with a single “pick-me-up.”
At a small public-house on the roadside some
distance from his home the devil had demanded
drink, and had got it. Two other teamsters were
camped there, and the loafers about the place
remarked confidently that Big Ben and Black
Harry would have a fight before night. They had
fought many times before, and were the greatest
possible friends. Just then they were lovingly
drinking together. But soon a question arose as
to whose turn it was to shout, and they agreed to
settle the question by single combat. Ben sug-
gested that they should first clear all the loafers
out of the bar, which they did, so suddenly and
unexpectedly that much personal damage was
done.
BenS mate and Harry’s mate, who, on a strictly
honest principle, always kept, sober, and looked
after the horses when their principals got drunk,
came up to be seconds, and some of the loafers
who had escaped unhurt crept up to see. When
they had slogged themselves to a standstill they
were hauled off to their waggons by their mates,
and lay in a drunken stupor all night.
In the morning, when Ben awoke, he could tell
at the first move that he had had a tough fight
with some one, and probably Black Harry. He
pulled himself together, and was about to go oyer
to see how Harry was getting on, when he spied
the mailman, with a letter in his hand.
Ben took the letter, and sat down on an inverted
bucket to read it. It was from his wife, and he
felt sure “something was wrong ” for her to write
to him so soon. Nothing was wrong, but the
letter contained something that sent Ben into a
fit of pleasant musing. She had not told him be-
fore he left —she wrote—she thought that she
would sooner write it to him, but—in fact, there
was a prospect of his at last becoming a.father..
Poor Ben, it had been one of the hidden dis-
appointments of his life, and now it was to be
banished, and instead of a lonely old age he pic-
tured a sturdy boy, whom he would make the best
horse-driver in all Queensland. He felt he must
tell somebody, so he got up and went over to
Black Harry’s waggon. That worthy was en-
gaged in repairing damages, and. hailed his late
antagonist with effusion. Ben, in return, con-
fided to him the news that he had just received.
Harry’s congratulations were unbounded, and he
insisted that they must go up and have a royal
drink together. But Ben’s devil was cowed by
the news in the letter, and though he accom-
panied his friend he only treated the devil to
sarsaparilla, which that spirit did not like. The
men parted after arranging that Harry was to be
godfather when the time arrived ; and Ben went
off to make a late start.
Now, the whole was a pious fraud on the part
of Mrs. Bolter. She had longed for a child with
the morbid longing of the childless woman. As
Ben had concealed his disappointment from her
so she had concealed her more acute feeling from
him.
On the day after Ben left she received a letter
from her sister, who lived on the Monaro Plains,
where both of them were born. Her husband had
met with a sudden death through an accident; she
expected a ohild in a few months, and she was deli-
cate and troubled with evil anticipations; could her
sister come to her ? It was not until she sat down
to answer the letter that the idea of deceit came
into Mrs. Bolter’s mind. If her sister died, why
should she not bring the baby back as her own ?
Ben was going a long trip, and it would be six
months, or more, before he would be back. It
would render Ben steady and happy, and take
from her the reproach of the childless woman.
So she wrote Ben the letter, telling him that she
would go on a visit to her own people , while he
was away. She always managed their money
affairs, so she drew sufficient for her journey, told
some of her women friends, as a secret, that she
“expected something,” and left by the coach.
It was over six months before Ben got back,
although he had made as much haste as possible,
but floods study no man’s affairs, and the country
had been under water. He had a letter from his
wife just before he reached home containing the
news of her safe arrival home and of the baby’s.
Of her sister’s death she said nothing.
Ben had a joyous home-coming. Ever since
the fight with Black Harry he had kept the devil
chained up, and he was in the best of health and
spirits. As for the baby, he was the most
astonishing boy for his age that ever was (con-
sidering that his birth had been post-dated two
months, that was not wonderful). Ben’s trips,
after the new arrival, were made as short as
possible. The promise with Black Harry was
duly kept, and he vowed on the baby’s behalf
with the greatest enjoyment, and henceforth
seemed to consider that he had a joint share in
the infant. As for Mrs. Bolter, she seemed to
grow young again under the new home influence.
She grew stout and happy-looking, and lost the
strained, wearied look that had come to her in her
lonely days. Time went on, and baby Ben had
attained the middle of his third year, and his
great delight was to be always with Big Ben
when he was at home.
One evening Ben was smoking at the door of
his house, on the dusty main road of the town-
ship, when he heard the voices of two men spik-
ing, on the adjoining plot of vacant ground, lhe
child was playing out there, and they had stopped
to notice him. „ _ ,
“Is that Ben’s kid?” said one. Isnt much
like him, is he ? ” , ,
11 No,” returned the other. “If I did not know
better, I’d say he was Black Harry’s.”
“ He’s awfully like Harry, isn’t he? said the
other voice, and then the conversation died away
in the distance. ..
Ben remained still with a queer sort of feeling
in his brain, as if somebody had struck him. 1 hen
he went out, picked up the child, and carried him
into the house. His wife was out, and with the
child in his arms he went into the bedroom and
looked in the looking-glass.
No, there was no resemblance between them
whatever. Strange that he had never noticed it
until the chance words had attracted his attention.
The child was dark—eyes, eyebrows, and hair,
even his skin had a slightly swarthy hue. Ben
was fair and blue-eyed, so was his wife.
He set the boy gently down and tried to think,
but only one idea found room in his troubled
brain, that he was not little Ben’s father. A fixed
idea had got hold of him which could not be
shaken off. But as yet no feeling against his wife
had arisen. Somehow, although satisfied that the
boy was not his, he did not connect her with any
sense of injury. He had loved her too truly and
too long, was too convinced of her fidelity and
truth, to admit any doubt of her as yet.
Then the devil who had been chained uj> so
long whispered to him to go out and get a drink.
But he spoke in vain. Ben had a dim idea of what
he might do if he drank, and the devil remained
chained.
His wife came home and soon noticed his listless
and absent manner, but he would give her no ex-
planation of it; and she could only follow him
about with wistful eyes, wondering what serpent
had entered their Eden. Evening drew on, and
with a rattle and a rush the coach came in, puttmg
on, as usual, the spurt the driver had saved up for
the finish.
A shriek from his wife, “Ben! the boy!
Right in the road stood little Ben. Ben s feet
seemed clogged; through his tormented brain
surged the words, “I’d say he was Black Harry s
child.” An instant vigorous dash would have saved
the child. A dash was made, but that one
moment of hesitation on Ben’s part before his
right self reasserted itself, was the moment of
doom. The driver made a manful struggle to pull
his horses off, at the risk of overturning the coach,
but an iron-shod hoof struck the laughing child on
the head, and the bright little life flickered out on
the spot. It was a dead form that Ben carried m
and laid on the bed.
Night had long fallen. Sympathising friends
had come and gone, and Ben still sat, his head in
his hands, as he had sat since he had carried the
little corpse in. His or not, he had loved the
child, and he knew, though no one else did, that
if he had not lost that one precious moment, he
would have saved him. .
Some one came to him in the semi-darkness
someone who dropped upon her knees, sobbing
bitterly. “ Ben ! Ben ! I must tell you the truth
now. It was not your child that you sorrow for.”
Even this did not rouse him. “ I know it,” he
answered, wearily.
“Know what?”
“ That I was not little Ben’s father.
The woman started to her feet. “My God,
Ben, you surely do not mean that, or think I mean
it. Listen, listen,” and, in her hysterical eager-
ness, she shook him with trembling hands, “Ben,
he was not our child ! Our child, do you under-
stand ? ”
“ Not our child ! ”
“No, he was my sister’s, who died. I never had
a child nor hope of one. I lied to you when I
wrote to you that I had. But oh ! I did long for
one so. When my sister died I brought the child
back with me as mine, my own. I never told you,
for you were so happy, and I grew almost to think
the child was really mine.” .
In Ben’s brain the tune had changed ; it now
ran that if he had been told the truth, and the
child been adopted straightforwardly, the still
form on the bed would be alive now. It was all a
tangle that he could not put into shape.
He would have welcomed the child. gladly as
his orphan nephew. Why, then, was it imposed
upon him as his own ? Poor Ben ! —the woman
and mother were a sealed book to his understand-
ing.
Suddenly he saw light. The weeping woman at
his knees must never know of the words he had
heard, of the thoughts suggested by them, of the
moment’s cruel hesitation they had caused. She
must never know that her deceit had indirectly
brought about little Ben’s death. That was clear,
amid all the web of untruth. It was something to
do, and it gave him strength. He lifted his wife
up, and let her cry her heart out in his embrace.
Long afterwards, when Ben had buried his
wife, and, a lone and wearied man, had turned
his face to the West, lying on the ground,
dying, with a flint-headed spear through his lungs,
he thought with satisfaction that she had never
known.
ERNEST FAVENC.
The Prahran branch of the A.N.A.
(which professes to be Australian and non-
political) has cancelled Jack Murray’s engagement
to lecture to it on the Temperance question, on
the ground that the plucky Warrnamboolian
doesn’t approve of Britain’s Boer annexation
policy in South Africa. As Murray has been of
the same opinion all along, it is difficult to see
why the lecture engagement was made, unless the
A.N.A. didn’t know its own mind at the time, or
unless it was done in order that the arrangement
might be cancelled later on with a great blowing-
off of loyal steam and advertisement. . Possibly,
after this, the Rev. lan Maclaren, who is expected
to shortly visit Melbourne, will not be allowed to
preach because he termed the miserable South
African brigandage a stock-jobbing war.
“Safety-valve”: Re “casual” and all-
round “temporary” nature of working the Vic.
railways. Even the permanent hands have duties
allotted to them out of all proportion to their ex-
perience. Writer knows of an instance. At a
country station one of the recently-appointed
engine-cleaners was left solely in charge of the
washing out, lighting up, etc., of two or three
locomotives after three days’ experience in thp ser-
vice. His previous knowledge of locomotives was
nil. And this is not an isolated case. These men,
in small country sheds, are mostly employed at
night—when no other men are about. Some night
one of them will light up an engine with insuf-
ficient water in the boiler, or, after washing out
the engine, he will leave a “ mud-plug cross-
threaded, and it will blow out when the steam
pressure rises behind it. House rent near loco-
motive engine - sheds will come down rapidly
afterwards!

A Marching Song.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
There’s a mighty army marching to the music of
a band,
And the front rank steps out gaily, bearing nothing
in its hand;
There is laughter there and gladness, for the
breezes kiss and sing,
And the quickstep rolls and ripples, and the
soldiers proudly swing;
But, alas! the rear-guard follows in a loose and
weary mob—
For the people’s backs are burdened, and you’ll
sometimes hear them sob :
As they march, march, march —
Oh ! the hand's so far in front
That they scarcely ever hear it,
Though the trumpets hr ay and grunt.
Yet they march, march, march,
In a dogged, hopeless way,
Oh l I hope they'll swing together
To a marching-tune some day.
They are lingering in the vanguard. Can’t you
raise a song behind ?
For ’t would clear the leaden dullness that is
clogging heart and mind.
But I only hear the sobbing of a woman close at
hand,
Where another fighter’s perished where he cannot
hear the band.
And an old man toils before me—mumbling lips
and silvery hair;
And a ringless mother staggers far too tired to feel
or care.
Oh, you dreamers in the lead there! and you
buglers ! swell your strain
That the burdened ones may hear you and be
lightened of their pain ;
As they tramp, tramp, tramp —
If the hand would only play
It would give them heart to labour
In the hot and tiring day.
Oh l they tramp, tramp, tramp,
And they don't keep step at aU,
And there are so few to notice
And to help them if they fall.
To the thunder of its marching there’s a mighty
army goes,
With the big drum fairly jumping to the laughing
drummer’s blows —
And, long dusty leagues behind him, in a silence
grim and grand.
Comes the noble, toiling rear-guard, but—they
never hear the band.
If the power were mine I’d help them (if a song
would only do) —
Oh ! my outcast, toiling comrades, here’s my
hand in love to you !
As you march, march, march
In the dull and dusty rear,
Where your aimless, weary stumbling
Is the only sound you hear.
Comrades, march! march ! march l
For you'll hear the music soon
And you'll step and swing together
In a rocking, rolling tune.
M.L.
QUILP N.
Two Wooden Legs.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“THIS was how it was,” said old Paddocks,
slowly rubbing a handful of black twist between
his palms. “ Our Mollie had contracted to marry
Jim Brady, him as went to the war. When
they called fer troops, Jim, like a lot more young
fools, thought as how it was jest a picnic party to
go out and cut them Boers’ throats. So he ups
and jines the contingent. But, afore it starts, he
wanted to take Mollie to the church. Then I
puts my fut down. ‘No,’ says I; ‘ I’m not a-
goin’ to have my gell a-snivellin’ and a-slobberin’
about the house,’ says I, ‘ awaitin’ fer Jim ; and
expectin’ this and fearin’ that, and redoocin’ her-
self to a skellington,’ says I. ‘ Let her run free,’
says I, ‘ and if she’s of the same mind when Jim
gets home, all right.’ Well, they was forced to
come to my terms, and off Jim goes to the war.
“Now you must understan’ there was an ole
cove wi’ a wooden leg, named Burr, livin’ just
across the crik from us. He didden used to do a
hand’s turn but ’tend to his garding. And he
had no need to, cos he was wuth ’bout twenty
thousand, true as yer bom. He wasn’t a bad ole
cove, and used to stump across that crik and
smoke a pipe in our verandy. He thought a
ter’ble lot of Mollie, and used to bring her bushels
of fruit and flowers and sich. After Jim left he
came oftener; and the flip-flop, flip-flop of his
timber leg got as familiar as the rooster’s crow of
a mornin’.
“Between you and me, I b’lieve my ole woman
used ter encourage him. Well, months and months
went by and we never heered from Jim. Once we
saw a line in the paper that a James Brady was
mortally wounded and in the horspital; but he
never wrote, which he should have done. We
heered arterwards that his letters missed fire
somewheres.
“ Howsomever, ole Burr kep’ on stumpin’ across
that crik, and the flip-flop of his timber leg was as
reg’lar as the kitching clock. And gradually
Mollie got to listen for it. And her mother kep
a-eggin’ her on and a-eggin’ her on, and tellin’ as
how pore Jim ud never, never come back; and
how it was no jise a waitin’, till Mollie didden
know what she wanted.
“At last, things got to that pitch that Mollie
used ter sprig herself out every blessed evenin’,
with a bunch of old Burr’s flowers in her breast;
and d’reckly she heered the flip-flop cornin’ along
the verandy, then she’d up and run to the dure to
meet him. Well, yer may guess things were
gettin’ serious when he called her ‘ my own
Mollie,’ and she called him ‘ dear Samuel! ’ I
didden hear ’em, but my ole woman did. She
could hear a mouse squeak t’other side of the
paddick. 9
At last one day the ole woman ups and tells
Mollie she must clue matters up and choose be-
tween a man wi’ a property and a big bank-
account and a man who might be a dead-’un and
who had nothing, even if he was alive. ‘ He’s
got a wooden leg,’says Mollie. ‘What of that,’
says the ole woman. ‘ Why, I’d marry him if he
had two wooden legs and I had to wheel him ’bout
in a pram.’ Yer can’t beat my old woman by
much. Well, she got Mollie worked up to proper
order, till she said as how she’d settle things that
night. So she togs out extra fine in a white
musling with little blue things all over it and old
Burr’s last booky in her front, till she looked as
fresh as new paint and as blooming as a rose.
“Ole Burr gen’ally sounded on the path jest
afore sundown, but this night it was gettin’ dusk
afore the ole woman heered the flip-flop, flip-flop
of the ole reliable wooden leg a-comin’ along
the verandy. She gives Mollie the orfis, and
the gell runs past me to the dure. I heard
the ole flip-flop stop all on a suddin’; then
Mollie gives a hawful scream and drops on
the dure-mat as flat as a tin plate. And
there was Jim Brady standin’ like a fool, wi’ a
wooden leg, same as ole Burr’s. True as yer
born. Well, ’ere was ago! We got Mollie to
bed and locked Jim up in the wood-house. Then
the ole woman and I waited fer timber-toe number
two. But he didden come. Jim got rusty and
played up bobbery dick, till we let him see Mollie.
He swore he’d go and shoot ole Burr fust thing
after breakfast if Mollie and he weren’t married
next day. I think he’d have done it, tew. Parson
hitched ’em up, and we all thought it curious that
ole Burr hadn’t turned up till we heered he’d been
found a’most dead on the crik bridge. Yer see, his
oleleghad gone clean through the floorin’, and there
it stuck, hard and fast, all that arternoon and all
night, and somehow he warn’t able to unhitch
it. _ The ole cove cudden speak above a
whisper when they sawed the leg off him, being
as how he had yelled like forty cows fer twelve
solid hours; and they had to pour two gallons of
ile down his throat afore he could swaller his food.
I felt sorry fer the ole chap; so did Mollie. I
dunno about Jim. The ole woman said to me as
how she wished another wooden leg had stuck in
a bridge over in S’Africa somewhere. Yer can’t
beat my ole woman by much.
O. H. READ.
O’Flaherty's Troubles.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
O’Feaherty was a toilsome, inoffensive scion of
nobility, working out his perdition as a “ sthrug-
gler,” or 80-acre selector, in a district too high
above sea-level for any blessing to rest upon it.
The whole area of his estate was originally colon-
naded, at the rate of 50 trees to the acre, with a
variety of messmate which simply would not
bum. The land, when cleared, might have been
adapted to the growth of unsaleable root-crops,
only for its being hopelessly addicted to sorrel,
cockspur, and similar pests.
But O’Flaherty’s eye of faith perceived a time
when that allotment would represent a com-
petency —and his forecast has long since been ful-
filled.
My friend was a widower, with one daughter,
named Bridget, and therefore in service. As with
many men of greater celebrity, his ambition was
to found a family; and to this end he battled
hard for a freehold home, where Bridget and the
present narrator might enjoy an easier and more
dignified life than had fallen to his own lot.
But, a couple of years after taking up the
selection, he had been assisted over some difficulty
by a genial and business-like person, named Moss,
who obligingly took charge of his Land Office
documents for safety, and from time to time got
him to sign various long blue papers as a mere
matter of form.
The original advance was £75 nominal —equal
to £5O nett; and, for four years, all O’Flaherty’s
revenue, beyond bare subsistence on the Conne-
mara scale, went toward liquidation of the loan.
He kept no books except “Moore’s Melodies”
and “The Lives of the Saints,” and knew no rule
of arithmetic except the rule of thumb; but in
process of time his mental memoranda of repay-
ments seemed so like a fair thing between man
and man that little could remain beyond the
moral obligation. You will observe that he was
“more Popery than fiscal” —to quote the im-
mortal words of a one-time politician of New
South Wales.
But Israel, of the Lord beloved (a partiality, by
the way, open to reverent criticism), was a past-
master in finance as well as a friend in need.
Anyway, lulled by the fanning of the vampire’s
wings, O’Flaherty took no thought of his own big
toe, till he woke with a start, to find Moses avail-
ing himself of his right of foreclosure.
Macaulay quotes Pitt to the somewhat ungram-
matical effect that, “though the cottage of the
poorest British subject might be frail, the roof might
shake, the wind might blow through it, the storm
might enter, the rain might enter, but the King of
England could not enter it! In all his forces, he
dared not cross the threshold of that ruined
tenement! ” This is intended to convey, and does
convey, to the mind’s eye a picture of the poorest
British subject (which is saying a lot) standing
inside his “tenement,” with Magna Charta at
full cock in one hand, and the British Constitution
glittering unscabbarded in the other ; tranquil,
defiant, almost contemptuous, whilst Dei Gratia,
arrayed in the regimentals of his rank, and
supported by the various branches of the Service,
desperately essays that inviolable threshold, and
recoils baffled, like an ant from a line of chalk.
But in the ever-sacred name of common horse-
sense, why should the potentate in question want
to cross that threshold ? Of what use would the
immunity be—even if it did exist, which it never
did, and never could —to the unfortunate wretch
whose threshold can be crossed with such facility
by a royal officer, vulgarly termed the bailiff, and
still more vulgarly, the bum ? Pitt had an object
in his insidious bombast; but there is no excuse
for people who, at the present day, repeat it and
print it, otherwise than as a monumental example
of balderdash.
However, I dropped in to collogue with
O’Flaherty, when I heard of his calamity. The
representative of Royalty lay in the spare bunk,
smoking. The garrison of the impregnable for-
tress sat on a block by the embers of the last fire
he could legally kindle on his capacious hearth.
The Advocate —the Victorian organ of the wan
thrue Church —was in his hand. He had been
reading the paper till the firelight failed, and
now he was dispirited, despondent. He explained
to me that the Italian Government was making
an attempt to drain and plant the marshes of the
Campagna ; and he was sure, from the tone of the
Advocate article which noticed the enterprise,
that the attempt was foredoomed to failure.
Hence his dejection. O’Flaherty was a philoso-
pher. He had attained the Everlasting No.
JOSEPH FORPHY.

are many forms of it, any of them bad enough to
tax your patience. Hemorrhoid, a plague of the
night; no rest for the sufferer from that com-
plaint. Eczema, too. Hives don’t sound danger-
ous, but they cause much misery to those
unfortunate enough to be troubled with them.

trouble. Any troublesome irritation of the skin

IN addition to the inconvenience inherent in the infirm-
ity itself, Deafness has a marked influence on the
Mental Development of the individual, when the latter
has been attacked by it in youth.
For a long time Deafness and Chronic Affections of the
Ear have been considered almost incurable, but recently
a very simple means has been found in the jElectricon to
successfully apply Metallic Galvanic Electricity direct W
the ears, permanently curing l Deafness, and completely
banishing all distressing head noises.
Those who are interested in the subject would do well
to read the complete description of this method of treat-
ment in the Journal for the Deaf, published by the
l. R. VERNON COY., 60=64 Hunter St., Sydney,
and sent FREE to anyone asking for it.
